The Red Page
Fiction.
In What Will People Say? (Harper, per
Dymock’s Library), a novel that rather
overpoweringly exudes the aroma of sex,
Robert Bridges opens up an interesting
question or two. His hero, returning from
the Philippines, is struck by the flaunting
challenge of New York’s society women.
“All these women are paid fot by men. For
everything that every one of these women
wears some man has paid.... The men who
pay for these things are not here: they are
m their offices or shops or at their tasks
somewhere, building, producing; or in their
graves resting from their labors, while th
spendthrift sex gads abroad squandering anc
flaunting what it has wheedled; What do
these women give in return? They musi
pay something. What do they pay?”
Forbes is a poor lieutenant in the toy
army of the U.S.A. He falls in love witi
Perns, and Persis belongs to the Smart Set
”he gallops with the rich runaways. Their
life is one long stampede.” And Persis is
always thinking of what other people woulc
think. Hughes knows his idle N’York rich
ms society” pictures are painted with
brutal realism. Persis, who loves Forbes, is
too clever to marry a poor man. She sells
herself into slavery, as a Mrs. Millionaire
with a cynical cheerfulness. Forbes gets
the answer to his question. “These women
.... paid with their bodies, their kisses, the
encircling of arms, the cooing of tender
words. In return for so much money they
granted permission to spend yet more.” And
.L ersis has an ingenious answer to Forbes’s
indignation. Men will sacrifice everything
for their career. Well, why shouldn’t women
and a woman’s career is marriage?
But Persis, having made her bargain, will
not pay. And the end is a sordid tragedy
in which Persis is murdered by her feeble
little, enormously rich husband, a weak
character capably and even sympathetically
drawn. Another exposure, if exposure is
needed, of the selfish, cold-blooded heroine
of modern U.S. fiction—and probably of
fife. What Will People Say? just misses
being a great novel: it is too seductively
luscious.
Algernon Blackwood grows a trifle tedious.
His latest collection of stories of. the spook-
world, Incredible Adventures (Macmillan)
is incredible enough, but his art is occasion-
ally ineffective. He fails quite to create his
magic: one has. an uneasy suspicion that one
is reading fiction. His ghosts are merely
spooks. Not that he is gauche enough to
deal in ordinary spooltery: his ghosts are
forces, like fire and mystical
Iheys and dead Egypt and the reincarna-
taon of forgotten ages. And when he uses
the haunted house motif he is not content
0 .... ave haunted by one ghost, but by
millions. 8ut....0ne does not quite be-
lieve —as one believed in Jimbo and The
Centaur, as he made one willing to believe
m those, triumphs of the mystic. And his
method is too discursive; there are pages
of “The Regeneration of Lord Ernie” that
grow wearisome simply because of the over-
elaboration of his description.
‘‘The Damned” is a cheerful conception.
A house is haunted by Hell. The millions
of the damned are trying to get out—evi-
dently the door is somewhere in the cellar.
There is a reason for the trouble. The house
is haunted by the thought and belief that its
former inhabitants had left behind. It is
haunted by a succession of dour intolerances.
‘The site on which this modern house now
stands was Roman, before that Early
Britain, before that again, Druid. The older
building that Franklyn altered and practi-
cally pulled down was a monastery; but
before he came here the house was occu-
pied by Manetti, a violent Catholic without
tolerance or vision; and in the interval be-
tween these two, Julius Weinbaum had it,
Hebrew of the most rigid type imaginable.
Each in turn has left his layer of con-
centrated thinking and belief behind him;
because each believed intensely, absolutely,
beyond the least weakening of any doubt —
the kind of strong belief and thinking that
is rare anywhere to-day, the kind that wills,
impregnates objects, saturates the atmo-
sphere, haunts, in a word. And each, be-
lieving he was utterly right, damned with
equally positive conviction the rest of the
world. One and all preached that implicitly
if not explicitly. It’s the root of every creed.
Last of the bigoted, grim series came Samuel
Franklyn.”
The house was haunted by generation after
generation of wowsers. The concentrated
essence of woWserism saturated the whole
place. But, as one character sisked: “Why
does nothing ever happen? A place so
thickly haunted ought to produce a crop of
no ordinary results!”
There were so many varieties of wowsers,
layer on layer, each in turn damning all the
others, that the final effect was merely to
keep -Hell in a ferment and prevent any
variety of wowserism getting a real work-
ing majority. Hence Hell could not do much
harm. “The thought and belief of each occu-
pant m turn kept all the others under. They
gave no sign. of . life at the time. But the
results of thinking never die. They crop
out again the moment there’s an ope.ning.
And with the return of Mabel in her nega-
tive state, believing nothing positive herself,
the place for the nrst time found itself free
to reproduce its buried stores. Damnation,
hell-fire and the rest —the most permanent
and vital thought of all those creeds, since
it was applied to the majority of the world
—broke loose again, for there was no re-
straint to hold it back... .The place is satur-
ated by that appalling stream of thinking—
the terror of the damned. It concentrated
upon Mabel, whose negative attitude fur-
nished the channel of deliverance. Nothing
happened, because no one layer could ever
gain the supremacy.”
However, things nearly, very nearly, hap-
pened. Mabel and her guests heard the doors
of Hell open and shut, and they heard
another awful thing: they heard “weeping
and gnashing of teeth”! Blackwood is to
be congratulated upon having made a horror
of that gnashing. Not only did they hear
it, but gentle Mabel started gnashing her-
self. “I am not sure that it was a sound I
had ever heard before, though children, I
half-remembered, made it sometimes in blind
rages when they knew not what they did.
In a grown-up person certainly I had never
known it. I associated it with animals
rather—horribly. In the history of the
world, no doubt, it has been common enough,
alas; but, fortunately, to-day there can be
few who know it, or would recognise it even
when heard.”
Only in this story does Algernon Black-
wood get his convincing shudder; and he
finishes the story, with admirable art, with
the conventional happy ending. It is a
good, satisfying gnash.
d*
. “I knew an old man in Connaught one
time, and he was a great lad for the stories.
If that man was to break off in the middle
of a tale the neople would stand up and kill
him, they would so. He would tell you a
story about nothing at all, and you’d listen
to him with your mouth open, and you
afraid he would come to the end of it soon,
and maybe it would be nothing more than
the tale of how a white hen laid a brown
egg. He would tell you a thing you knew
all your life, and you would think it was a
new thing. There was no old age in that
man’s mind, and that’s the secret of story-
telling.”
That is one secret of story-telling: the
other is to be James Stephens. His new
novel, The Demi-Gods (Macmillan), is
merely a story, but there is in it something
more than white hens and brown eggs. But
there is no need to analyse The Demi-Gods;
for James Stephens takes so huge a delight
in the telling of the wanderings in Ireland of
Patsy and Mary Mac Cann and the “three
big buck angels” and the donkey, that he
carries the reader’s critical faculties hilari-
ously away with him. If Stephens assures
you that three big buck angels did alight in
Ireland and that they had detachable wings
and haloes, you accept his statement just as
the ass did. Delightful personages these
angels, especially the one called Art, And
the donkey is just as human as the arch-
angel—but more sensible. And the story
is as extravagant as his heroes. Yet it is
no rollicking odyssey; it is full of human
tenderness and pathos, and its glittering tex-
ture is shot through and through with wis-
dom and humor. James Stephens’ novels
are like no other novels. You may think
them silly, you may know them wise; but if
you are wise you will read them.
And into this fantastic plot stray real
people with their real tragedies. There
is Eileen Ni Cooley, “a bad woman,” a mem-
ber of a very old profession, who is yet
to be pitied—-and loved. And there is that
bad man, Brien O’Brien, who got thrown
out of. Hell, together with the Seraph, owing
to their historic dispute over a stolen three-
penny bit. James has told that tremen-
dous story before: here we meet the actual
characters in it, and learn of their aston-
ising adventures after hitting the earth,
“just outside the village of Donnybrook
where the back road runs to the hills.” And
it turns out that one of the big buck angels
is Patsy Mac Cann’s Guardian Angel—and
Patsy had been making his Guardian Angel
“drunk every second night in the little
pubs!”'. However, the Guardian Angel de-
nies this. He states that it would have
taken more than Patsy to make him drunk.
And, engrossed in these miracles, the reader
never notices that, all through, Mary had
never had speech with Art, the youngest of
the. angels. But over the fire, one night, in
their eyes love is born, sex is born, and then:
“She could not think, she could not try to
think, that was her stillness ; but she could
feel and that was her movement: she was
no longer a woman but a responsiveness.”
And there is the final scene, where Art, the
youngest of the angels, renounces his celes-
tial glories—-and, incidentally, his loose'
halo and his detachable wings—for the
beauty of Mary Mac Cann.
The Glean Heart, by A. S. M. Hutchinson
(Hodder and Stoughton, per Dymock’s
Library), is a fine successor to The Happy
Warrior. It is the genuine open-air novel, as
contrasted with the popular fakes by Jeffery
Farnol.. Philip Wriford is a successful
journalist and author, who all his young life
had overworked in the ultimately successful
effort to provide for his dependents, simply
because he had not the pluck to shake him-
self free from their tyranny. And he never
took a holiday—never had the courage to.
The inevitable consequence was a nervous
breakdown. Hutchinson paints a graphic pic-
ture of Wriford. the editor sending his weekly
to press, conscientiously satisfying himself
that every line in it was just as he wanted
it, worried almost to madness, yet too weak
to delegate an atom of his own authority to
his subordinates, somehow “pulling through,”
spent, sick, nerveless, and then “beginning to
think of going home and beginning to think
of the task of taking down his coat from
behind the door and of the task of getting
into it. He began to think of the paper that
had just gone to_ press, and*began in his mind
to go slowly through it from the first page,
enumerating the title of each article and
each picture. Somewhere after half a dozen
pages he would lose the thread and find him-
self miles away occupied with some other
incident, then he would start again “A
picture that will appeal to the heart—or
whatever it is he has got—of every sub-
editor.'
So Wriford goes mad, attempts to drown
himself, and, being unwillingly rescued, flees
raving from himself—his other self. Some-
how he finds himself out in the country, and
meets Puddlebox. Puddlebox, as grotesque a
character as his name, takes kindly to the
looney; and the two start on their adven-
tures. And they are adventures! Quite
purposeless, since the only way that Wriford
can escape his other self is to dare his spook
to follow him in the maddest escapades. It
is only by. torturing his body that Wriford
can give his brain a rest: his madness com-
pels him to-incessant physical activity. But
an end comes to this phrase, marked by the
death of the grotesque Puddlebox. At times
this gentleman seems too obviously a liter-
ary creation; but the author cunningly re-
stores his caricature to humanity by the
nobility of his death. Wriford has a long
and dreary convalescence, during which he
puzzles over; the appalling fact that he has
lost the capacity for happiness.
“In the old days he had been slave of his
brain, hounded by his brain. He had cast
that away. He had escaped from it. In the
new he had turned for joy of living to his
body, and had mastered his body and all his
fears and all his thoughts. He had lived
through two lives—life that was not his own,
but given to others; life that was all his own
and to none but himself belonged. Fruitless
both. Was there some secret of happiness
that he had missed?” Yet Puddlebox had
been happy. Had Puddlebox some quality
unknown to him? You see, Wriford had
not come to a recognition of the meaning of
the fact that Puddlebox had given his life ’to
save Wriford. It was necessary for Wriford
to learn again, and more bitterly, the mean-
ing of sacrifice. So Wriford meets Essie—
one of the most delightful feminine creations
in recent literature. Essie is real, whereas
Puddlebox never quite escaped from litera-
ture into life.
The story of the new Wriford, slowly
learning the meaning of unselfishness as a
master of a boys’ school, and as slowly learn-
ing the meaning of Essie, is the most charm-
ing portion of an otherwise too hard and too
intellectually brilliant a novel. And in the
end .it is Essie’s tremendous, but hardly
credible, sacrifice for him that restores him
to himself, cured of his ingrained egoism.
Yet here, too, Hutchinson strains the credu-
lity of his sympathetic reader. Essie he left
under sentence of death, and returned to
London, to take up his work again, without
attempting to find out whether she had lived
or died. It is true that he had made a vow
to himself: he regarded this trial as an expi-
ation, £s a contract with God. But he loved
Essie, and Essie loved him. And none of
Hutchinson’s readers could have been so cal-
lous about Essie; and Hutchinson cannot
persuade us that Wriford could have been
so inhuman, even if still mad. It looks, to
the hardened fiction-reader, as if the author
had imposed this condition on Wriford
merely to achieve his tremendous climax.
And that is a sin against literature.
There is in The Glean Heart a similar
moral to that of W. B. Maxwell’s In Cotton
Wool. Both fine novels deal with an exag-
gerated egoism, and both lead to madness.
Let Wriford speak: “All my life from the
beginning has been self. Back in the London
days when I was working day and night,
when I was longing to be free, when I
thought I was giving up my life to others,
it was all self, self that was destroying me.
It was not ceaseless work that wrought upon
my peace of mind, robbed me of my youth—
it was pitying myself, thinking of myself,
contrasting my lot with that of others. It is
not work nor trouble that kills a man, robs
him of sleep, loses him his happiness—it is
turning the stress of it inwards upon himself,
never forgetting himself when occupied with
it, always keeping himself before his eyes,
watching himself, pitying himself. .. .1
thought I was sacrificing my life—l was
grudging every moment of it given away
from my own pursuits. How could I be
sacrificing when in doing so I was unhappy?
.... To give—to give time, money, life itself,
and have compassion for oneself in giving
them, that is the very pit of self, worse than
self open and wilful.”
A fine, clean book, full of life and its
iridescent humor, yet a little overlaid by
literary artifice, a little too full of affecta-
tions of style. But, in every sense, worth
while—an achievement.

The Australasian Photo-Review
(Kodak, C. A. 4d. monthly, edited by
Walter Burke) opens the new year with an
entirely Australasian issue, all the articles
being contributed by Australian camera-
workers. The success of the experiment,
late though it is in the history of this estab-
lished journal, should be sufficient encourage-
ment for a much more liberal Australasian
flavor in its future issues. But the usual
exception must be taken to the statement
that this is its first “colonial” number.
“S.T.S.”: “Red Page” says that Wells’s
new novel ends with Lady HaTman “slam-
ming the door of the modern Doll’s House—
marriage” (though she may surrender to
some more masculine suitor later). Can’t see
the facts that way. The delightfully Meredith-
ian scene in which she pursues the gentle
Brumley through the woodland, and ends by
kissing him, seems to me to imply
that she abandons her claim to free-
dom, and accepts him in the most
practical way. Evidently the ingenious
Wells has devised a “Lady or Tiger?” finish;
for a critic like the “Red Page” can take it
as a “Doll’s House” ending, while the vast
majority, preferring Happy endings to a
story, will certainly accept this as being one.
The Writing on the Back-yard
Fence
Like the war, it has been a long time
coming, but now that it has come it is
awful. The Paralysis of a Nation, “a Candid
Indictment of the Policy, the Methods and
the Morale of the Labor-Socialist Party of
Australia,” by. Bruce Smith (The Liberal
Association of N.S.W.), draws the serious
attention of Australians to “the origin,
growth and steady development of a political
disease that has come to Australia from the
revolutionary hotbeds of Europe, and which,
if not treated promptly and drastically, as
we treat leprosy, small-pox and other foul
diseases in the human body, will in a short
time overwhelm the body-politic of our
otherwise fair land, and end in veritable re-
volution.” There! It is not as if we had
not been warned- Bruce Smith states that
even in 1887 he saw the approach of the
Socialistic Tiger, and publicly proclaimed
the. coming present dire condition of things.
It is a terrific indictment, before which the
horrors of the war pale. It was in 1890
that “there were sown upon the fair land of
Australia the seeds of a fell disease: a verit-
able social and political malady, which has
spread from year to year like an orchard
pest, sapping the people’s traditional love of
freedom, undermining their reverence for the
high conditions of peace, order and good
government, which had grown up and pre-
vailed among their progenitors for a thou-
sand years, and striking at the root of the
mutual goodwill that had hitherto existed
between the various clases that go to make
every truly progressive community.”
By the year 1902 “the paralysing policy of
Socialism had become the (Labor) party’s
openly-accepted creed; and the party has
since spread its baneful influence over a
wider and wider field, by the gradual de-
moralisation of all those to whom they have
communicated their infectious moral
malady.” Painful, indeed; but there is
worse to follow. “To such an extent has
this reckless and selfish disregard for the
rights and liberties of civilised persons been
carried, that the children of Pacific Island
laborers—brought into the community for
its own commercial purposes—who had been
schooled amid our civilised conditions, edu-
cated -and Christianised in our public schools,
and nurtured in the manners and customs
of our British conventions, have been
ruthlessly forced to leave their homes
and the country of their birth, and
submit to tie carried, against their will,
back to the islands of their savage ancestors,
and there to be left to the brutal conditions
and surroundings of a barbarous people,
probably.to.be gradually dragged down to
the uncivilised, heathenish and immoral
level of the native islanders.” Well, Bruce'
Smith might have saved at least one of
these bright, civilised, Christianised kiddies,
by adopting it into Ms own family, and
bringing it up, nurtured in the manners and
customs of our British conventions, pro-
bably to the fulfilment of a brilliant politi-
cal career as the heir and successor of
Bruce Smith. And deeply it pains this page
to hear the champion of the mottled and
varnished races speak of them as “heathenish
and immoral.”
The outlook for Australia is hopeless.
The Labor Party will writhe helplessly be-
neath this scathing indictment. A whole
chapter is given up to his charges; no less
than 32 paragraphs begin with these words
of doom: “I charge the Labor Party with
. ...” A sample will be sufficient : “I charge
them with ingratitude to the mother country,
and with ignoring Australia’s Imperial obli-
gation, by refusing in 1903 to contribute a
miserable pittance of £200,000 a year to the
up-keep of the British Navy; and with re-
fusing to equip Australia to assist in the de-
fence of the whole Empire, upon which the
safety of Australia depends, until con-
strained to do so, later, on political grounds,
by the force of loyal public opinion.”
Amazing! Bruce Smith remains the sole
survivor of the Prehistoric Tory; he is
thoroughly consistent: he has never moved
one inch from his early convictions; and he
is. the only Tory who has stood steadfast in
his proud prejudices, rejecting, or oblivious
to, the evidences of Socialism in his own
mother-country—the dreadful Socialistic
legislation of Lloyd George.

Self-Loading Shotgun, 12 Gauge
The Winchester Model 1911 Self-Loader is a wild-fowl gun par excellence. The
recoil ejects the fired shell, feeds a loaded shell from the magazine into the
chamber, cocks the hammer and leaves the gun ready to be fired again. In wild-
fowl shooting, the game often comes fast and when least expected. Shoot a
Winchester Self-Loader and you can always be prepared for a series of shots; for
the loading and firing of this gun are controlled by the trigger finger. As the re-
coil of this gun is used to operate the reloading mechanism, the “kick” of heavy
loads is minimized, making the gun very pleasant to shoot. It is a hammerless,
two-part, take-down, five-shot repeater, that works well with all standard loads.
The working parts being made of Nickel steel, make it strong and safe.
Ask your dealer to show you the Winchester that’s
LOADED BY RECOIL, CONTROLLED BY THE FINGER TRIGGER.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Hot Night.
A night so hot the very shadows sweltered
From the late rage of an appalling sun.
The earth seemed burned with fire, the grey
boughs sheltered
No cool, sweet spot from that wide torment
won.
Tasting the bitter of that evil hour,
All hearts seemed dust and all ill things had
power.
’Twas then we spoke. The fruit was parched
and rotten
That in its ripening gave a promise fair;
The things said could never be for-
gotten—
Mad sounds upon the hot and stagnant air.
The taint of that vile evening must remain
Eating our hearts through twilights sweet
with rain.
On life’s strong wind we float as floats a
feather.
The thistledown has purpose strong as ours;
Fate with her blowpipe breathes upon the
weather,
And dry and sere are all the soul’s best
flowers.
Had the thermometer dropped five degrees
Our words, remembered still, might soothe
and please.
N.S.W.
D.M.W.
The Three Calls.
The soft snow drifted down the creek
And swirled beneath the broken bridge;
The shadows played at hide-and-seek,
Among the trees on Charlie’s Ridge.
Across the moon there swept a cloud,
The moon herself was pale and cold
As any dead thing wrapped in shroud
For burial in grave-yard mould.
There is a bird that calls at night,
Along the reedy water ways.
A lost soul, calling vainly, might
A note of equal anguish raise.
And thrice across the burthened creek,
And thrice across the broken bridge,
While shadows played at hide-and-seek,
The Curlew called from Charlie’s Ridge.
I watched the sleeper on his bed.
He breathed indeed, a troubled breath—-
I think his dreams were of the Dead,
Of Death or, maybe, After-Death.
A second time the curlew called
(Called ever bird in such distress ?),
And then, as one enchained and thralled,
He rose and waited motionless.
The world outside was bright as noon —
The dead trees gleaming, row on row,
Unchequered in the sky the moon
Lit up the newly-fallen snow.
Where, months before, red fires had sped
There lay a burnt and blasted track,
And through the soft snow, whitely-spread.
The prone logs thrust their startling black.
It was a strangely-muted hour,
And silence reigned o’er earth and air;
It seemed as if some mystic Power
Had made a stillness everywhere.
’Twas such a hushed and haunted scene,
As one in ’wildering dreams might dree,
When, ears alert, he needs must lean
In cold, acute expectancy.
Or, maybe, some relentless sin
Stood dominant with deathless guilt,
The while the moonlight creeping in
Lay in chill pools upon his quilt.
When came the. first call down the night
(Called ever bird so hauntingly?)
He woke, and in the icy light
His dark eyes were a hurt to see.
For, like the eyes of one nigh-rlead,
Their depths were wild with fierce appeal.
And dread beyond the worst of dread
That, till that hour, ’tis ours to feel.
The world outside was bright as day,
And white the serried trees and stark;
Snow-weighted somewhere, far away
A limb fell crashing in the dark.
It woke an echo in the rocks.
It made the prowling dingo howl;
I heard the barking of a fox,
The hooting of a hooded owl.
And then the curlew called once more
(Called ever bird in such a wise?).
He took three paces down the floor
With out-thrust arms and staring eyes.
Three paces down the floor he took —-
The chill snow gleamed on bank and
bridge—•
He looked fas one who fears to look)
Across the creek to Charlie’s Ridge.
Ilis breath upon the window-pane
(He standing nigh it hollow-eyed)
Ran down and down like drops of rain
Or tears that will not be denied.
What ailed his thoughts I cannot tell,
The Thing he saw I did not see;
But this I know, and know full well,
It was not rock, nor stone, nor tree.
Perchance, if I had but his eyes
(How dark they were and fixed and wide !)
Then had I seen a dead man rise
With red blood gushing from his side.
And, maybe, I, too, visioned thus —
And standing in the icy light,
With yielding limbs leaf-tremulous —
Would stare and stare into the night!
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 4, 1915.
SERVING SHORT INJUNCTION ON
THE GERHOG.
The great war, which has been ferocious
enough in the past, doesn’t improve as time
goes on. The chief sufferer, of course, is
Belgium. That prosperous little State
struggled hard to keep out of the tiouble.
It meddled in no European complications.
It attacked nobody; in fact, it was so
peaceful that its Constitution forbade it to
make war on anybody, though it might
protect itself if assailed. It maintained not
a single warship, cruiser, gunboat, armed
punt or other means of offence at sea. . Its
army was fairly efficient, but it was liable
to serve in home defence only. -The
neutrality of its territory was guaranteed
by the Great Powers of Europe, and by
almost everybody except the Shak:speake
Society and the Baptist Conference. It
was 81 years previous to the outbreak ot
the present trouble since any Belgian soldier
fired a shot in anger upon European soil,
and even 81 years ago he was, as usual,
fighting on his own territory. When the
temporary union of Holland and Belgium
broke up, a philanthropic set of Powers
took a hand in refixing things—some of the
Powers were very philanthropic, and ideal-
istic in those days, for the Russian theories
of the Holy Alliance weren’t quite forgotten
—and resolved to establish, before the
bloodshot eye of all the world, the beautiful
spectacle of a progressive monarchy where
tranquillitv was fixed and assured and nailed
down. It was a Quaker experiment, com-
pared with which Pennsylvania was a not.
Belgium fell in because it refused to break
all the pledges and agreements under which
the kingdom was established, and allow its
territory to be used as the military stamp-
ing ground of the Gerhog, who had specially
pledged himself to respect its neutrality.
And it committed a sin which is beyond all
pardon, because its armies held up the
Kaiser’s legions for 14 days, and thus dis-
arranged the time-table of God’s senior
partner beyond repair. # #
The doings of the Gerhog in Belgium —
the rape and torture and mutilation, the
wholesale murder and wholesale destruction,
the use of Belgian women and children as a
rover for the invader’s troops, the levying
of contributions which have left the country
almost without a crust or a cent, the wiping
nut of manufactures and agncultuie, the
impressment of the people to dig trenches
for the Prussians, and to bury the Prussian
dead, and even to serve in the Prussian
armies—would seem incredible if the
Kaiser’s own bad characters hadn’t supplied
such abundant evidence of their truth. For
years Germany has had an output of mili-
tary books which broadly hinted _ that old
Fatherland, having a divine mission to
govern the world, wasn’t bound by the
ordinary rules of war. It was laid down
as a sort of principle that Germany wasn’t
quite human, but was almost divine. And
the idea that Germany isn’t quite human
begins to eat into the European conscience.
Belgium—-or at least the greater part of
that unfortunate country —is Germany’s one
conquest. No country ever suffered more
since the days of the Huns, and even the
Hun—-with his broad nose, and his _ greasy
mane, and his squat figure, and his long
arms, and his short legs, and his general
aspect of being an ape—hardly did worse
damage. The position of the Belgian now,
in the provinces that are under Gerhog
control, is interesting as the result of a great
experiment in the way of peace and inoffen-
siveness. Those of him who remain can
only leave the country by stealth. If they
get away they depart as paupers, and have
to ask foreign peoples for a crust and a bed.
Their industries are obliterated. They pass
here and there by surreptitious by-ways,
because on the high-roads they are liable
to meet Gerhog officers and soldiers who
search men and women on the off-chance
that there may be in their possession some
stray coppers which have escaped the
collectors of contributions. Around Brussels
some 500,000 people are reported as slowly
dying of starvation —equal to one-fourteenth
of the whole population of the kingdom as
it was before the war. And it is now inti-
mated that the sale of food may be entirely
stopped, so that whatever remains may
serve for the furnishing of the Gerhog
forces. Apparently, unless stronger assist-
ance comes at an early date, there will be
something like an end of Belgium—the
State whose only offence was that it stood
in the way of Gerhog ambition. As the
very bad poet Montgomery alleged on
another occasion, thpre will be “a blank
among the works of God.”
Those people who are in the thick of the
misery begin to wonder if they have enter-
tained a gorilla unawares. The Gerhog,
when he is back in the last ditch, will doubt-
less appeal to the recognised theories of
war, and the propositions of the Hague
Convention, and the Declaration of London,
and the ordinary doctrines and practices of
humanity, and demand to be treated accord-
ingly. The question is raised whether
ordinary laws and rules apply in his case.
Various writers suggest that he should be
served with what the law courts describe
as “short notice of injunction.” It is
proposed that he should receive intimation
that the damage he has already done in
contravention of the ordinary procedure of
civilised but hostile folks will have to be
naid for in cash, in addition to the usual
indemnity to cover all expenses. That for
future outrages the old Hebrew law of a
tooth for a tooth will be adopted—in other
words, that field will be burned for field,
and town for town, and that for every non-
combatant who is proved to have been killed
a Gerhog will be hanged. That the Kaiser
will be executed when captured, and his
family and his relations exiled, after
confiscation of their private property. Also
that trade with Germany will be prohibited ;
that no Gerhog shall be admitted into, any
foreign country, and that any foreigner
who is married to a Gerhog may dissolve
the marriage at will, on the ground that the
other partner to the bargain is suspected
of being one of the lower animals, who
stood upright with intent to deceive and
wore a mean disguise of clothes.
* *• * *
In brief, the Kaiser’s demented conviction
that he and his crowd may break all recog-
nised usages, but that his. opponents must
always obey them in their dealings with
him. threatens to make Europe tired. The
tiredness only shows itself as yet in casual
writings here and there, but it is liable to
extend. If civilisation is to burst up, a lot
of people say it may as well burst on both
sides, and thus abbreviate the worry. In the
last resort the Gerhog people are responsi-
ble for their Kaiser,. who only exists by
their permission. Being a cultured people
and a shining light, they are liable for
their soldiers, and their officers, and their
Government. No great nation can shelter
itself wholly and permanently behind the
orders of one lunatic or monstrosity. Every
civilised community makes, or maintains, its
own monstrosity. So a craving begins to
prevail in those sections of Europe which
have lost most heavily by Gerhog horrors,
that short notice of injunction shall be served.
In other words, that the soldier and officer-
even the field-marshal or prince or Kaiser —
who exceeds the speed limit in future shall
be treated as a common murderer, when cap-
tured and identified. In some more words,
that if the Gerhog nation takes the profit
of its atrocities it must pay not only m
money, but in flesh and blood and fire and
desolation and public contempt. If it puts
the blame on its Kaiser it must take on the
job of civilising its Kaiser, or getting rid of
him. If it can neither civilise its Kaiser
nor get rid of him it must admit itself a
failure among nations.
* * * *
The new literature which appears here and
there in Europe suggests that culture has
been set back many years. For that the
bumptious Prussian murderer carries the lia-
bility. In order to save time and money and
men the Allies, it is pleaded, mav be driven
to shed the garment of art and literature
and godliness as an encumbrance, and go
bald-headed for the Hoiienzotxern accord-
ing to his own methods —and for his people
if they continue to support him and his
methods. It is a lamentable business, but
too much Kaiser is demoralising to even the
best-regulated enemv.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Still Further Consideration.
It is a depressing circumstance that the
Administrator of the Northern Territory has
been around in Melbourne consulting with
the Minister who is responsible for the show
as to the best thing to be done. Too much
consultation has gone a long way towards
killing the Territory. It is always being
preached to death by wild curates, or talked
to death by tame philanthropists. There is
a weary farce of pretending that it. is . a
strange, lunatic place with straws in its
hair, and that none of the ordinary rules of
business or conduct apply to it. Hence all
these heaps of consultation. When it was
decided that something ought to be done for
Westralia the transcontinental railway was
started, along the shortest and cheapest
route that could be found. Nobody consulted
worth or even 2d. It was held that
the position was obvious. The Northern
Territory railway, to the construction of
which the Commonwealth is pledged, will be
a cheaper proposition than the Westralian
affair. It will run through better country.
Also it will develop the Commonwealth’s own
property —not a State property. But, un-
fortunately, owing to the shape. of Australia,
there are possibilities of diverting the north-
and-south railway, whereas the east-and-west
one had a single possible route ahead of it.
If the plain, simple agreement, made when
S. Australia handed over the Territory, for
the completion of the road from Port Aug-
usta to Port Darwin can be broken, a lot of
things may happen. The line may be
changed from a transcontinental to a coastal
affair. It may be brought to Townsville or
Brisbane or Sydney. Every member in the
three eastern States, save the representative
of One Horse Gulch, and possibly the mem-
ber for Big Tree in Gippsland, sees a vision
of the great undertaking coming his way, and
ending right at his ash-barrel. The result
is deadlock. While all the subterranean
negotiations are going on the situation is
disguised by the pretence that the Territory
is in some way different from any other bit of
land, and that before even a mile of line is
built two centuries of profound contempla-
tion are required. The wear and tear of
Gilkuth and the travelling expenses of
Gilruth are consequently tremendous ; and
if Gilruth was a drinking man—which,
thank Heaven, he isn’t —and his refreshments
on long trips were also debited to the Com-
monwealth, the result would be ruinous. All
this leads to nothing save that it postpones
the evil day when the route will have to be
decided, whereupon the Ministry will make
one or two ungrateful friends and a heap of
enemies.
A New Country and an Old Story.
Cockatoo Dock (Sydney) has had a feeling
even since Christmas that relations were
strained. There had been small, previous
troubles, but the big trouble arose when the
employees were offered double f>ay to work
during the Christmas holidays, because it
was a matter of urgency to push on the con-
struction of certain war vessels. They de-
manded treble pay, and as the Labor
Government of the Commonwealth has to
look hard at ways and means just now no
business was done. The dock closed up for
the holidays, and the urgent work was post-
poned. The employees can’t be blamed very
seriously over the matter, because, hard-
borrowing and hard-importing politicians
have so assiduously preached the idea that
because this is “a new country” there ought
to be 50s. in its f, that it is difficult to get
things into proper focus. The alleged fact
that we are so poor that we have to borrow
more per inhabitant in time of peace than
any other land on earth is set down to the
newness of the place. So is our alleged ina-
bility to manufacture our own goods. On
the other hand, the alleged fact that we are
so rich that we can pay higher wages for
shorter hours of labor than almost any other
community is charged up against the blessed
newness of Australia. In fact, the professed
freshness 'of the country accounts,. in the
abundant vocabulary of the politician, for
almost everything—good, bad and indifferent.
To return to the Cockatoo matter, the Dock
had a picnic on Anniversary Day, and there
was food, also oratory. This is an extract
from the latter :
Mr. R. Howe, M.H.R., said Ms idea of manage-
ment at the dock was not that of a general, to
dominate the men—a kind of militarism in industry
that he did not like —but of a manager, who would
consult the men, and from whom they could obtain
the redress of grievances. No doubt Mr. ,King-
Salter had come here with the idea of carrying out
Admiralty regulations as in the Old Country, but
such regulations had to be interpreted in the light
of the circumstances surrounding industrial life IN
A NEW COUNTRY.
The Bulletin has no desire to be dis-
agreeable, yet it proposes that any politician
or pressman who gets off the “new country”
gag after St. Valentine’s day of 1915 shall
be struck off the rolls and disqualified for
life. His job should be taken from him, and
his conversation for all the balance of his
days should be restricted to the weather.
Australia had three wonderful streaks of
good luck, inasmuch as it always escaped
war; it got the landed estate of a whole con-
tinent for nothing; and it discovered the
richest goldfields in all the world’s history.
It had two amazing streaks of ill-fortune,
inasmuch as it acquired a set of politicians
who said it was so young and rich that it
could only live by borrowing money from
poorer and older communities; and a set
which alleged that, because of its
young and splendid disadvantages, it
couldn’t do any skilled work for itself,
and must depend on imports. So it
borrows and imports because it is poor .and
crude; and it pays high wages because it is
rich and early. And it does both those
things because, according to the brilliant
peroration of R. H. Howe, M.H.R., and many
political time-servers of his kind, it is a
place that has just been invented. Some-
thing ought to be done to this amazing and
complicated complication.
Three Cheers for the Trust.
Dear Bulletin,- —It is a pity that so much
should be said about the foreign Trusts
which take goods out of Australia (the Meat
Trust, for instance), while so little is said
about the ones which bring goods into Aus-
tralia. The Meat Trust may aim at con-
trolling the Australian beef supply, and it
may, when it gets its hooks right into the
business, pay less for the flesh victual than
it should do. Still it promotes local
industry, for it urges the community to
grow animals; and it brings United States
money into the country (not so much as 1
should do, perhaps) to pay wages and to pay
for bullocks. On the other hand the UU
Trust, and the Harvester Trust, and the
Picture Theatre Film concern, and the sho
which supplies boot-making machinery, an
all the rest of the importing concerns so
beloved of Fisher and Co. take money out
of the country and repress local
The meat-exporting monopolists who ha
settled in Australia help this country to keep
solvent, and the worst that can be said ot
them is that they do it through purely selfish
motives. They at least make us lessi de-
pendent on foreign loans and foreigi P
talists. The machinery-importing and on-
importing and clothes-importing and jo&-
lots-importing monopolists are QUi
Bill’s Mate.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Turkey seems to have gone quite mad with muddle
and poverty and defeat.—The Bulletin.
Out of the murk he came
Slipshod and suddenly;
Into the dust he goes
Swiftly and finally.
Thrown where the garbage of nations is
flung.
For the time of the great cleaning-up has
arrived, and neither Islam nor Europe
has any further use for the Ottoman.
Out of the murk he came,
Waving a meat-chopper,
Smiting the world with the
Whiskers of faith.
Islam amazed at him,
Honored him, boosted him,
Made him Chief Plunderer,
Gave him first call on her
Knife and her prayers.
Then he went like the wind of the desert,
blowing a tornado of snakes, and burst
in the door of Constantinople.
Sharp was the knife of him,
Bitter the boots of him,
Idly he chortled
The name of the Prophet,
Mopped up Belgrade with a
Whoop of his pants.
Startled Vienna beheld the long spears of
him,
Leaped at the danger
And kicked like an emu,
Taught the proud vanguard
Of Turkey to trot.
Then he went with great rapidity across the
Danube, carrying his wounds and scars
of battle mostly in his spine and the
soles of his feet-
Slipshod and suddenly
Through the long centuries,
Bumped with some violence
Out of his plunder grounds,
Slowly his threadbare
Whiskers retired.
Calm as an over-ripe
Case of tomatoes
The bag of his pants _
Grew accustomed to kicks.
Once in a while with a start he awakened,
Felt the old madness rush hot in his blood.
Then, in spite of the fact that Greece,
Bessarabia, Moldavia, Wallachia, Ser-
via, Bosnia, Herzegovina, Bulgaria,
Roumelia, the Crimea,, and large tracts
of his dominions in Asia and Africa had
been torn from his futile grasp, he
began killing on general principles and
causing suffering in a large, almost-
German way.
Fierce as a shark among
Surf-bathing nursemaids,
Flabby as whale blubber,
Solemn as ink,
Europe observed him,
Examined him carefully,
Prayerfully, wonderingly, uncomprehendingly,
Praised him —or told him
To go and get work.
Then a tradition arose and was fostered,
Vaunting his valor in spite of ill-fortune,
Called him the best fighting man on the
continent,
Though in such evident neeed of insecticide,
Also of reading and writing and soap.
But he wasn’t the best fighting man by
several streets. He had been, but he
had degenerated into a lamentable slack-
trousered shirk who only shone by con-
trast with other shirks even more
lamentable. And so Macedonia, Crete,
Albania and part of Thrace were taken
away from him, and he sat foaming at
the mouth by the Bosphorus.
Slipshod and indolent,
Blind but still truculent,
Waiting for fate in the shape of a boot,
Far to the north he observed a bold gentle-
man
Playing a game that had points ot resem-
blance
To pastimes the world recognised as his own.
“Truly,” he said, “if my friend be an infidel,
Actions like his show a glimmer of faith.
His is the sport that I learned in my
infancy—
By the prophet’s true beard he is brother
of mine!”
So with perfect willingness, but a general
absence of bootlaces, the Turk joined
brother William in his attempt to beat
civilisation on a knock-out.
Frightful the pants of him
nagged to nakedness.
Slouching to battle
Unkempt and unfed,
He who had been, ;
But decidedly wasn’t ,
An object of terror
Woke laughter and scorn.
“Look at Bill’s mate,” cried the
World in astonishment,
“Turning his hind rags
For Europe to kick !”
So the Russians kicked, and the British
kicked, and the French kicked, and the
Servians kicked. At first they'okicked
singly, and then they kicked altogether,
and the Turk went out on his purple
ear, and even Islam joined in the
laughter.
Out of the murk he came,
Slipshod and suddenly,
Into the dark he goes
Swiftly and finally, . .
Flung where the garbage of nations is Hung.

war on the Trust which pays us money and

Fisher advance agent.—Yours abundantly,
Henry Horsecollar.

Let Us Contend.
If there is any merit at all about the old
Party Government System it is in the fact
that it makes for the persistent criticism of
Ministers. As an outcome of the war its
last justification vanishes. No one seri-
ously criticises anybody in high authority,
under penalty of being accused of pro-Ger-
manism. In the Australian Parliament,
where Defence matters are concerned, there
is a certain amount of reason in this —and it
is in accordance with the eternal cussedness
of things that what criticism has come of
late from the Opposition has been aimed
almost wholly at the Defence Department.
But there is no reason why there should be
a truce to comment in regard to the acts
of Ministers who have nothing to do with
Defence matters, and there is no shadow of
excuse for silence on the question of State
Ministers and their doings. The effect is to
turn the Ministries of the day into so many
autocracies; and an autocracy is an unpleas-
ant thing to have about the premises for
however brief a period. The present war
has evidently come to stay for a considerable
time—possibly for years. If Parliamentary
criticism of Ministers is going to be
accepted during all that time as an indication
of disloyalty to. the Empire, old wrongs are
going to flourish tremendously and new
wrongs are going to be born. The perils of
the situation have already been shown in
connection with the Censorship. The cen-
sors, all over Australia, have, to put it
mildly, shown great incompetence. With
doubtless the best of intentions they have
done the worst service to the cause which
they presumably have at heart. At a time
when the Germans were surging over all
opposition, the press was allowed to speak
only of victory. No one who had access to
an atlas was deceived; it made a laughing-
stock of us abroad; and it gave the citizen
of fighting age an excuse to hang back at a
time when he was most needed. The censor
has been the first real despot in our brief
island story, and the verdict on him is that
he is a failure. Last week C. G. Wade, of
N. S. Wales, announced that “peace inside
and outside Parliament was of more import-
ance than anything,” and the local papers
gave, him three cheers for his clear-sighted
patriotism. Politician and press were
alike wrong. If criticism is useful at normal
times, it. is equally useful in a crisis, pro-
vided it is administered in the sort of spirit
which the public insists on just now. There
is nothing that is worse for a Ministry,
nothing that puts it out of touch with the
general feeling sooner, than the knowledge
that, whatever hapoens, its members are
immune from assault. What the country
needs to recognise as soon as possible is the
circumstance that the war has become a sort
of institution. The Government of the
Commonwealth and of the States must pro-
ceed, in normal fashion, side by side with
it. Where a Federal or State Minister errs
on a financial or tariff or other issue he
should be. told about it in the House in the
old forthright fashion. In fact, he should be
spoken to, if anything, in more forthright
terms than ever. The fact that the country
is in danger is the noorest of reasons why its
Governments should be given a blank cheque
and told to go and do its worst.
The Victorian Irrigation Fraud.
Unless Victoria produces an axe and a
leader with the will and energy to use it,
another of that State’s great undertakings
will. go to more or less permanent smash,
leaving another load of debt for posterity to
liquidate. The matter was lately set forth
by irrigation-expert Elwood Mead, at
Shepparton, to the public in general and
Premier Peacock and three of his Ministers
in particular. In effect, if the present Min-
isterial intentions with regard to irrigation
are. carried out, Mead says the whole State
policy will result in a fiasco. Eastward
from the Goulburn head-works runs a
channel capable of irrigating 40,000 acres,
and “if the whole of those 40,000 acres are
not being irrigated the channel and the
water are wasted.” The same Goulburn
scheme provides for the .adequate watering
of 200,000 acres in the Rodney district.
“Here are small tracts producing up to their
limit, but alongside them are large tracts
producing nothing.” Money is thus thrown
away and water wasted. The present policy
is to run channels wherever anyone may
want water. These channels supply intense
culturists here and there along the route,
but pass acres on acres of irrigable lands.
In a dry season the negligent persons grab
as much water as they want, leaving the
regular and persistent irrigationist at the
end of the channel to go without. The lat-
ter’s so-called water right is accord-
ingly a fraud and a sham. Not only is this
policy shiftless and extravagant, but it is
wasteful in another and direct way, because,
though in running (say) three acre-feet of
water along a channel you may lose one
acre-foot by soakage and evaporation, you
can run six acre-feet along the same channel
without losing more than 1| feet. Mead’s
remedy is the one advocated long ago—levy
an irrigation rate on lands supplied with
water, so as to compel the owners to use
the fluid to the land’s full capacity or sell
out to those who will. Then Mead would
reduce all irrigation schemes to the spider’s
web plan—the head works in the centre, with
radiating channels wherever needed, and
extended only as the lands already served
are taken up and utilised. The present plan,
which works out much like the track of a
fly on a wall, is utterly wasteful. More-
over, it is financially immoral, inasmuch as
at public cost it enhances the value of locked-
up territory, and thus plays into the hands
of that curse of the country, the loafing land
monopolist.
The Silver Bullet.
The daily press is still full of complaints
by elderly people on the subject of the young
Australians’ alleged unwillingness to volun-
teer. There is a type whose blood boils with
rage every time he sees a civilian male under
30. Judging by the way he writes, nothing
would please him more than to see the youth
of the Commonwealth blown to pieces en
masse, just to teach it a lesson. The theatres
feature songs which urge the young men to
get off to the front, and pour scorn on those
who stay. behind. The old men applaud
these ditties madly. A great deal of this
agitation begins to look like an impudent
attempt on the part of the elderly to obscure
the particular war-issue which affects them-
selves. The British Chancellor of the
Exchequer has said that “money and
material will be greater factors in this war
than men,” and that “victory will probably
go to the owner of the last 100 millions.”
For months the efficacy of “the silver bullet”
has been on every pavement strategist’s
tongue. Now, the silver bullet is chiefly for
the old and middle-aged to mould. Well-to-
do old men can’t go to the front. But they
can subscribe money freely. Regardless of
the effect on the import trade, they can
support a fiscal policy which will enable
this country to start making war materials
without delay. And they can advocate com-
pulsory service for adult males, and accept
the resulting disorganisation of their staffs
as one of the sacrifices demanded of them
by the Empire. Are they acting in that
way? Everyone knows they are not. The
importers are as anti-Protectionist as ever.
Many large employers of labor grow cold
at the mere thought of “conscription.” And
as regards what may be called spot-cash
patriotism—well, any issue of any paper
will supply a means of judging. For in-
stance :—•
The authorities are apparently to appeal in vain
for motor ambulances. So far, none have been
received at district headquarters. One Field Ambu-
lance Company was despatched with the First Con-
tingent, but it was only equipped with TO horse-
drawn vehicles. The latest advices from the British
War Office state that each ambulance section must
be provided with seven motor and three horsfe ambu-
lances. These vehicles cost approximately £550
each.
No State’s war fund reaches a quarter of a
million. Four citizens who met casually in
a Sydney club six years ago gave £40,000
among them to the foolish old Dread-
nought fund in the same casual fashion as
a tram-guard might have given 2d. No one
has given £lO,OOO to the War fund, though,
in the present crisis, the Empire is not
merely threatened with attack by Germany,
but is battling for its life with that nation.
In fact, a study of the lists goes to show
that the rich man who has weighed in with
as much as £2OO is a rarity. The military
authorities state that recruits have been
offering freely since the beginning of the
war, and that more can be got when they
are wanted. That is to say, the young men
of Australia are freely offering their lives,
which is all most of them have to offer. How
many of the men and women whose incomes
range from £2OOO to £40,000 per annum
have offered anything like the sum in cash
at which they value life and health? How
many would risk death or mutilation or an
attack of cholera for £20,000? And how
many have given a quarter of that, to them,
paltry sum? If any one class is failing in
its duty over this war it is not the young
who are poor, but the old who are rich.
Even if our capitalists can’t draw the sword
for the Empire they can at least draw the
cheque, and the unanimity with which they
are not doing it is disgraceful. \

The Sevenpenny Drink.
The protest of “Steady Drinker” :
The publican is one of the few who earns big
money easily. The community has grown to associ-
ate him with diamonds, a behemoth appearance, and
cigars. So long as he behaved at all decently a big
section of the public has, nevertheless, been willing
that he should be guaranteed the security of his
license. • In this spirit his patrons have for years
allowed themselves to be robbed, and never seri-
ously kicked. But the trade may presume on that
too much.
The other day the Australian Government was
forced into levying some mild war taxes on the
nation. The publicans—l write in N. S. Wales —■
decided immediately that the new imposts necessi-
tated a rise in prices. Let us see what that means
by considering the position of the sixpenny bar.
The trade has carried a resolution to put up prices
there to sevenpence. The whisky with the largest
sale costs to the trade 545. 6d. the dozen, or 4s. 6£d.
a bottle. The publican figures that 16 drinks run
to the bottle, so that the return is Bs. per bottle at
sixpence and 9s. 4d. at sevenpence. But measured
by the quartern, which is more than the average
drinker takes each time, the bottle runs to 20 drinks,
and so returns 10s. at sixpence and 11s. Bd. at
sevenpence. Still, taking the publican’s own estimate
of 16 drinks, by raising this price to sevenpence he
makes an extra 16s. a dozen. He does this to meet
the Government war excise, which is 6s. the dozen!
Of course, he gives in soda —at Is. a dozen. As
against the soda he sells soft stuff. A typical line
costs him Id. a bottle, and he sells it at 6d. Also,
he gets 24 per cent, discount on his whisky and
sells the empty bottles for Is. a dozen.
For bottled lagers he pays from 6s. 9d. to Bs. a
dozen. Say, 74d. the bottle “in and out.” Each
bottle holds five drinks. The return at sixpence a
glass is half a crown a bottle, and now it is one
penny short of 3s. English stout costs him 12s. 3d.
the dozen, and the return is the same.
But his star robbery is with draught lager. This
costs him Is. 9d. the gallon, so if sold at sixpence a
pint it returns 4s. a gallon. If sold at sixpence the
half-pint it: retur.ng Bs. the gallon. But what it
really is sold by is the glass—not the long-sleever,
but the saloon-bar glass—and then it goes 20 drinks,
or 10s. to the gallon at sixpence a drink, and 11s. Bd.
to the gallon at sevenpence a drink.
There are a few hotels which, defiant of the Trade,
have refused to ask the sevenpenny rate. As a
means of bringing these recalcitrants to heel, the
Trade has approached the wholesalers, and attempted
to get the supplies of the rebels stopped. This action
amounts at Common Law to a criminal offence. But
that is a side-issue. The features that chiefly con-
cern beer and spirit drinkers are that the pub busi-
ness was capable of being run at a colossal profit
under the old system, and that the pub-keepers have
seized on the war to turn their concerns from stead-
ily-paying gold mines into Goltondas. The new
move plays suicidally into the hands of • the Aboli-
tionists, and it provides another argument • in support
of the nationalisation of drink.
The Bad Man at the Front.
Something is said about the weeding-out of
“undesirables” —drunks and gamblers, and
soldiers who howl at night, and fellows who
haunt the back streets of Cairo during the
dark hours and wink at Egyptian girls—
from the Australian forces in Egypt. These
dubious characters, it is alleged, are to be
sent back to Australia in disgrace, so that
they may marry undesirably, and rear un-
desirable families, while the good men go
to the fi’ont, and do battle with the Turk,
and are shot by Abdul Fez. This idea that
the worst citizen should be carefully pre-
served from destruction, and get a free
passage home to the place where he used
to be a nuisance, and become one of the
fathers of the community, and help to make
our laws, and even go into Parliament, while
the best citizen is killed, looks like an
absurdity. Every undesirable who is already
in the foi’ce should be carefully preserved.
Every criminal, hunchback, forger or
other bad character who seeks to enlist
should be enlisted with enthusiasm. The
drunkard should be taken on the evidence of
his breath and his nose. The worst scoundi’els
should have extra pay, if necessary; and
extra special uniforms, and on the day of
battle they should go first, being the folk who
can best be spared. They would certainly
kill somebody before they died. The front
ranks should include the most quarrelsome
drunk, the most chronic beer-chewer, the
worst poet, the hardened loafer, the publican
who adulterates his liquor, the counter-
luncher, the inveterate assault-and-battery
person, Bill Sikes, Butler, Deeming, all
the discoverable bushrangers, all the avail-
able pirates, the man with the largest
number of convictions, the constant mur-
derer, the religious maniac, the sexual per-
vert, Oscar Wilde, the deviser of indecent
postcards, the Plunchback of Notre Dame,
the Outlaw of Iceland and Jack the Kipper.
And when they had done their bit of trouble
with the enpmy the reputable citizens who
came behind would have an easier row to hoe.
“Happy Jack’s” Fish.
Having accumulated a deficit of close on
£900,000 in little more than three years, and
lifted Westralia’s debt well up to £lOO per
head, “Happy Jack”- Scaddan is dazzled
with the magnificence of his achievements,
and casts round for fresh worlds to conquer.
His brickyards haven’t cheapened bricks, his
State sawmills haven’t reduced the price of
timber, his steamers and meat shops have
not caused the price of the grill and the roast
to pine away. Nevertheless, when lately
there was a howl for cheaper fish he was
inspired to buy a trawler, which, he assured
the populace, would knock the bottom out of
the Dago “ring,” and enable the community
t.o revel in sea produce for next to nothing.
Of course, the usual thing happened. After
an absence of a fortnight the trawler turned
up with eight tons of fish, which meant about
30dwt. to each Perth inhabitant. To keep
faith with the public and his fish stall occu-
pied, Scaddan is therefore now buying sup-
plies from the Dagoes at sixpence and seven-
pence per lb., and, consequently, either has
to sell at a higher price than fish ever
fetched before or show another big loss. But
that doesn’t trouble “Happy Jack.” Like
the Kaiser he is a universal genius—ship-
owner, fisherman. fishmonger, publican,
brickmaker, financier, general statesman —
in fact, he has done everything save keep the
Treasury solvent and write an opera. He
rushes about assuring the State that, though
the future looks a trifle blue, rain and peace
will make everything in the garden bloom
again. The original Micawber never had
the luck of the Micawber at the Westralian
Treasury. Wilkins was sure something
would turn up, though he had no idea what
it might be. Jack has a blessed and definite
war to comfort him, and a still more blessed
though not quite so definite peace to look
forward to for putting all things right.

Many Bankrupt Counties of N.S.W.
The protest against the wholesale reduction
of the country railway services in N.S.W.
is loud, but unhappily there aren’t sufficient
people in the reduced areas to. protest
in a convincing fashion. Still the
facts remain that the railways repre-
sent the bulk of the “permanent and
reproductive” loan works of the State, and
more than half of them were run at a dead
loss long before the war started or the recent
little drought was heard of. The loss of
more than half the N.S.W. railways was
bad in the rich, prosperous year of 1912, and
it was worse in the rich, prosperous
year of 1913. Also it had been bad
for 20 or more years previous to that. Some
people propound a theory that the reason
why many remote country railways don’t
pay is that the craze for shutting up every
deep-water port except Sydney makes the
country bare and bald and scant of popula-
tion. They hint that the outlying lines don’t
pay for want of people to use them. In
other words, that their districts suffer from
premeditated emptiness and a conspiracy to
prevent inhabitants —all this being done with
the misguided idea that the one reliable way
to boom Sydney and the bloated landlords
thereof is to kill every other place. Cer-
tainly the policy of killing the country has
been pursued strenuously. At the date of
the census of 1891 the population of N.S.W.
—apart from the people of Norfolk Island
and the handful of sailors op board ships in
various harbors —>was 1,118.250. and the"
three counties of Cumberland. Northumbei’-
land and Camden contained 574,950 people,
or 15,825 more than one-half. In 1901 the
population was 1,346,720, and the; three
counties of Cumberland. Northumberland
and Camden held 702.937 inhabitants, or
29,577 moi’e than one-half. In 1911 the
population was 1.638.578, and two counties
—Cumberland and Northumberland : in other
words, Sydney and Newcastle —showed a
total of 843,099, which was 23,810 in excess
of half the population. By 1921, if the
One-Big-City policy has any luck, one county
—Cumberland —out of all the 144 counties
of N.S.W., will hold more than half the
population. Meanwhile the railways in the
other 143 counties are naturally worked at
a loss. How can it be otherwise?
The Penny Crocodile.
Last Thursday that incurable advocate of
cheap imports, the Sydney Telegraph,
broke into a weep about the sufferings of
the women toilers of Australia. A sample
of the lamentation ensues:—
In some classes of factories where the wages are
fixed at from 235. to 255., a big percentage of the
girls are going through a particularly bad time. In
the panic following immediately upon the war declar-
ations numbers of hands were dispensed with ; but
the pendulum having quickly swung back to normal,
most of the girls were reinstated. The few weeks
away from employment, however, threw them so far
back, from a monetary point of view, that they have
had no opportunity of regaining lost ground, especi-
ally as, since the subsidence of the Christmas rush,
the greater number of them have been on “half-time.”
This means that instead of the weekly 235. or 255.
they are forced to exist upon 11s. 6d. or 12s. fid.
It is impossible to imagine how so small a sum can
be spread over the needs of seven long days, or how
these girls, who. taking them collectively, are a
bright and intelligent body, anxious to maintain
their respectability, and with a feminine regard for
appearances, can manage to keep on the narrow path,
with their bodies half-starved and their souls crying
out for only the things that would make life toler-
ably easy.
But, after all, why should the Freetrade
paper repine? Australia, by reason of its
low tariff, imports annually millions of
pounds worth of the goods which these girls
might otherwise be making, and taxes them
to pay interest on the loans which help to
pay for the goods which they aren’t allowed
to make. And if anyone suggests that the
girls should have more work the Telegraph
accuses him of trying to put up prices, to
obstruct commerce, and to knock a hole in
the immortal doctrines of Cobden. In fact,
the old King-street publication wants to have
the tariff reduced, so that there may be more
imports and less work. Someone ought to
buy a handkerchief for this weeping penny
crocodile.

The mid-ocean “removal” of Count von
Keller from a liner which was taking him
back from a “secret mission to the United
States.” and his subsequent trip to Gibraltar
in a British cruiser, is about the last pos-
sible proof that Britain really does rule the
waves. The dreadful humiliation of Ger-
many may be measured by applying the same
set of circumstances to Britain. Sup-
pose the British fleets had been chased
into port and that somebody of the
calibre of Curzon or Cromer tried
to sneak out to Japan by crossing secretly
to Marseilles and there embarking on a
French ship. Imagine the French ship
stopped by a German cruiser, the distin-
guished Englishman taken off and forcibly
transferred to the nearest stronghold
of the enemy. What an agony of shame
would rack England! Proud Germany,
which has just suffered the unimaginable
humiliation, will probably retaliate by blow-
ing up some Norfolk farmer’s hen-house
with a Zeppelin bomb.
It seems as though the Gerhogs have
exhausted one of the great advantages with
which they began the war. At the outset
they had a whole bagful of carefully-pre-
pared tricks, and they worked them with
good results. But a trick is no good after
it has been found out. For instance, the
German submarines operated for a while
under cover of trawlers carrying a neutral
flag; but now an honest-looking trawler
merely invites suspicion where formerly it
served to deceive. So, also, it is now prac-
tically certain that naturalised Gerhogs in
motor-cars directed the course of the air-
ships which recently bombed a bit of Britain’s
border; and that sort of trick is not good
for oftener than once. In desperation the
creature has now either to hide or fight more
or less in the open ; and then things happen
like the very distressing accident to the
Blucher.
In the N.S.W. Legislative Council last week
Dr. Doyle directed the Health Minister’s
attention to the possibility of contagious
disease being spread throughout Australia by
retux-ning soldiers. Flowers replied that
he would “consult the Federal authorities in
the matter.” That many soldiers are suffer-
ing from disease, acquired either in Egypt
or on the way there, has been stated by the
official journalistic representative of the Com-
monwealth Government, Captain C. W.
Bean ; and such a report ought to be fol-
lowed by the authorities taking the most
stringent precautions against the spread of
the trouble upon the return of the troops.
It would be in the interests of Australia if
the Defence Department utilised this oppor-
tunity to confer with the Special Committee
appointed by the last Australasian Medical
Congress.

Now that the N. S. Wales liquor retailer
has, by the grace of the Government, been
enabled to wring additional profit out of
consumers, The Bulletin asks once more
when the Board of Health proposes to com-
pel the publican to remove the danger of
the filthy drinking utensil. Tubs of standing
water remain unchanged until they are
nauseating. Into this stew glasses are
dipped, half dried or dirtied by towels that
would be unclean in a lavatory, and passed
over the counter again for fresh (or not so
fresh) customers. Amongst the drinkers in
the bars are sufferers from every imaginable
disease, from influenza to syphilis; yet,
without the least remonstrance from the
Department, established to preserve the
.public’s health, the bar-keeper is permitted
to spread any and all germs broadcast. In
Victoria every person who serves food,
liquors, etc., in any licensed premises is
bound under penalty to thoroughly clean,
in clear running water, all eating or drink-
ing utensils immediately they ar.e done with,
and occasionally an offender against the
regulation is discovered and fined. In N. S.
Wales, while there are some publicans who
follow a similar rule, there are so many who
don’t that they are a menace to the com-
munity and a reproach to the Government

HIGHEST AWARDS AT ALL THE GREAT EXHIBITIONS. «
Our Old Friend, the War.
During last week the torpid troops —a good
many millions of them —have been getting out
of their trenches as the days grew a trifle
longer, and shooting with renewed energy.
The war bill is now alleged to be £10,000,000
per day, which is possibly an exaggeration.
The German has been making another effort
to break through the French lines and reach
Calais, though nobody has yet decided what
good that shallow port and picturesque old
town would be to him. It is the nearest
place to England, and the handiest place
from which to make grimaces at England,
but owing to the increased dimensions of
warships it lost all value as a naval port
200 or 300 years ago. Anyhow, the attempt
to break through has so far failed. All the
parties concerned are bringing up gigantic
reinforcements, and Kitchener’s alleged
statement that the war will really begin in
May appears to have evidence behind it.
The boys who have just come of age for
service are being gathered in by hundreds
of thousands, and put through their drill,
and presently there will be a fresh war on
top of the old one, though the old one was
certainly big enough. A few unarmed mer-
chant ships belonging to the Allies have
been sunk by German submarines. One of
them was the property of the Shaw, Savill
Shipping Co., which makes the second
Britain-Maoriland trader to be sacrificed
in the great scrap. The boats were
insured, and Maoriland didn’t lose much, but
the anger of Auckland and the rage of Wanga-
nui will probably help on the business of
enlistment. The main German fleet still
keeps out of sight, and accumulates barna-
cles in harbor. Whether it has_ got cold feet,
or whether it lias a card up its sleeve and
four aces hidden in its boots is uncertain.
The Grand Duke Nicholas has lived up to
his reputation as a plausible peer as
well as a great general. By a persistent
process of hovering he created the impres-
sion that his next big effort would be made
in Hungary. It was characteristic of the
silent man that he is now harrying the
north-east of Prussia, where he was hardly
expected at this cold season. Italy and
Roumania still dream of joining the Allies.
The Turkish army which tried to invade
Russia by way of the Caucasus and Ararat
and the debris of Noah’s Ark is scarcely
heard of these days, so badly has it been
wrecked among the snows; but the Un-
speakable is making a genuine effort to in-
vade Egypt, and the Anglo-Austral-Egyptian
army, which is tired of looking at the Pyra-
mids and the old Sphinx, hopes to see his
topknot on the horizon at any moment.
Persia, which is a hopeless, shambling
country, first demonstrated on the side of
the Allies and then drifted into the camp of
the enemy. So Russia has taken it on.
along with Turkey and Austria and
a share of Germany. Persistent rumors
drift around that Hungary will try
to break away from Austria and make
peace by itself. The Hungarian com-
plains that the Austrian persistently_ shoves
him into the post of danger, while the
Austrian complains that the German shoves
him into the post of danger. Also, the Turk
says the same thing about the German,
whose unpopularity appears to be almost un-
limited. There are still immense fighting
possibilities about the German-Austrian-
Turkish league, but it becomes more and
more evident that the Kaiser at Berlin —
however sick and sorry he may be —is the
driving force. President Wilson, of the
United States, has sent his best wishes to
the Kaiser on his birthday. The neces-
sity to pass the hat round for Servia
becomes apparent once more. Even Bel-
gium hasn’t been reduced so completely
to hunger and ashes and desolation
as the little isolated kingdom which
stood the first shock of the war. According
to a descriptive writer who lately wandered
that way : “It is three months since a Ser-
vian baby has been known to smile.” The
statement isn’t original, but it is probably
true.
“The Dazed Australian”: I couldn’t tell
the exact manner in which _ the German
cruiser Blucher came to her timely end the
other day in the North Sea. It had been
cabled that she had been sunk by—
(X) The Lion’s gunfire.
(2) A broadside from the Tiger.
(3) A shell from the Princess Royal.
(41 A torpedo from the Arethusa.
(5) Pot shots from the British ships as they tore
past after the fleeing Hogman battleships.
I was certain there was a mistake some-
where, and the latest message bears me out.
It explains:—
The battlecruiser New Zealand rammed the
Blucher. crushed her decks, and demolished her
guns, while the Tiger’s 13$in. guns swept the fore-
turret, with its gun crews, over the side.
That, in addition to making the action clear,
shows the precision of the British gunlayer.
Here we have one battleship ramming the
enemy on (say) the port side while another
sweeps the alien decks without damaging her
consort. Nothing could be more inspiring to
us Australians who are just beginning to
understand the ABC of seamanship.

“The Scarlet Pimpernel” dontinues to
bloom bravely at Sydney Royal, and Julius
Knight and Irene Browne nightly astonish
their audience by singing Edgar Allan Poe’s
“El Dorado.” When they have finished the
ditty and resumed the play there is a general
sigh of relief. After getting one American-
made turn the onlookers half expect Julius
and Irene to fall into a clinch and give a
sprightly demonstration of the Kentucky
Cotton Hop to a delighted gathering of old
English rustics.
“High Jinks” transpires at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, on Saturday night next. It is all
about a magical perfume which affects the
characters in a manner, deplorable or other-
wise, according to whether you happen to
be a wowser or the reverse. There _ are
parts for Dorothy Brunton, Field Fisher
and the rest of the favorites.

place on or about February 9. Till then the

Wright and Dietrich add most to the other
part of the entertainment.

“At Cripple Creek” is suffering a second
or third revival at Melbourne Princess’s,
and there is life in the old joke yet.
It is not a self-confessed joke, of course.
Officially speaking, it is a drama which
fascinates and thrills you. All the same, it
is funny. The two villains who supply
the plot never get near to the consummation
of their evil purposes. They swagger around
a wild and lawless pub in the Rocky Moun-
tains, offering girls for sale by auction and
putting poison in the glass of Thirsty Ann,
the Female Firebrand, and saying “Curse-
you-I'll-be-even-with-you-yet” to the johnme
who defies them. But they never score a
point. A young wowser miner, aided by an
Injun in a crown of many feathers, and
abetted by a polite stranger from Ifoo Yark,
defeats the two villains all along. The
girls, without exception, are on the wowser’s
side. Even Thirsty Ann gets converted and
joins Joe’s morality class. Finally Joe’s
gold mine—a fine, spacious property, which
Seems eminently adapted for a beer store —
breaks out into a rash of nuggets, and the
two bad men are wiped out by Speckled
Rooster, the faithful Injun.
* * * *
A strain of gentle sadness runs through
the elocution of Conway, Beckett, Stanford
and some of the ladies engaged in this drama
of unbridled passions. One feels that they
would be better able to give a powerful per-
formance of a Sunday-school picnic. They
set you thinking of buns and milk rather
than of blood and thunder. But perhaps
they were tired on Saturday, after changing
the bill every seventh night for many weeks.
Besides, they are all about to leave the dear
old Princess’s stage, where they have so
long shrieked together. On Saturday another
co. comes to present “The Beggar Girl’s
Wedding” at the same popular prices.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s, Melbourne, the grand
annual frivolity continues as before. The
first matter for merriment is a miraculous
extraction of fishes from the Baron’s pond,
and the last scene of all is the
Gorgeous Wedding of Cinderella —at the
Prince’s Palace, or it may be the Fairy
Queen’s flat. “Cinderella” is a high-toned
story wherein all the characters are con-
nected with the aristocracy by birth,
marriage or estate. Even the he-dame, who
wears a fraudulent top to her head, and
patters about her first husband after the
manner of her kind, all he-dames being
widows haunted by recollections of their
dead victim and the things he used to say
in his courting days—even the he-dame with
the blushing nose becomes a Bar-o-ness in
the course of this harmless entertainment.

At Melb. Royal each week of “Bought
and Paid For” continues to add six
nights and a matinee to the score. Next
week the mat. will be laid on Thursday, in
order that all the mummers in Melbourne
may be present, whilst the spare seats are
filled by ordinary public “at the ordinary
prices.”

Ywoxy, another of the tribe of tramp vio-
linists, is the celebrity of the week at
Melbourne Bijou. Ywoxy is a musician;
even his annoying weakness for ragtime
fails to convince you otherwise. One
Doblodo reappears with a party of trained
sheep; and a charmer named De Yere in-
dulges in dashing eyeplay in one or two
ditties of endearing character. Finally,
“Hullo, St. Kilda” continues to give a dis-
tant Amurkan greeting to the seaside suburb
without mentioning it, and in general it is
no brighter than it used to be.

No novelties have come to pass at the
Tivoli (Melb.) or late, but recent reappear-
ances are numerous. The aerial Lesters,
who perform mostly with their feet upper-
most, are back for a term. Likewise Helen,
Leach and Wallin, two of whom are iron-
jawed ladies, the third being a wire-walking
youth of equally ornate appearance. Also
George Hall, the genial and elderly story-
teller from America. Odiva and Laura
Guerite are in the last-nights’ stage, and
the Follies’ revue is promised for the 13th.
