With Itoyal and Tivoli and King’s all
closed for the moment —the last-named is
just reopening with the ape and elephant
which have been showing in Melbourne—
other Adelaide shows have, had a special
chance. Waller’s “Strollers” have a big
public demand, and are beginning to book
dates in other capitals. The show centres
round Sydney James, ventriloquist and all-
round actor, but the orchestra, with Ivanoff,
its Russian fiddling conductor, is a strong
item. Branscombe’s “Dandies” make a fea-
ture of dancing, with Collins and Browne,
and Marguerite Leroy; and of concerted
scenes, with the clever Dorothy Gardner and
Cuthbert Rose. In pictures, the little Star
is putting up a film locally produced, show-
ing (more or lessl the escapades of Lindsay
Kemble when he lately pervaded Adelaide
streets in feminine attire for several weeks.
The suddenly-famous youth himself is now
doing a stage tour of the country towns.
“Bildad”: Some people in N.S.W. com-
plain that Private Enterprise is being over-
done by the present Government. It is
hardly possible for Selector Smith to sell a
bag of seed wheat to Selector Jones on the
other side of the three-rail fence without
having a lengthy interview with a Board, or
seeing one of the three enormously wealthy
firms to whom the whole wheat-buying busi-
ness of the State has been entrusted at a big
commission. This makes Smith angry, and
it also makes Jones infuriated. Smith says
it is useless, and Jones concurs.
They are making a small fuss in Mel-
bourne over the number of motor fatalities
in 1914. For the metropolitan area they
totalled 22, as against 17 in 1913 and 12 in
1912. Nobody is making any fuss at all
over the number of unknown dead babies
discovered. They totalled 18 last year, and
the number for many years has been a trifle
higher, and never less. These babies don’t
die by accident, nor do they commit suicide.
They die because their mothers don’t know
what else to do with them, and because a
community of Stigginses or Pecksniffs or
Heeps will not provide a State refuge for
them.
The painful history of Henry IX. —
The other day, at Ballarat, Henry Webb was invited
to be frank and explain why he continued to owe
Victoria a large sum of money for the maintenance
of his six “illegitimate” children. He didn’t
appear. It was, however, explained for him that
after Henry had been guilty of the sixth infant, he
had left the mother of the brood and had married
another woman, by whom he had two children.
Further, it was mentioned that, being consumptive,
he was now unable to work, and his regular wife
and the two authenticated children were on the verge
of starvation. The P.M. —well, what could the P.M. do?
He said, of course, that it was all very unsatisfac-
tory, but what was the use of sending the man to
gaol and thus merely adding to the burdens of the
State? That prize-fool institution had, as usual,
come in when the damage was done, instead of calling
Henry aside before he married the lady and asking
him to produce his bill of health. Certainly, if he
hadn’t married the lady he would probably have gone
ahead all the same, and a consumptive wreck can’t
earn tucker for an unmarried wife any better than
he can for a married missus. That being so, segrega-
tion seems the only remedy that the community is
likely to stand. f There is another, but its time hasn’t
come yet.
Tremendous is the dignity of the Court of
Law. A peanut vendor who dresses eccen-
trically was before the Manly (N.S.W.)
Court last week for some piffling offence,
and even before the wheels of Justice had
begun to revolve, “was admonished by the
Police Magistrate for his disrespect to the
Court in appearing in such clothes.” The
notion that Courts have to be deferred to by
the wearing of special garments comes from
England. A London King’s Bench Judge
has, in quite recent times, refused to hear
counsel because he whs' wearing check
trousers. It was the late Kekewich. Pie
said: “I can neither see you nor hear you,
Mr. Brown.” In Australia, G. B. Simpson
(N.S.W.) sternly followed the English prece-
dent. For years he would hear no one who
wore what he considered the wrong Court
clothes. Then Jack Want, K.C., made a
demonstration in a violent tweed fishing
costume, and beat him. But he held out
against the weaker vessels to the end.
The Historic Memorials Committee, an
Australian institution which is entrusted
with the job of selecting and purchasing
daubs of the great men who helped to make
the Commonwealth, has rejected Lambert’s
portrait of George Reid. George did very
little for the Commonwealth except to say
“Yes-No,” and he never made a good por-
trait. His insane pride in the bow-window
of his dinner always spoiled him from an
artistic standpoint. Still Parkes and Griffith
and Dudley and Denman and Albert Gould
and Hopetoun and Northcote, who had
nothing to do with the making of the Com-
monwealth, have had their portraits
accepted, so even the worst improbability of
George Reid might have been allowed to
pass.

AT POVERTY POINT.
‘‘Floater”: Pansy La Milo Montague,
assisted by the indefatigable Cruickshank, is
now making her contours public in America.
Described as “the famous poseuse from
Great Britain,” the Melbourne Pansy is
appearing in an act wherein her bare impres-
sions of ancient divinities are supplemented
by motion pictures of herself in the newest
London and Paris frocks. The films adver-
tise Pansy in her lately-invented role of Per-
fect Lady, in which she advises on matters of
dress and “form.” Lately she assailed the
Amurkan female’s habit of carrying shopping
bags, assuring her abashed interviewer that
the_ receptacles were never handled by the
Naicest People. “Indeed,” added the god-
dess, “the bag on the Continent is the badge
of the other sort of person.” Miss Mon-
tague affects a cane herself, leaving her maid
to follow behind with her purse and bag—
and presumably incur the suspicion of being
The Other Sort of Person.
"CINDERELLA," AT
HER MAJESTY'S
MELBOURNE.
Barry Lupine
Leslie Gaze singeth
a song.
Australian mummers stand to suffer in
two ways from the war. Not only has the
demand for theatrical entertainment slack-
ened, but managers, going in for retrench-
ment, can get a supply of 'unemployed from
Europe at reduced rates, if they want ’em.
One of the first mummers to make the best
of this bad situation was Harry Halley.
He set out as agent in Victoria for an illus-
trated History of the War.
“Ami” : I knew Dan Barry well —a quiet
individual, of strong common-sense, like
Sarah Bernhardt, Coppin, Barnum, and
others who have practised the reclame, by
way of advertisement, to keep in evidence.
I often wanted him to give me racy theatrical
experiences, but he despised all that, and
would only talk about politics, sociology or
something useful. He had a Sliakspearian
kind of contempt for his avocation. As a
paymaster he was good, though what you
might call not extravagant. Still, bad winds
or fair, Dan was faithful to his little knot
of people, such as Hollyford, Rede, Stone-
ham, Hoppy Keogh and daughter, Lila
Byford, Dora Mostyn and Rita Aslin, just
as Bland Holt was to Baker, Styan, Norman,
Charley Brown, Maxwell, Miss Ross and
Harrie Ireland, with the ever-delightful and
indispensable Mrs. Bland. Off stage Dan
kept much to himself, and liked best of all
to roam about with a vagrant sort of dog.

“E.T,”: Re the “Silver King” burlesque
mentioned as having been performed by the
Brough-Boucicault co. in Australia, I recall
that Robert Brough appeai-ed in the skit
when it was done in London first of all. He
was the faithful servitor Jaikes to the
Master Will of tubby Teddy Righton. That’s
a few years ago! The burlesque was played
at the old Strand as a first piece—or was it
after-piece?—to the stage version of
Anstey’s “Vice Versa,” with “Bill” Haw-
trey and Tom Cannam prominent in it.
Cannam may also have had a part in “Silver
Guilt” (as the parody was called), but as to
that I am old enough to disremember.
“T. 0.1.”: Apropos old-time comedian J.
,C. Lambert (B. 31/12/T4). He, like H.
R. Harwood, made his first Australian ap-
pearance at Geelong (Vic.). This was in
1855. He was G. V. Brooke’s oldest theatri-
cal friend. When “G.V.” made his very first
appearance at the Theatre Royal in Dublin
Lambert was playing juvenile business
there. He was a native of the decayed Eng-
lish seaport of Wells, and, after making his
final stage appearance in Melbourne as Sir
Peter Teazle, he returned to his birthplace
to end his days—mostly on the local bowl-
ing green.
“Cherub Aloft”: The names of some scene-
painters who work chiefly in Sydney may be
added to “Carmine’s” chiefly-Melbourne list
(B. 14/1/T5). In recent years Leslie Board
has been responsible for some attractive
things. The mill scene in “The Girl on the
Film” is his latest and among his best. The
Clints and Dixon are still at work. So is
Harry Whaite, after a spell among the lakes
of Killarney, the mountains of Wales, and
other old-world landscapes. Waite and Dixon
are represented in “Seven Little Australians”
and “Dorothy o’ the Hall,” and the Clints at
the Adelphi. J. J. Ricketts, for many years
with Holloway, and afterwards with Ander-
son, is best known nowadays on the business
side, but has not altogether dropped the
brush. George Upward deserves to be re-
membered for some of the scenes in “Joseph
and His Brethren.” I think “Carmine” is
too lukewarm about J. S. Mann. It must be
remembered that Sydney Adelphi, where
Mann keeps his paint-pots, is usually a
house of quick changes. This allowed for,
he maintains a very good average. A painter
who can put forward scenery like that in
“Mr. Wu” and the wood scene of the “Babes”
has nothing to worry about. Mann is especi-
ally good with Chinese stuff. Among other
things in this line he painted last year’s
“Aladdin” for Willoughby, and provided
good stuff for a poor play in “Queen of the
White Slaves.” Not all scene-painters, by
the way, like to be called “scenic artists.”
George Gordon objected to it as affectation.
Does anyone remember Howard Russell,
the heavy villain of a thousand blood-and-
thunder dramas in his younger days, and a
respected “Drury Lane actor” since he grew
old and shaky? He died recently, aged 81,
and a good story was told of him. Charles
Reade, the author-manager, was giving out
the parts for a new play, and each member
of the co., bar Howard Russell, weighed his
“lines” as he got them in his hands, mum-
mers being disposed to estimate the value
of their parts by the number of words they
run to. Reade, angered by his co.’s contempt-
uous handling of light parts (like waiters
dumbly deriding a paltry tip), called Russell
back to ask him why he had simply put his
little portion in his pocket without trying
to guess its weight. The courtier bowed
gracefully and said, “Sir, we do not weigh
bank notes!” Which, coming from a minor
actor to the man who had written the stuff,
was an inspired reply.
“Harrison O.”: For refreshing candor I
don’t know where you could find anything
to beat the turn of Skipper Francis, at
Melbourne Bijou. S. Francis once swam
across the Bristol Channel, which is wider
at some places than at others ; and he delivers
a stirring oration descriptive of the heroic
performance. The lecturer is visibly affected
by his own eloquence when speaking of the
dangers and difficulties he overcame, and one
feels that it is only by the exercise of strong
self-control that he refrains from giving him-
self three hearty cheers. Pictures are shown
of S. Francis sparring, wrestling, saving
goals at the rate of many a minute in a
football match, running, cycling, and in the
bosom of his family. In addition to being a
great athlete and a talented father, Mr.
Francis is a gifted poet and vocalist, and
before, leaving the stage he shows our people
wherein lies thejr duty to the Empire, in a
song which, it is gratifying to record, was
written and composed entirely by himself.
Ella Airlie, who graduated at an open-air
theatre, is now with the Fuller-Brennan firm
at Melbourne Bijou. Ella sings to her own
piano accompaniment, and her enunciation
is exceptionally good. When she appeared
in a St. Kilda show, under the Taits’ man-
agement, some two or three summers back,
the bright little woman caught the seaside
audiences by the ear. The crowd at the
Fuller-Brennan house seems equally pleased
with her incisive style of singing story-songs.
“Monkbarns”: Hamlets in Australia since
the Year One: —Adams, Anderson, Arabin,
Bandmann, Barrett. Batten (Melbourne
Garrick), Bentley, Booth (Sydney, about
1861), Brooke, Byers, Buchanan, Louisa
Cleveland, Creswick, Dampier. Defries (the
Sydney Gasman), Dewhurst, Duggan (ama-
teur), Rose Edouin, Rose Evans, Fairclough,
Heir, Holt, Hoskins, Howe, Ireland, Irving,
Kean, King, Maddocks (Melbourne), Miln,
Montgomery, Nesbitt, Louise Pomeroy,
Shearcroft, Stark, Sullivan, Talbot, Waller
(of yore), Warner (two). I have not a
record of Catlicart, Dillon, Knight or Rig-
nold acting Hamlet in Australia. Frank
Cates acted it as an amateur in the little Soho
Theatre, London, with Beerbohm Tree in
a small part, and I think Flemming was in
it. At the Soho you bought your part.
Hamlet might cost you £5, Ghost £3, Laertes
£2, Polonius £l, and so on, down to half a
crown. Possibly you might walk on for a
bob. Each found his own costume. Coppin,
when about 20 years old, acted Osric to
young Brooke’s Hamlet in an English pro-
vincial town. This gave him the germ of
his idea to engage Brooke for Australia 15
years later. Brooke, growing too heavy,
seldom played Hamlet in Australia. It was
a favorite part with Clarence Holt. Bland’s
dad ; but he hardly ever enjoyed a shot at it.
David Bispham, the ex-Wagnerian singer
who visited Australia a while ago, is devot-
ing his old age to the management of a film
factory in the U.S. His declared intention
is to render in photography some 20 or 30
operas of which he has secured the bio-
graphic rights. After that he will get on
to some Adelphi dramas.
“Masetto”: Giacomo Sgambati, who
passed out last month in Rome, aged 71, was
about the best-known of modern composers
for the piano. In his boyhood he was a
superb executant, and challenged comparison
with Liszt when that master was in Italy.
Like Verdi and others of the Italian school,
he was afterwards swayed by Wagner’s
influence. He turned out a lot of works,
including a requiem on the death of King
Humbert. But his chamber music is of
the best, though Australia being moribund
to that branch of thart, this allegation, for
a while anyhow, will have to go for what it is
worth.
“C. Ockney”: Beerbohm Tree is doubling
the parts of Micawber and Dan Peggotty in
the new version of “David Copperfield,”
which he produced at his Haymarket play-
house during the Christmas season. It has
been done before, but, never satisfactorily.
If the actor is a “good double” —that is to
say, if he differentiates the two parts suc-
cessfully in both mumming and make-up—
the extreme cleverness of the proceeding is
lost to most of the kind folks in front, who
rarely study programmes. They simply
think that the two characters are played by
two different people, as is usual. If, on
the other hand, the doubling is not cleverly
accomplished, the audience spots that it is the
same man, and reckons that the management
is hard up. The present “Copperfield”
adaptation is by Louis Napoleon Parker,
adaptor also of “Joseph and His Brethren.”
A well-remembered version in Australia was
that produced bv George Fawcett Rowe, in
which that excellent comedian was content
to appear as Micawber.
From London : There is a grand Christmas
revival of “Henry V.” —George Rignold’s
finest role—at London Shaftesbury, which
is run by Robert Courtneidge, an old friend
of Australia’s. In the caste is Rose Edouin,
the Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis who managed the
Melbourne Bijou in the olden days. She
plays Mrs. Quickly, and scores. She must
be the grand old woman of the contem-
porary stage, for, as a small girl, she was in
the company of Samuel Phelps during his
famous Shakspearian revivals at Sadler’s
Wells way back in the forties of last cen-
tury. In the ’fifties she was touring Aus-
tralia as a member of the Edouin Family, of
which brother Willie became the most
famous.
“T. 0.1.”: Melbourne’s first panto, was
produced at the old Queen’s in Queen-street,
on May 13, 1850, and was for the benefit of
Charles Young. The “book,” entirely new
and local, was written by Young expressly
for this one occasion. It was entitled “The
Goblin of the Gold Coast; or Harlequina
and the Melbournites in California.” It had
not much literary merit, but it crowded the
house.
“ J.F.H.” : A London Judge has been in-
vestigating a quarrel between Yesta Vic-
toria and Moss Empires Ltd., the wealthiest
music-hall circuit in Britain. Yesta failed
to turn up at a rehearsal, and her contract
was cancelled. The jury thought this too
autocratic, and in awarding her £3250 dam-
ages took into account the fact that she had
been drawing as much as £l4O a week—for
warbling “Daddy Wouldn’t Buy Me a Bow-
Wow.” “See what Percy’s Picked up in the
Park” and other classical ditties. The
salaries of several top-liners were sworn to
during the hearing. Harry Lauder’s London
representative, giving evidence, said the
comedian’s salary in England was £OOO a
week and in America £lOOO. It was testi-
fied that he was never known to regleet a re-
hearsal, although he had complained of its
length while the taxi outside was registering
twopences against him.
The astounding salaries paid to variety
entertainers, as revealed in evidence at the
Yesta Victoria trial, recall an article in
Blackwood’s describing the London music-
hall artists of 70 years ago :
The singers are usually a prima donna or senti-
mental leader, a basso, a primo buffo, and a few
nondescripts. Six shillings is the usual nightly re-
muneration for instrumental and four shillings for
vocal performers, with a rehearsal allowance of gin
and water. Pricer of admission vary from twopence
to one shilling.
The halls were generally apartments attached
to pubs.
“Masetto”: Jean Baptiste Faure, the
great French baritone, just dead at 84, did
not long survive his famous pupil Plancon.
Faure was the originator of most of the big
baritone parts in opera written during his
singing life, notably Neluska in Meyerbeer’s
“L’Africaine,” and the name part in
Thomas’s “Hamlet.” He was the first
baritone wlio made a success of Mepliisto in
Gounod’s “Faust.” The part was written
for and was generally sung by a deep basso,
but Gounod re-wrote some of it for Faure,
and that arrangement is usually followed,
good bassos being scarce. In Australia,
Yerdi was about the best of the baritone
Mepliistos, with Julius Riinger and Rupp
close up.
One of the craft in defence: —
According to a cable, London Morning Post
describes the Australian Historic Memorials Commit-
tee’s rejection of George Lambert’s portrait of
George Reid as “grotesque treatment of an artist.’’
The picture was the most prominent feature of the
Exhibition of the Society of Modern Portrait Paint-
ers in London last year, so the critics of the great
city know all about it. Remarkable portraits have
always appeared ridiculous to the contemporary
come-by-chance. The painter who is of value i?
ages ahead of his time; but any stray is allowed
to pass judgment on his work, and to say things
about it which would be libellous if applied to a
grocer’s porridge. Confronted by a John, an Orpen,
or a Connard, this committee would become trans-
fixed with disgust. Yet those who are best qualified
to speak say that these men are great artists. Thirty
years ago the equivalents of the Historic,- Memorials
Committee said that Whistler was an “impudent cox-
comb” who was “throwing a pot of paint in the
public’s face.” Now they say that Whistler is a
great artist. They say it, not because he is great,
but because he is dead. The important artist always
comes in for “grotesque treatment” from their sort.
Rodin, El Greco, Ribera, Degas, Manet, Millet and
a legion of others, of all peoples and periods, are the
proof of it.
There was what sounded like 15 simul-
taneous dog fights at the Sydney wool sales
the other day when the selling brokers
were deaf to the bids of German
buyers operating, as they said, on United
States account. The overlooked buying
brokers howled and barked, and mentioned
the Law, but the selling brokers remained
deaf. So far as the Law is concerned, the
Germans were quite fight. For any
purpose of civil proceedings, a German resi-
dent here with the license of the Crown is
not an alien enemy; though an Australian
resident in Germany is. At the same time
the Law looks with a suspicious eye on the
transactions of Austral- or Anglo-German
firms, and has appointed a Controller to
watch things.
While Maoriland has been howling Im-
perial patriotism with its mouth, it has been
using its hand to sign a contract with the
United States to supply 10 new locos. Britain,
of course, wanted the job—it never wanted
such jobs worse—but it quoted a price a
little over the Yankee figure. So Maoriland
gives the work and the waves to the Yankee,
and the Britisher can pull his belt in another
hole. _ Of course it makes an excuse —
Britain wouldn’t undertake to supply the
goods in less than seven months, whereas the
Yankee says he will do it in 60 days.
Heaven only knows how that improves the
position. To this paper it simply sounds
like a confession of utter shiftlessness. What
sort of management is it that doesn’t know
more than two months in advance that it
will want engines?
THE ADVANCE OF THE 3147 ARMY CORPS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Note. —My latest war-poem may be recited at
Patriotic concerts without fee. — C.P.
Hundreds of leagues,
Hundreds of leagues,
Hundreds of leagues onwards!
On, through mud, blood, snow, ice, dust, slush,
drains, trenches, morasses, ploughed fields, fever,
scattered brains, and putrefaction!
On towards Berlin, Vienna, Calais, Petrograd, Bel-
grade, Paris, London, Cairo and Constantinople
the 3147 Army Corps are plugging!
Cannon to right of them, cannon to left of them, also
in front of them, behind them, and occasionally
above them, including 75 m.m Creusot guns;
Krupp Field Artillery; giant siege guns;
howitzers; quick-firers; Maxims; 4.7 s fired from
monitors; and incidentals in the way of bombs;
hand-grenades and mines.
On through the Valley of Death, which includes most
of Continental Europe and parts of Asia and
Africa.
On through the mouth of Hell, which is an alter-
native phrase for above-mentioned regions—
On they are thundering.
With all the world wondering
At the spectacle of British, Germans, Serbs, French,
Russians, Austrians, Hungarians, Cossacks, Sikhs,
Pathans, Ghurkhas, S’Africans, Moroccans, Abys-
sinian negroes, Australians, Maoris, South Sea
Islanders, Belgians, Turks, Arabs and many
others, shooting, stabbing, bayoneting, club-
bing, disembowelling and blowing one another
to pieces for God and the Right.
When shall the Glory fade from those who are re-
sponsible for the oceans of blood, the moun-
tains of corpses, the agonies and ravishings and
ruined homes, for orphaned children and dyna-
mited cathedrals and starving old women and
men, and for all the rest of the infamies of the
past six months!
When shall
N.S.W.

The 1.W.W., which once threatened to
become influential by dragging in dissatisfied
trades-unionists and recalcitrant believers in
strike-at-any-price, has been digging its grave
since the war started. At first it used to
get a safe distance from volunteers and shout
“Murderers!” but it has since grown bold,
and riots have threatened to chew the ears
off several of its street meetings. A recent per-

formance was at Port Pirie (S.A.), where one
of the “revolutionary unionists” mounted a
box and greeted the ore-smelters and refinery
hands with “Now then, you Port Pine
hooligans !” That was the end of the speech.
The crowd closed in, and the I.W.W. was
hammered through the streets by an in-
furiated crowd of about a thousand. Iwo
hours later the supply of Workers gave out
—they had crept into the police cells for
safety—and the crowd assembled and
declared the rout of the enemy hy cheering
Australia, Andy Fisher, the Empire the
defence forces and itself. Then it went home
quietly and told its wife that it had put in a
good night shift.

Heine ran the measure over the Prussian
character, with this result: —
I could not trust this Prussia, this tall, pietistic
hero in gaiters, this braggart with the capacious
maw, carrying a' -corporal’s staff which he first dips
in holy water before bringing it down on your head.
I had great misgivings about this philosophic,
Christian, military despotism, this medley of beer,
deceit and sand. Repulsive, deeply repulsive to me
was ever this' Prussia, this pedantic, hypocritical,
sanctimonious Prussia —this Tartuffe among the
nations. >

“The Wflslierlady”: These able-bodied
women I see about the houses where I char
are not really dodging work —they are just
waiting for the right sort of work to turn up.

WANTED a Blonde Young Lady to arrange flowers
and read Charles Garvice’s novels.

Persian cat kept.

haired Protestant preferred.

young girl fond Of motoring.
MIDDLE-AGED Gentleman, musical, rich, seeks
services young lady play accompaniments. Not over
25. Clergyman’s references if . necessary.

ance companionable lady of good family, willing to
give hints on dress, etiquette, etc., in return tor
luxurious ■ home.

cert-goer, offers home to lady kindred tastes capable

YOUNG WIFE, English, lonely, would like make
friends Two Nice Girls willing admire baby and tell
love affairs in exchange for afternoon-tea.
WANTED Lady willing to go motoring afternoons
with small dog. Must be kind. .
LADY who has had 15 serious operations would
like to meet another sufferer willing to compare
notes. Afternoon tea, pocket money.
WIDOW with comfortable country home offers
dresses and pin money to Young Lady willing to go
for the mail and cut roses for table.,
As I perspire over my tub, I think, if people
would only advertise jobs like that, there
might be fewer idle females hanging round
me and asking me to take particular care of
their best blouse. Blow their best blouse!

The news that “Germany is running short
of chloroform” recalls that in the United
States’ civil war Abraham Lincoln, acting
on the principle that all things possible should
be done to make war. terrible for the enemy,
declared all anaesthetics contraband. As the
North was able to maintain a practically
effective blockade of the Southern ports, it
was awkward for the slave-holders. No one
took much notice at the time, but what an
uproar there would be if Britain adopted
that line in regard to the people who have
been making war terrible in Belgium and at
1 Scarborough !
“Gordon G.”: It’s a joyous ass, the Law,
anyhow. Try this helping from an Act
passed in 1770: —
All women of whatever age, rank, profession, or
degree, whether virgins, maids or widows, who shall
impose upon, seduce and betray into matrimony any
of his Majesty’s male subjects by virtue of scents,
paints, cosmetics, washes, artificial .teeth, false hair,
Spanish wool, iron stays, bolstered hips, or high-
heeled shoes, shall incur the penalty of the law
now in force against witchcraft and the like, mis-
demeanors ; and the marriage under such circum-
stances, upon conviction of the offending party, shall
be null and void, and— .-
Let it go at that.
* * * *
A youthful but enterprising pirate of 19
years was given an official reception at Judge
Docker’s Court, Sydney, a few days ago.
Inspired by the capture of Papua and other
Pacific islands, he decided to fit himself out
for adventure. Being short of cash he
forged orders on the Commonwealth Govern-
ment, and so procured a motor-launch in
Port Jackson and tuition in its management.
Next, he fitted out the craft with stores
and hove short preparatory to departure.
Somewhere about there a suspicious John
Waterhop arrived on the scene and returned
to town towing a dispirited Columbus. Judge
Docker gave the accused the benefit of (1)
the First Offenders’ Act and (2) a large
helping of sound but uninteresting advice.
The struggle between craft and industrial
unionism has definitely turned during - the
last few years into a series of victories for
the industrial brand. The Railway Workers
and General Laborers’ Association, started
originally as an organisation of N.S.W. nav-
vies employed on railway construction, has in
five years roped in 18,000 members, absorb-
ing sleeper-cutters and half a dozen other
crafts which had their own small associa-
tions. In the same way, amalgamation has
produced the formidable Australian Meat
Employees’ Union. The A.W.U. has ab-
sorbed'the timber-cutters, the sugar-workers,
the carriers and the rabbit-trappers, all, until
recently, isolated in separate unions. Now
there is a likelihood of these three big bodies,
together with the General Workers’ Union
of Westralia, and some others, forming a
combination to confront employers’ combina-
tions. If it comes off the membership will
be something like 100,000 and its punch will
thud down into history if it is ever landed in
earnest.
THE INTELLIGENT BATTLESHIP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
General von Mackensen christened the Dermmger.
After a rousing speech he said to the great waiting
vessel: “I commit thee, proud fabric of mens hands,
to thy element.” The band played the National
Anthem, the blocks were removed, and the ship
remained where she was. All efforts to move her
were without avail. —News item.
The General orated and the band played like the
deuce;
The people waved their beers and shouted Hocli!
The Derfflinger refused to budge: it didn’t see the use
Of moving from a comfortable dock
(A place supremely suited for a German ship to be)
And taking to the perils and discomforts of the sea.
The cultured German working-men got busy in the
ship.
They pushed and shoved it till they nearly burst.
In vain! They could not shift it half an inch along
the skip ;
It lay like some insensate triiffel-wurst,
Nor made the slightest answer to the men of weight
and worth
Who struggled to induce it to get off the safe old
earth.
The General was staggered. In his simple, forthright
view
A battleship was something that should float,
And not remain in dock or drain, with captain, guns
and crew.
The speech which follows issued from his throat:
“Go seek thy proper element, proud work of Wil-
helm’s hand” !
"I’m in it,” said the ship. “My proper element’s
the land.”
N.S.W.
10-2-4.
PLEASE DO NOT SPIT!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One of the Blucher’s superior officers when
rescued spat in the British officer’s face.
—-Further cabled evidence of German Culture.
To bombs and shells we don’t object r
Such modern missiles we expect.
That they are perfectly correct
We do not mind admitting.
But when from out the ocean wet
We rescue you, please don’t forget
We do not think it etiquette
When you respond by spitting.
We are a simple folk who try
To keep our city pavements dry;
Our trams and ferryboats that ply
Show of your art no traces ;
And thus we can’t retaliate —•
We have, we much regret to state,
Not learnt how to expectorate
In other people’s faces.
It is not from the coarse non-com.
That comes this cheerful clotted bomb:
’Tis gentlemen we get it from —•
. That worries us a little.
And yet we don’t your methods blame;
You merely play the German game
When at your rescuers you aim
Your cultured German .spittle.
Your guns are good, your aim is true ; ■
Cathedrals your bombardments rue,
You bomb a little child or two.
But we would not insult your
New methods of the missile wet:
It may be German etiquette;
You may, when you your venom jet,
Be spreading German Culture.
N.S..W.

The Law is an accidental sort of ass. A
German resident of Ballarat signed a Fed-
eral electoral card prior to the last elections
representing himself as a naturalised British
subject. Nobody made any remarks, and the
thing might have been forgotten but for Bill.
Bill made trouble in Europe, whereupon the
Ballarat man suddenly desired to become a
naturalised Australian. The Law sat down,
scratched its mane with its hind hoof and
ruminated. Then it mentioned another scrap
of paper to a very surprised foreigner and
told him that a fine of £1 for making a false
declaration would meet the case.
After the lower and the higher branch of
the Law, and the law-clerk and the office
boy, and the Iv.C. and his junior, and the
Bench and its associate and its tipstaff and
its declamatory door-keeper, and all the rest
had pretty well eaten up the mouldy build-
ing in Costs, it was decided last week, by
the Privy Council that the N.S.W. State
Government was perfectly within its rights
in annexing Federal Government House,
Sydney. It is a triumph of a sort, but no
one knows exactly what to do about it.
Money is too scarce just now to allow of the
establishment of a museum or conserva-
torium of music in the old premises. The
State Governor can’t be installed there, for
a new house has been acquired with much
borrowed money for his accommodation. The
Governor-General keeps away from Sydney
for lack of a house to stay in, and this aggra-
vates the peerage of Sydney, which wants
to wear its feet out at the festive guberna-
torial dance. His Principal Excellency waits

“W.D.”: Lights are
low at Birmingham,
the home of Kynoch’s,
where the high explo-
sives are made. Even
in the tramcars only
sufficient is permitted
to allow of the collec-
tion of fares. Much
the same in London,
where complaint is
made that Paris is
allowed to have all its
lights full on. The
discrepancy is ex-
plainable, of course.
Just after aeroplanes
“came in,” all flying
over British towns
was _ forbidden, and
especially over Lon-
don. Just as a ship
has to get its position
with a sextant or by
dead reckoning, so an
aeroplane has to get
its position in the air.
The trifling Aldershot
dirigibles, though of
little use for fighting,
were good enough for
making aerial maps of
London, and those
aboard were the only
ones in the world who
ever had the chance to
make them. The
German, who has for
years been flying at
will over Paris, has
long since got his air
maps of that city. He
could get his bearings
there at night whether
the lights were full
ou or half out. In London he couldn’t be
accurate even in the daytime.
* * * *
A special horror of the war is the Face at
the Window. The Window is generally that
of a railway carriage, and the multitudinous
Face is that of the soldier or officer who has
gone mad with the roar and suspense and
horror of it all. There are thousands and
thousands of these gibbering creatures. Some
of them will recover—it is estimated that a
promising lunatic may be patched up in
nine months or so; and a lot more won’t
recover. By the close of the war the
statistics of crazy folk will be incredible,
unless the war patients are simply put down
as retired soldiers and nothing is said about
mental failure. What a wonderful number
of things Bill of Berlin will have to reflect
on in his declining years—provided Bill is of
a reflective mood !
Melbourne’s annual morgue statistics show
that the motor hog was directly responsible
for 22 deaths in 1914, almost equalling the
23 corpses manufactured by all the suburban
railways combined. Deaths by motor are
increasing each year; during 1913 17 per-
sons were killed, and in the year previous
only 12. The number of inquests and post-
mortems last year was 701, which consti-
tutes a record. Suicides numbered 64, as
compared with 75 in 1913, which shows that
lots of people are postponing their lysol till
they see how the war goes—just as lots of
people every year postpone it till they’ve
had the result of the Cup. Bullet and
poison were still the most popular means of
despatch—l7 in each department. Others:
hanging, seven; drowning, five; miscellane-
ous, six. Miscellaneous includes the now
almost-discarded razor. This paper is glad
to see that its continued protests against
that messy and untidy method have at last
soaked in.
*•* * *
Brother Robinson and his Orange crowd
held a dreary demonstration in Sydney on
Anniversary Day. The bite had gone out of
the Walls of Derry, and the Boyne was a
back number. The British Empire is fight-
ing for its life against Prussia, which is
mostly Protestant and has a strongly Protes-
tant State Church; for Prussia is the head
and front of the offending. Its other enemies
are Roman Catholic or Mohammedan. Its
allies are Roman Catholic, Mohammedan,
Greek Church, Buddhist, Shinto and sun-
dries. It hopes that the Catholic States of
Italy and Portugal will join in, also the
Orthodox Greek State of Roumania. Bul-
garia, which runs a church of its own, will
be welcomed if it is willing to assist. The
mostly-Protestant United States keeps aloof.
So do Protestant Denmark, Holland, Nor-
way and Sweden. N.S.W., the most strongly
Roman Catholic State of the Commonwealth,
reckons that it is making the biggest effort
in the Commonwealth. The only insurrec-
tion which started in the British Empire
was among the Protestant Boers of South
Africa. The Pope flatly refused to give his
blessing to Catholic Austria in its war with
mostly-Protestant Britain. In fact, all the
religions have become mixed, and the annual
struggle to represent the Scarlet Woman as
the cause of all the world’s troubles lacked
its wonted enthusiasm.
The oration of “Harrison O..”':—
A cable mentions that the Cold Country Soldiers
and Sailors Families’ Association negatiVed a, pro-
posal that women should be helped upon the' produc-
tion of their marriage certificates,, hut not before a
reverend protest had been made against putting; con-
cubinage and marriage on the same footing. It is safe
to assume that the holy person’s protest was based on
moral grounds; and as morality, when not a ques-
tion of latitude and longitude, is mostly a matter
of taste and convenience, the protest, In this regard,
is worth little or nothing. At the same time there
are economic reasons why the unmarried wife should
not be placed on the same footing as the wedded
lady. The concubine is essentially a blackleg who
trusts to the generosity or otherwise of her employ-
ers, instead of demanding (marriage) union condi-
tions. The question arises: Is she any more entitled
to help than a non-unionist “free” laborer would be
to strike pay? .The concubine and the prostitute
between them have had much to do in making
marriage the comparatively ill-paid profession it
is for most women, and, in a general and economic
manner of speaking, should not receive the same
treatment as women who are in the union. It may
be granted that, having regard to the extraordinarily
upended conditions now prevailing, it is a fair thing
in many instances to disregard nice economic and
sociological considerations; neverthelesss the time is
ripe to protest, on strictly non-moral grounds, against
a growing tendency to treat the non-unionist lady
the same as the unionist wife.
President Wilson, of the United States,
will be remembered as the mildest-mannered
man who ever sat on a fence, and it is the
mildest possible milk-and-water fence that
he sits upon. Within the last few months
he has expressed his friendship for the Allies.
He has opposed any increase in Uncle Sam’s
military and naval forces lest the act should
look like hostility to Germany or to some-
one else. He has objected, but not violently,
to the conduct of the Allies in objecting to
United States trade with Germany. He has
congratulated the Kaiser on his birthday,
not because he approves of the Kaiser’s
methods but because he hates to be rude to
the maddest thing God has made. He has
urged the German ambassador at Washing-
ton to keep cool. He has taken a polite and
unbiased view of the action of his Govern-
ment in permitting the transfer of certain
German steamers to the U.S. register. And
he has hinted that his country will be glad
to mediate between the belligerents when
occasion offers —which suggests a Sunday-
school teacher standing with an olive branch
in one hand and a Thanksgiving Day turkey
in the other between a pair of maddened
elephants. The one occasion when Woodrow
Wilson, the most friendly person who ever
came with a beaming smile out of New
Jersey, did anything definite was when he
refused to recognise Huerta as President of
Mexico. Huerta was an Aztec ruffian with
spectacles, but he was a strong ruffian, and
but for United States’ opposition he might
have established a durable Government. Now
Gutierrez is President of Mexico one day,
and Carranza another, and Garza rules on
the third day, and Villa comes in next, and
all is chaos. The railways are mostly closed
and industry is practically dead. The
breakage is mainly owing to the fact that
Wilson objected to Huerta as a man
who was unfit to take round the saucer
in church and collect the peso in a righteous
spirit. The democratic President will be a
failure as a mediator. A humble and peace-
ful and unarmed person doesn’t count for
much in these days of vast collision.
Thus a man just in from Europe:—
If, as has been often said, this is to be a war of
attrition—meaning human attrition—it is commonly
held by people who only count the armies, and the
dead, and the wounded, and the prisoners, that the
combat has hardly commenced. The numbers in the
fighting lines are enormous, but the numbers that
have never got into the fighting line are far in excess.
On paper, the slaughter hasn’t really begun. The
great majority of able-bodied Frenchmen and Ger-
mans and Russians and Austrians haven’t fired a
gun. So if man could live on nothing save rain
water, and his clothes and arms and ammunition and
pay fell from Heaven, the struggle of attrition
wouldn’t be yet in the opening chapter. Unfor-
tunately the scheme of the complete nation in arms
is impracticable in a long war. Men must remain
behind to run trains and tram-cars and steamships ;
to make clothes, rifles, cartridges and cannon; to
lepair roads; to bury the dead; to help the infant
into the world ; to earn a little money in factories,
and to run banks and insurance offices, and to grow
food, and to dig in mines. Otherwise there would be
nobody to subscribe the £3011,000,000 loan when the
£300,000,000 loan is put on the market. The theory
of calling up the whole manhood of the community
is glorious as a subject of after-dinner oratory, but
unless there are at least two sturdy men at the rear
to feed and clothe and arm and finance each man at
the front, also to look after his family and belong-
ings, the man at the front is liable to become use-
less. If the whole male community went to the
front, and there was nobody left to subscribe the
war-loan save the old woman and the infant and the
cow, it would be a humble look-out for the soldier.
Despite the vast reserves of capable humanity which
still exist in theory, the war of attrition has com-
menced. Already the German banker is perishing at
his desk through the struggle to make cash out of
nothing, and many other exhausted soldiers of the
rear are finding the burden too heavy for them.

Dr. Westhaeffer, who has been investigat-
ing the diseases of the peoples of the South Seas,
ascribes the transmission of elephantiasis (which is
very prevalent in the Samoan group) to a mosquito
similar to the little devil that spreads yellow-fever.
White residents who remain continuously for long
periods in the islands are not exempt, and the investi-
gator reckons the only way to shake off an attack is
to hit out for a temperate climate before the swell-
ings become permanent. Yaws, a disease which pro-
duces a crop of running sores, highly contagious, is
also very common amongst the Samoans, who enter-
tain about it a similar superstition to that which
some white people hug about measles —that it is an
inevitable ailment, and should be suffered and done
with in childhood. To this end the disease is fre-
quently deliberately brought about by contact between
healthy and infected children.

“Mr. Ocky”: A stone has been dropped
on the grave of Billy Leigh in the Geelong cemetery.
Billy was the last of what was known as the Leigh
tribe of aboriginals, and he flickered out not long
ago at a good age, although nobody knows exactly
how much it amounted to. Billy, known and liked
by everybody in the Geelong district, was in the
service of the Armvtage family, who have put up the
monument. The words describe him as a friend of
the family for over 60 years. An epitaph by Harry
Armytage, ex-M.L.A., is engraved on the tomb. When
the then Governor unveiled the Flinders memorial
at the summit of the You Yangs, near Little River,
Billy Leigh was one of the principal guests, and
showed his Ex. how to make a speech.

This is how Bill wrote home about it: —•
Yorta seen one of them fly Sydney tram-conductors
taken down a treat on Christmas Eve. I was ridin’
in a car up William-street, an’ there was a bloke, a
tart an’ a young girl about 13 all got in together.
“Fares, please,” says the guard. “Two twos an’ a
one,” says the bloke, handin’ him a tray an’ two
coppers. “Another penny, please,” says the guard.
“That girl’s over 12.” The bloke just smiles an’ says
again, “Two twos an’ a one.” “Another penny for
that girl,” says his nibs, short. “Two twos an’ a
one,” sings the bloke, like a bloomin’ chorus. The
conductor clears off an’ brings an inspector. “You’ll
have to pay another penny for that young lady, sir,”
says he. “She’s over age.” “Two twos an’ a one,”
chants the bloke, still wearin’ his bright smile.
At last the inspector says: “Give him his two twos
an’ a one an’ take his name.” The boy passed over
his monniker like a toff, an’ took his two greens and
a pink. Next stop was top of William-street —end of
the penny section. The bloke hands the two greens
to the two tarts an’ says: “I get out here, conduc-
tor, but the ladies is travellin’ two sections. Take
care of ’em, please, an’ put ’em off at the right
place.”
“Lagani-N.”: For lime-making for build-
ing purposes, such South Sea Islanders as use lime
prefer the close, cauliflower-shaped madrefiora variety
of coral. This they dive for and break off from the
live reef-bed. Some of the knobs are six feet
through. They are first smashed into small pieces,
then a big pit is dug, a pile of firewood as high as
a man is raised therein, and the coral is spread a
foot deep on top. As the heap subsides and the
flames die down the heat is sometimes sealed in by
means of a light layer of earth over coarse matting.
Tbe lime which is eaten with betel-nut is almost
invariably burnt from shells.
“Calabash”: Re “The Oldest Inhabitant’s”
story of the shooting of Constable Nelson by bush-
ranger Dunn at Collector (N.S.W.) half a century
ago (B. 14/1/’l5). Some years back an aged and
dishonored man of that locality told it to me this
way. The bushrangers were at the pub—Hall and
Gilbert below in the bar, and Dunn on guard on
the balcony facing the town. Dunn saw Constable
Nelson approaching along the road, carbine in hand,
and when the officer was three chains away ordered
him to “bail up.” The order was not obeyed, and
Dunn shot his man. The part of the business that I
could never understand was why Nelson should prefer
to advance along an open road when he could have
made use of a rocky knoll, clothed with scrub and
leading down almost to the back door of the pub.
Nelson’s bravery is unquestioned, but his strategy
called for improvement.

“Outcast” : The Papuan custom of supply-
ing visitors with a lady companion (B. 1/10/’l4) is
still going strong from the Dutch boundary to the
Purari Delta, and for at least a couple of hundred
miles up the Fly River. At one time traders could
do absolutely no business unless they agreed to be
burdened with a lady companion. So far I have
heard of no bankruptcies.

“Paddy Melon”: Amongst the S. Aus-
tralian and West Queensland aboriginals, “pitchery,”
whether to chew or smoke, is a big-fella luxury. It
grows in good quantities out on the Mulligan, a sand-
bed in the south-west corner of Queensland, and the
Diamantina blacks make periodical trips to collect
it. It is chiefly used for chewing, but generally
after being chewed, rechewed and chewed again a
few times it is mixed with a bit of cured tobacco
and smoked. In times when it is scarce any old
buck who has run out will give you anything he
may possess (bar his dogs, and he never has much
more) for the smallest parcel of the herb. He will
even lend you his gin to wash your clothes, etc. Par-
ticularly etc. For years past—perhaps for hundreds of
years—Goyder’s Lagoon, on the Diamantina (S.A.),
has been a great rendezvous for a pitchery cor-
roboree. Every year large numbers of the S. Aus-
tralian blacks meet there to greet their Queensland
cousins, who always come loaded with the vegetable.
I have seen as many as 500 of all ages meet at the
lagoon, and for a partly civilised region 500 is a
big mob.
* * * *
“Lagani-N.”: The Santa Cruzians (and
I believe the remote Tahitians) preserve their
surplus bread-fruit in subterranean silos. When the
fruit is ready it is peeled, and, freed from seeds,
is piled in a heap to ferment. Meanwhile a hole
is dug, and lined with big green leaves of the tree,
and into this the fruit, mashed to a sticky con-
sistency, is put. Then more leaves are placed on
top, and the hole is filled in and sealed with clay.
When other supplies fail the silos are opened up,
and the mush is mixed with sago or grated cocoanut,
wrapped in leaves, baked in ashes and eaten. There
are about 50 varieties of breadfruit. Usually it is
about three times as big as a cricket ball, with the
skin marked with slightly elevated circles, like a
golf ball. The pulp is greyish-white, with about a
dozen walnut-sized seeds scattered through it. The
trees, which have serrated leaves, grow to a great
size, and yield tons of the useful vegetables. The
bark, beaten under water, is made into one of the
commonest tappa cloths.

(9/l/’l5), more in sorrow than in fury:—
PUBLIC NOTICE.
I, the undersigned, wish to inform the public and
general that I am not in Brisbane Gaol, and not
likely to get there, as some low culprit is spreading
lies around that I, on three different occasions, bea.t
and ill-treated my wife, and she ran away from her
home, and that I am in Brisbane Gaol. Also some
skunk is spreading lies that some man gave me a
terrible hiding down here. That is all false, as I
never had a wrong word with a man down here. My
wife never left her home ; she is still living at ,
with live children, and my two sons and myself are
down there taking care of our stock through the
drought. I am proud to inform you all that I never
in my life raised my hand to strike my wife, so all
fair and honest-minded people can see what cruel,
wicked, lying people we have still living in our
district.-—(Signed) .
* * * *
“Ysabel”: As the Maoriland weka, or
woodhen, is fast on the road to extinction, Parliament
is to be asked to protect it by special legislation.
The practically wingless bird has been dying in very
great numbers in the last few years. For some time
his destruction was put down to stoats and weasels,
but, while there is little doubt that these pests are a
factor, bushmen now state that they have dis-
covered the real cause of the trouble. There is, they
claim, an insect, something like a sheep tick, which
fastens itself into the bird just where the neck joins
the head. The bird’s attempts to scratch it off make
a wound, and from this the weka dies in a month or
two. Eighty per cent, of dead wekas found are said
to have this wound in the back of the neck. In 1911
hundreds died, and this year the trouble is recurring.
The insect is said to be larger and a bit darker in
color than the sheep tick, and his reputation is just
about as good. How “special legislation” is going to
give it to him in the neck I haven’t yet heard.

“Tbe Oldest Inhabitant’s” lament:—
Careless habit is changing the names of many
Australian historic places. Of late, Panbula, so
spelled for 70 years at least, has been altered to
Pambula. Milson’s Point was originally Milsom’s.
Now, I see, Cootamundra or Cootamundry is
“Coota” in railway-travellers’ language, and for
the first time I find it clipped so in metropolitan
print.
THE BEER OF VICTORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a summer afternoon,
Old liaspar’s drink was done,
In hope to fill his pewter soon
He blithered in the sun.
His lies, as very well he knew,
The beer of wandering tourists drew.
“It was the English,” Kaspar said,
“Who messed and marred our show ;
And, over graves of German dead,
AVith heavy blow on blow
Smashed in the bolted German door
And dragged the Kaiser round the floor.”
A British cotton prince arose,
His glance was proud and free,
A glance that said: “The whole world owes
A heavy debt to me.”
Three times old Kaspar’s mug he filled
AVith beer in Britain's honor spilled.
The sun was sinking in the west,
The cotton prince was gone,
And in his place of evening rest
The ancient blithered on;
For beer is beer, as all agree,
In spite of death and victory.
“It was the Frenchmen,” Kaspar said,
“That in our evil hour,
With beating drum and pelting lead,
O’erthrew the Prussian power.
Had fiery France withheld her blood
The name of England had been Mud.”
Uprose a wight who wore an inch
Of hair above his chin.
The ancient’s words appeared to clinch
The faith his soul within.
To France’s glory, high and clear,
He bought a heedless mass of beer.
The stars came out above the trees,
Dusk shadows filled the land ;
Old Kaspar lounged in pleasant ease,
His pewter in his hand.
The Frenchman gay with pride had gone,
And still the ancient blithered on.
“It was the Russians,” Kaspar said,
“AVhose countless armies smote
Our vaunted valor on the head,
And drove it like a goat.
For what had France and England done
Save for the battles Russia won?”
Old Kaspar looked among the crowd,
He searched it far and near ;
But saw no bearded Russian proud
To pledge his race in beer.
“Alas!” said he, “that thirst should be
The price of such a victory!”
NiS.W.

“The Reefer” : Here are a few of the things
made by the Papuans out of bamboo: Beheading
knives, arrows, pourri-pourri boxes, smoking pipes,
lime pots, Pandean pipes, drums, combs, water bottles
and hairpins. They boil water in bamboo ; and one
up-to-date village has laid a line of bamboo pipes to
drain a swamp. The white man also finds bamboo
handy. At Lakekamu the miners use it as a launder
for fluming water from a race over a gully. AVhen
a river happens to be running in the direction one is
travelling, look along the banks till you find a clump

“F.M.” : While tightening up the guy ropes
of my tent I noticed a curious thing. A little round
trapdoor opened in the ground and a spider emerged,
evidently with the intention of putting
an edge on his appetite by a walk before
breakfast. He was a middle-aged and
, highly-respcctable spider with a wife and
family, and I could see had been asked
to call at the butcher’s and bring home
something tasty for breakfast. He started
investigating a cigarette-butt I’d thrown
on the ground, and when he got his
coat-tails singed he swore horribly and
lost all interest in the pursuit of know-
ledge on the subject of tobacco. Just as
he turned the corner round a matchbox
he almost fell over a tender young fly.
Before she could recover from the shock
he swatted her over the head with his
club, wrapped the coipse up carefully
and started for home. When he’d retired
downstairs he shut the little round door ;
but that didn’t prevent me hearing him
tell the missus they’d have stewed fly
with ant sauce. When I opened the
trap with my penknife a few minutes
later, Mr. and Mrs. Spider and the Spiderettes were
just sitting down to breakfast; and after grace had
been said by little Jimmy you should have seen them
make that fly disappear.
“Huflin - Guffin”: My dairy-farmer’s
daughter is going to be married. She is, of course,
happy. She will have a holiday in Melbourne—the
very first she has ever had in her 20 years of dairy-
farm life. Hitherto, she has never travelled in a
train, though living within a few miles of a railway.
No time to go away—“ The milkin’,” you know. She
has never looked on the sea, though it is only 50
miles away—couldn’t leave the cows for so long a
journey. Now she will have a rest from the cows
fpr a whole week or 10 days. And, after that, there
will be different cows. They will be her husband’s.
So she is, naturally, very happy. But her joy is
tinged. Her elder sister, who was married a few
years ago, gave 16 years unremitting service to the
cows. She will have completed 12 years only. Given
a fairer chance she might have broken Nell’s record.
It could have been done, for instance, if she had
started milking earlier than eight years old. Thus is
life is interrupted bv mere weddings.

GO-BANG.
“Bill Bowyang” : The needle bush, which
grows on the sandhills and red giound in the Darr
and Diamantina (Q.) country, will, if properly used,
give enough water to save a man’s life. Natives of
the western plains know it well, and its virtues
entitle it to a whole publicity department. A mate
of mine, after his first investigation, refused to
believe this. He took a spade, dug down a foot and
struck the roots. These he cut, but no water came.
However, he took them home and showed them to
an old black-gin. She peeled the bark off them, put
one end of a root in her mouth, sucked it for some
time, and then shook the root in his face, sprinkling
him with water. A good way to get the water out
is to peel the longest root procurable (roots two or
three feet long may be got) ; put one end into a
billycan and the other end into the mouth, and blow.
It is surprising the quantity of water that will exude.
The liquid is clear, and has very little taste. The
plant has no leaves, but is covered all over with
needle-like thorns. A pear-shaped nut also grows on
it. The vegetable is very common —there is hardly
any red ground without it.

(God rest her soul!) brought him his dinner
av stewed shamrocks, so she did! Maybe
ye think he’d get up once in a while an’ go

divil a bit of it! Th’ dure av th’ Aboriginal-
ities hut (That’s th’ powerful fine word!)
was his home, an’ his family an’ th’ blessed

there an’ puffin’ slow and contintid-—(lf

only way I know —I will say no more about
it, and you will be distrest and discontintid
in your mind for ivir afterward, so you
will! And why should I care for your
thrrains, or your thrams! Is it me that
would give a mavourneen if you nivir
stopped again, but went hoppin’ an’ skhip-

never sat down to listen like a sociable man
to as good a sthory as man ivir heard!
Would ye have me tell you th’ sthory as if I
were a newspaper an’ not a man at all, at
all! D’ye not know what this ramblin’ and
circumlocution is to me? Pill up that glass
iv yours with begorra —ye’ll find it good, so
ye will —an’ I’ll raysume my cogitations
about this larrge onaisy lump of a Terence).
“Well, as I was about to re-marrk, this
great onaisy lump iv a man was sittin’ there
one fine bright day, thinkin’ iv nothin’ an’
doin’ the same with great iligance, whin up
come a few bits iv b’ys in uniform with bay-
nits in their hands an’ a look of martial an’
onraisonable fury in their eyes. They were
directed by a small child wid a gurrl’s eye-
brow on th’ lip just foreninst his nose.
‘Halt!’ says th’ child, an’ th’ division
comes to a standstill an’ remains there as
still as angils on th’ Day iv Judgment. ‘Are
you,’ says the child, addhressin’ th’ brave
Terence, ‘are you th’ person who calls him-
self Terence Byrne?’
“ ‘Oi am!’ says Terence, shiftin’ his ili-
phant’s feet onaisyly.
“Th’ child dhraws a bit iv blue paper from
his pocket. ‘I have here,’ says he, ‘I have
here a warrant for your arrest!’
“ ‘What’s th’ cliarrge ?’ roars th’ great
onaisy lump, shiverin’ an’ shakin’ an’ so
spoilin’ th’ vocal effect.
“ ‘Obstructin’ Aboriginalities,’ reads th’
officer-child, as ca’m as you please. ‘Ob-
structin’ Aboriginalities, forgin’ th’ Irish
language an’ false pretences !’
“ ‘False pretences iv uihatf’ wails th’ dis-
consolit Terence.
“ ‘False pretences in pretendin’ to be
Irish !’ schnaps th’ child, an’ makes a sign to
his mates with th’ baynits. Then, before
you could repeat th’ names of all the kings
of Leinster, they storms Terence and stands
him on his head, an’ a snowstorm of sacret
rayports to the Kaiser falls out of his
clothes, and continues to fall till th’ escort
was ankle-deep in them. It was more like
arrestin’ a post-office than a man.
“Well, whin th’ storm was over an’ th’
sound iv rushin’ paper an’ plans iv forts an’
troop-shins had died down enough for a mild-
mannered man to make himself heard, th’
officer-child looks th’ pris’ner in th’ eye, an’
says he. ‘What have you got to say for your-
self?’ ‘This,’ says the pris’ner, mighty hum-
ble an’ lookin’ empty, as well he might.
‘That I am not raysponsible for my own
creation, nor for what I do, nor how I snake.
lam not an Irishman, an’ never was. I am
not a German spy, an’ never was. I just
made thim plans in me spare time, so that,
if any Irishman lost his temper with me
through me takin’ up space an’ misusin’ his
language, I could get away from him before
he killed me for a fraud. An’ now, sir,’ says
Terence, ‘will ye please let me go while you
go seek Misther Eddyson, th’ author of my
bein’? An’, sir, if you happen to be Irish,
sir, an’ my manner iv spakin’ is offinsive an’
contimptible to your ears, I would ask you
to rimimber that it is none of mv doin’, y’r
honor.' So, takin’ pity on th’ poor boy, they
‘wint away an’ lift him gatherin’ up his spy-
papers at th’ dure iv th’ bark hut where th’
Aboriginalities is kept, an’ if they saw
Mishter Eddyson, nothin’ came of it, for, as
ye see for y’rsilf (an’ God’s blessin’ on you
f’r givin’ a poor, tiresome man a patient
hearin’!) we are all workin’ overtime with-
out let or hindrance, an’ doin’ our best wid
a wild, sthrange, long-winded language that
is full of bad sphellin’ an’ long worrds with
little meanin’ to thim.’ ”
PATRICK MICHAEL O'KODAK.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
The vacant S. Aus. school inspectorship
has been given to B. S. Roach, who is just 50,
and seven years ago was released from teach-
ing to run the Children’s Hour. This is
not the sort of publication in which a mythi-
cal Aunt Maria encourages self-conscious
kids to advertise themselves and their pet
pussy. It is a guide to good literature of the
kind which Roach himself loves—and occa-
sionally lectures about to teachers—and is
claimed to be the best publication of its class
in Australia. Come to think of it, 8. Aus.
frequently makes that claim. Roach was
born 50 years ago at Penwortham, the South
Australian old English-looking village founded
by the ill-fated explorer Horrocks, who lies
in the churchyard there.
MINIS TER PEARCE , who has been
earning his salary of late.
• Drank Lethbridge, just passed out
in Maoriland, was not a brilliant political
strategist, he stopped a few Parliamentary
shindies in his time. One of the chief duties
he imposed upon himself was to hand a bag of
lollies round on the nights of angry debates,
and members got so soothed munching these
sweetmeats that they forgot to snarl at each
other. No one can tell how many riots
were thus averted by Lethbridge and his
modest bag of chocolate almonds. His main
business in life was farming, and his prin-
cipal virtue was gentleness.
T. R. Toby, a Tasmanian, knows more
about the Equator than' any member of the
Geographical Society. For 20 years or there-
abouts he has been chasing tin in Malaysia,
and for five years, on and off, lived at Ponti-
anak, in West Borneo, where the Equator
line is marked in pebbles down the main
street. Proceeding from his camp to the
club for dinner the Pontianak man would
probably walk parallel with the Equator, but
coming home at night he might wobble
casually from side to side of the earth’s
centre. During one voyage from a banquet
lie might, in fact, cross the line a dozen
times, or even oftener if he went back to
fetch his brolly.
THE USUAL QUERY.
The Kaiser sent his I personal congratulations to
Count Zeppelin on the success of the aerial raid on
Yarmouth.— Cable.
BILL: “ Congradulalions, Zepp.; and how many
babies vos you kill dot time?"
Colonel 11. B. Lassetter has been gazetted
a Brigade Commander in England. He is
Australian-born, a grandson of Launcelot
Iredale, who founded an ironmongery busi-
ness in Sydney in 1820, and was thus the
pilot fish of the present firm of Lassetter
and Co. The new Brigadier, who was edu-
cated mostly in England, joined the 38th
regiment (the Staffordshire) as second
lieutenant in 1880, and has since then done
more soldiering than storekeeping. His active
service consists of the Nile Expedition
(medal, clasp and bronze star) and the S.A.
campaign (medal and clasp and C. 8.). He
is 55 years of age, and in 1891 married a
daughter of the late .T. M. Antill, son of
that Major Antill who came to Australia
with Governor Macquarie.
Ritchie, S. Australia’s new Minister for
Works, doesn’t use a mere shovel
when he wants to heap adulation on
a Governor. Willunga had its rail-
way opened lately, and his Ex. Gal-
way waved the green flag to the first loco-
motive that ran the road officially. Ritchie
was so lost in admiration of the statesman-
like flag-flapping of Galway that for him only
superlatives met the case. “South Aus-
tralia,” said he, “had been fortunate in
regard to Governors; and if perfection was
ever to be found in man upon the earth, they
would have to look for it in one of the
Governors of the State.” The Bulletin
doesn’t know which particular Governor it
was that owned perfection ; but, as a rough
guess, was it Billy Robinson?
The new South Aus. bishopric,
to include all the northern and
western parts of the State, has
been offered to Gilbert White,
Bishop of Carpentaria for the past
15 years. The new see is Will-
oc-hra. that name 'being chosen
largely on the lamentable argu-
ment that, when you go a-begging
in England, something aboriginal
will fetch in more donations than,
for instance, “Flinders.” Bishop
White is a tall, thin, scholarly-look-
ing man of nearly 60, and a living
testimonial to the North Australian
climate. He came from Oxford to
Charters Towers for his health. 30
years ago, and has seldom been
south of Brisbane since. When
appointed to Carpentaria (which
includes the Northern Territory
and North Queensland) he made the over-
land journey to Darwin along
the telegraph route; and for years past he
has resided in Thursday Island, as a handy
place for getting about his scattered
dominions.
According to the family records, John
Bodey, father of George, a South Australian
M.P., is in his 105th year. He lives at
Camperdown (Vic.), and enjoys a rare glass
of whisky as much as lie did 80 years ago.
Surgeon-Major Hinton, of North Adelaide,
will be 102 in March next. Mrs. Foote, of
Walkerville, near Adelaide, is enjoying re-
markably good health at 101. And Henry
Rosewarne, a Cornishman living near Adel-
aide, hopes to become a centenarian in April.
In Sydney the other day George Reid was
committed for trial on a charge of assault-
ing one Barton and robbing him of a watch
and chain and money to the value of £l5.
According to evidence, Barton chased and
caught Reid, and the watch and chain and
half the money were found in Reid’s pockets.
Some slapdash historian of the future will
cite this as a dreadful instance of what pub-
lic life became shortly after Australia had
been ruined by Federation.
“Tomato” Jessep’ has been made Grand-
master of the Orange Kennels of N. S. Wales
vice Brother Robinson, ill. The Popes of this
institution are an undistinguished lot. Robin-
son was chiefly noted for his baldness; Jes-
sep’s liens on fame are his bull-like voice and
his calling-—that of banana merchant. He
sat for Waverley in the State House for
years, almost entirely on the Orange ticket.
Then Colonel Onslow, having successfully
opposed him as an Independent, briskly
transformed himself into a fervent party-
man. whereupon the Wadeites ceased to have
any use for their old Derry friend.
Gordon Inglis, the Sydney Grammar
School boy who wrote that admirable work,
“Sport and Pastime in Australia,” has got
off his stool in the Commonwealth High Com-
missioner’s office in London and gone to the
front. Fred W. Cutlack, the young Austra-
lian who came out on board the flagship
Australia, wrote up the voyage and returned
to George Reid’s office, has also gone to the
war as a commissioned officer.
At last the movement to erect a statue in
Adelaide to the memory of Capt. Sturt has
taken substantial shape. The Adelaide com-
mittee has deputed the new Agent-General
and others in London to negotiate with an
English sculptor for a bronze. The explorer
is to be represented standing in his corduroy
trousers, with belt, loose blue shirt, cabbage-
tree hat and high boots. On February 4,
1829 —86 years ago to-day—Sturt discovered
the Darling River.
Delfosse Badgery, who has been scouring
the clouds over Sydney and suburbs during
the past three weeks, is an Australian native
aged 24 years.. Some time ago lie migrated to
England, studied aviation, and, returning to
Australia, constructed the monoplane in which
he has been floating aloft. The driving
power in the ’plane is a British 40-45h.p.
Anzani engine. The Australian has added
equipment which enables a start to be made
without an army of assistants, and lifts the
machine off its wheels after a run of less
than 20ft. Badgery lias demonstrated his
fearlessness by altitude flights of 12,500 ft. at
Goulburn (N.S.W.), 4000 ft. over Mount
Wellington (Tas.), and in Victoria. He has
offered himself and machine to the Common-
wealth for war service.
David Moorhead, an old Victorian inky
wayfarer, passed out last week in his 91st
year. David, who left the distresshful coun-
try as a youth, mined the Melbourne Stock
Exchange in 1861 and outlived all the
“bulls” and “bears” of that period. For
some years he was mining rep. of the Argus
at Bendigo and elsewhere, then joined the
Bendigo Independent, but finally deserted
the press to become registrar of the
Bendigo School of Mines. Of late years he
had lived in retirement, but right up to the
end he was an omnivorous reader and a
respectable elocutionist. The latter short-
coming was inherited by one of his daughters,
the Titian-liaired girl who was known on
tlie stage as Maisie Maxwell before becoming
“Pomona” of the Melbourne Herald’s page
for women.
There seems to be a subtle connection
between culture and haggis. When Mel-
bourne Uni. decided to import a Professor
of Literature it brought R. S. Wallace from
Scotland. Now Wallace is leaving for the
ditches, and another Scot. Archie Strong, of
Melbourne Herald, has been chosen to act
for him, while Strong’s place as lecturer
in English is to be taken by another Cale-
donian. the Rev. F. Sinclaire, whose long
hair often causes him to be mistaken for a
poet, when in reality he is merely a Socialist.
A fourth Scot, the brilliant Walter Murdoch,
was for years lecturer in English literature
before the appointment of a professor was
decided on. and would have had the job had
he not been suspected by the Senate
of having become almost an Australian.
Perth University promptly grabbed him and
intends keeping him.
Australian Art is well represented where
rose-colored flame and green smoke of burst-
ing shells will soon be working new color-
effects. One night recently there met, in a
tent in Egypt, Jack Sommers, Jim Mac-
donald, G. F. Benson and one of the younger
McCubbins. Cartoonist Ted Colles was on
his way to join them, and a few others are
thinking hard about it.
THE PRELIMINARY CANTER.
Asked when the war would be over Lord Kit-
chener said he could not tell. “But,” he continued,
“I know it will begin in May.” —News item.
BILL: " Mine Gott -I mean Allah—pegin in
May! Vot does he link we've been doing
up to now—playing marbles?”
Father O’Reilly, whose name was in every-
body’s mouth during the last N. S. Wales
elections, is off to Ireland —it may be for
years and it may be for ever. During the
dozen years that he has been president of
St. Stanislaus, at Bathurst, he has supported
Labor with cheerful vigor. It is true he
opposed Holman last time, going even into
Holman’s electorate to do it; but that was
not because he loved Holman less but be-
cause he loved Labor more. lie is one of
the cheeriest and most persistent fighters
that Australia has known. He will fight
anyone about anything at any time, and he
never lets go and never stops smiling. He
has been appointed President of St. Vin-
cent’s College, Castlenock, Dublin. In, a
month or so Australia will hear sounds of
conflict from the neighborhood of the' Irish
Sea. It will not be the Germans. It will
mean simply that Father O’Reilly has begun
to settle down in his new job.

Died last week old man Donahay, who
had the distinction of being the original
grinder of lenses for specs, in Victoria. He
was not so very ancient —70 all told —but
his trade m “glasses” dated back to almost
the beginning of Melbourne’s demand for
them. Melbourne was quite populous before
it contained any weak-sighted citizens to
speak of. Its early fathers mostly
vigorous young chaps who were'seeking their
fortunes without the aid of specs.
THE SAILOR'S GIRL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Soldiers and Sailors’ Families’ Association has
negatived by 329 votes to 120 a proposal to give help
only upon the production of a marriage certificate.
The Rev. Mr. Wainwright protested against putting
concubinage and marriage upon the same level.
—Cable.
My Bill ’as gone away to fight— ; and now you’d turn
me off!
1 haven’t got no marriage lines—’e knew 'is bizness
best!
But Bill has gone to fight fer you, so pay up like a
toff,
And, wen it’s bitter cold an’ dark, it’s him will
guard your rest.
You wouldn’t like ter think ’is kids was singin’ out
for bread,
W’ile ’e was waitin’ out at sea to save th’ likes o'
you !
There ain’t no scrap o’ paper, but there’s youngsters
to be fed —•
Come, be a man the same as Bill, an’ see ’is
nippers through !
You don't mean to be hard on Bill—it’s just that
you carn’t see!
And then you don’t know Bill! No ! Bill an’ you
’d never mix !
1 f Bill could see it jus’ like you, o’ course ’e’d
marry me!
But, then, ’e's just a growed-up kid, an’ full of silly
tricks!
But, ’struth, there ain’t no ’arm in Bill! You orter
see ’ira w'en
’E gets ’is leave an’ comes ashore to ’ug us all once
more !. .. .
O Gawd, sir! Think o’ them Out There! Our
’usbands, sir! our -men!
They’re all we've got! They're all you’ve got! You
daresn’t shut that door!

This paper has much child-like faith, but not
enough to make it believe that Sweet Wil-
liam of Potsdam hasn’t really affected the
Q.N.’s earnings; yet the disclosed profits
have gone up still further, instead of climb-
ing down. What has probably happened
is that so much has been clipped off in the
past to provide for contingencies, that, the
business being normally better than it was
even six months ago, the management sees
no reason why the profits also should not
now be made to appear better. It simply
means, if this theory is correct, that rather
less than usual was clipped off the true
profits last half-year, and put aside for con-
tingencies. Certainly, the war shouldn’t
have seriously affected the Q.N. It no
doubt cut down its London exchange earn-
ings ; but there was no drought to intensify
the trouble, and the meat and sugar mar-
kets, in which Queensland is so much inter-
ested, were unusually firm.
Apart from the steady growth of the
profits, the most interesting thing about the
Q.N. is the progress made in wiping out the
debt which stands between shareholders and
dividends. When the boom broke in the
early ’nineties and the Q.N. had to throw
itself on the mercy of its creditors, things
looked so bad that one-fourth of the private
deposits were written off’ —to this extent, at
any rate, that they were only to be repaid
out of future profits; and until they were
repaid no dividend was to be declared. The
repayment has been a long process ; but you
can’t make a rule-of-tliree sum of it, because
in the earlier years the bank had to provide
for large payments to the Government, which
was also a creditor. Besides, it was at that
time specifically writing down its assets,
though of course the effect of paying credi-
tors out of profits has been to write down
assets all along. The shareholder can see
what progress has been made lately by this
table :—•
Current Repaid Balance

The “special” Is. in 1913 is not traceable in
the directors’ reports, and the total amount
then paid is consequently an estimate. The
directors at the time expected that another
“special” shilling would be available in
1916 —apparently from recoveries, or con-
tingency reserves not required, and other
odds and ends.
In four years 6s. has been paid and
enough accumulated to pay another 6d. At
that rate, as there is still 9s. 6d. to pay, it
would be about six years before the end
would come. But there is no telling what
“special” payments may be made out of
hidden reserves. Anyhow, evidently to
prepare the way, the bank, whose paid capi-
tal is now only £480,000, is taking power to
make it £2.000,000. The present authorised
capital is £1,000,000, in 200.000 shares of
£5. The issued shares are 160,000, and the
amount paid on them £3. The resolution
will thus permit of the issue of 240,000 new
shares. It is proposed to do it gradually,
giving shareholders the first call on the new
issues. It may be taken almost for granted
that there will be an issue before the first
dividend is paid. A 10 p.c. div. on the
present capital would call for only £24,000
a half-year; and on recent performances the
Q.N. can certainly do more than that.
The co.’s creditors are extraordinarily well
protected. Apart from the interminable
stock, which is fixed capital, the bank owes
£7,002,112. and it holds no less than
£4,128,837 liquid. With affairs more settled
it could with perfect safety give much
further assistance in the development of the
State with which it is so closely bound.
That is another way of saying that, even on
its present resources, it hasn’t reached the
limit of its earning power yet.

This bank had one bit of luck, at any
rate: Bill of Berlin didn’t upset the
pot till this institution was ready.
Until two or three years ago there were
plenty of people who had very gloomy ideas
about it. How gloomy indeed was the view
expressed on ’Change may be judged by the
fact that as late as 1911 shares £8 paid
were sold for as little as £4 4s. The fact
was the management had never taken either
the public or the shareholders into its confi-
dence. All that was known was that the
bank had made heavy losses which had not
been provided for by writing down capital,
and how things really stood nobody knew
who wasn’t behind the counter. Fortu-
nately things began to improve pretty
rapidly in 1911, and in a burst of candor
the chairman at last told shareholders in
January, 1914, that the position of the bank
was never stronger. So public confidence
has been gradually creeping back; and the
£8 shares which in 1911 sold at £4 4s. had
reached £7 ss. by the middle of 1914. With
confidence thus restored, the bank was appar-
ently not at all worried when in the first few
days of the war some patriots went so far
as to draw gold and hide it away in Safe
Deposits.
This next table shows how well the City
stands now, after six months’ war, compared
with its figures of 12 months ago:—
Liabilities.
