The hank thus holds actually more deposits
than it did 12 months ago; and, though cus-
tomers have also had a little more help, it
has more liquid assets in hand than it had at
the end of 1913. The year’s momevents in
all three lines —deposits, advances and liquid
assets —are quite satisfactory. The propor-
tions are also safe and reasonable. For every
£lOO which it owes to depositors and otho<’«
it has £34 in cash, etc., and for every £lOO
of debt not represented by liquid assets it
has £135 of other securities. There was a
time, doubtless, when many of these securi-
ties were over-valued. But a gradual pro
cess of exchange has been going on, old
securities being got rid of and the proceeds
put into new advances based on modern
values. The chances are therefore that the
present assets are little if at all over-valued,
provided that there hasn’t to be any forced
and hurried realisation. And there is no
reason whatever why there should be any-
thing like that.
Certainly the war will pinch us for a
while; but in the end Australia is much
more likely to gain commercially than to
lose, and some of its gains will come quickly,
too. Whether the City is quite so well
qualified t« scramble for those gains as some
other banks is another matter. For example :

It is quite true that of those two banks
which confine themselves to N. S. Wales the
City shows just about as big a proportionate
growth as the Commercial. But also con-
sider that while the City has been increas-
ing its advances bv less than half a million,
the Commercial has put over six millions on
to its little lot.

In the other States there have been good
patches here and there; but Victoria has
been all bad. For every 100 bushels of
wheat reaped in 1913 N. S. W r ales had for
the past harvest 41 bushels, S. Aus. 27
bushels, and Westralia 25 bushels. Vic-
toria had only 12. And, apart from the
London office, this bank has no branch out-
side that State. Add to the troubles over
wheat, the upset in the wool market and the
postponement of Australian sales, with the
consequent derangement of the exchange
business, and it isn’t marvellous that the
profits shrank. It is marvellous that they
shrank so little. The cheerful feature of
the case is that the drought wasn’t long
enough to destroy stock and thus affect the
productive power of the State. In fact,
after a year’s spell, the land should yield in
the coming year a bigger than average crop,
given only average conditions. Further,
while the chances thus favor an extraTgood
harvest, the State can look forward to the
. certainty of high prices.
For the present, however, many of the
bank’s customers must be nursed; and
because it adds a little to the assistance
which can be thus given, the bank has done
the proper thing in reducing the dividend.
A bank which regards it as more important
to maintain its lay-by than its dividend is
on the right track, and depositors and bor-
rowers alike are justified in putting a good
mark against this Bank of Victoria for its
present performance.
The balance-sheet figures compare with
those of 12 months ago like this: —
Dec., 1913. Dec., 1914.
Notes in circulation £9,611- £8,602 Dec. £1,009
Bills, etc. . . 461,229 351,359 Dec. 109,870
Deposits at interest 4,265,838 4,264,248 Dec. 1,590
Free deposits . . 2,214,407 2,210,432 Dec. 3,975
Liquid assets . . 2,400,528 2,136,898 Dec. 263,630
Advances . . 6,083,542 6,271,131 Inc. 187,589
Premises, etc. . . 354,482 347,720 Dec. 6,762
Naturally the figures are not better than
they were a year ago. But there are no
changes of any serious consequence. The
deposits, taking the season and all other
things into account, have been very well
maintained; and though there has been a
shrinkage in liquid assets, that was inevit-
able on account of the necessity for carrying
customers through a bad time. It is far
better to see a movement that way. In
fact, any Victorian bank which has of late
been obliged to call in advances instead of
giving further help, had been guilty of pre-
vious gross mismanagement. There is a
limit, of course, to the further assistance
which any bank can safely give. In this
case the margin is not now very wide ; but
the improvement of the wool market will
help, and the Government’s action in ad-
vancing seed wheat, etc., will also consider-
ably relieve this and other banks.

For nearly 30 years the business had been
carried on by a proprietary co., in £lO
shares, with the late F. R. Godfrey as chair-
man. In the later years there were pref.
and ordinary shares. In 1909 it was decided
to let the public in, and split the £lO shares
into tens. The ordinaries (£6000) at
that time had the co. by the wool ;
hut they agreed to rank equally with
prefs. as to dividend, provided they
got £l2OO more in shares,- the whole
£7200 then counting as deferred shares. The
capital has remained at £22,930 pref. and
£7200 deferred till last year, when the prefs.
were made up to £30,000.. This followed on
an extension of the factory part of the busi-
ness to Sydney. The underwriting of the
new shares (£353) was a charge against
last year’s profits. Still, giving the year the
benefit of that, it wasn’t as satisfactory as
the two preceding years, which between
them carried the cost of establishing the
business in Sydney—something over £lOOO.
War and drought, of course.
Balance-sheet items compare this way :
1910. 1914. 1
Premises ... .. £4,604 £11,184
Plant .. .. 10,244 14,193
Stock . . . . 6,833 11,166
Book debts . . . . 6,029 8,056
So in four years £16,889 has been put into
those items, plus £2899 written off against
depreciation: and there have been a few
trifles in addition. New capital has supplied
£7070; liabilities have grown from £4148 to
£BBl4 = £4666. Add the two = £11,736. In
four years the business has thus provided
(besides the few trifles before mentioned)
an 8 p.c. dividend, what looks like a proper
reserve for replacement of plant and furni-
ture, expenses of opening a Sydney business,
and £5153 towards general assets. The
Bulletin can’t, of course, tell whether the
Co.’s future can be gauged by its very satis-
factory past; but at any rate there seems
to be no deterioration in the management,
and there shouldn’t be any permanent
shrinkage in the demand for the co.’s wares.

Reserves transferred to capital, £63,000 808
June, 1913.-12,885 7 p.c. = 12,848 37 845
Dec., 1913.-13,652 7 „ ~13,876 621
Dec., 1914..28,229 64 „„ 26,596 1633 2254
For the first time this co. publishes a year’s
accounts in the form laid down by the N. S.
Wales Act of 1912. In the old days the
prosperous gas cos. of the State stuffed
profits aw r ay; the Act gave them the right
to capitalise these reserves, both hidden and
visible. Since June, 1912, this co.’s changes
in capital have consequently been great:—
Capital as at 30/6/’l2 . . . . £195,000
Reserves capitalised . . .. 145,687
New capital paid lip .. .. 92,691
Present capital . . . . £433,378
The actually paid capital is now £287,691,
as against the former £195,000, and a 7 p.c.
dividend to-day is worth to the old
shareholders as much as 12J p.c. would have
been in 1912. In other words, until this
half-year they have lately got 121 p.c. on
their old capital and 7 p.c. on their new.
Even a 6 p.c. div. on the watered capital is
equal to a shade under 101 p.c. on the old
unwatered capital. Wherefore no condo-
lences are due to the co. on the apparent
reduction of the dividend.
Of the present capital (£433,378)
£378,000 is in “A” shares, which are
entitled to 10 p.c. when the co. can pay it.
Until then the co. may charge more than
. the 3s. 6d. standard rate for gas. The Act
was, in fact, too liberal to the cos.: it per-
mitted them not only to capitalise their
reserves, but also to charge enough for gas
to allow of their paying 10 p.c. on their old
capital, including the transferred reserves.
The result is that the people are now actually
paying more for gas than they were before
the Act was passed to protect them, and the
shareholders are drawing a bigger dividend.
In the art of making a mess of things the
N.S.W. Government is unapproachable.
For the first half of the year the co. paid
at the rate of 7 p.c.; for the second half, at
the rate of 6 p.c. The profit for the year
gave the 61 p.c. dividend and only £1633
over. But the Act still allows something
nice and handy to he put aside. To a
“special purposes fund’’ 2 p.c. may be
diverted ; and that isn’t 2 p.c. of the share
capital only, but of the share and loan
capital combined. This co. has borrowed
and spent £123,993. Add that to the share
capital = £557,371. From that 2 p.c. is
written off for depreciation. The profit
shown is therefore reduced by £11,532. This
sum is in addition to amounts actually spent
on repairs and renewals. Before the Act
was passed we knew this and other cos.
were stuffing profit away. They are doing
it still. The only difference is that now we
know the extent of the stuffing.

The A.M.C., Ltd., formerly the Austral-
asian Motor Cab Co. (Sydney) :

That’s a cheerful-looking thing, isn’t it?
The original flotation was a London idea,
and proved that if the English Company
law is sound in theory it is rotten in prac-
tice. Nothing, however, is to be gained by
going far into that part of the business.
Having taken an outrageous first bite out
of the Australian shareholders, who alone
put up any money worth mentioning, Eng-
lish sharks proceeded to do some more feast-
ing. Towards the end of 1911, feeling that
they were getting weak from loss of blood,
the Australian shareholders engaged Albert
B orchard to prepare a real balance-sheet.
On his report the shareholders’ committee
found on December 13, 1911, that the sharks
had fed to the following effect: —
Capital .. .. £54,651
Preliminary exes., underwriters’
commissions and various rake-offs
and London exes. .. .. .. £20,396
Loss on trading .. .. .. 3,177
23,573
Capital intact . . . . . . £31,073
That was less depreciation and after only a
few months’ trading. The management was
then shifted to Australia ; but it took a long
time, and what happened in the period to
May, 1913, can be only more or less guessed
at in the foregoing table. Since then the
co. hasn’t been losing blood quite so fast;
hut it is nevertheless clear that either the
management has been bad or there is nothing
in the taxi business —for a co. that has to
depend on hired drivers, anyway. Which
of the two it is this paper isn’t prepared to
say, though prima facie it does look as
though a Board consisting of one solicitor,
one barrister and one commercial man might
be improved upon. The best feature of the
case at present is that the co. is not at all
in debt, so that what assets it holds—mostly
motor-cars, duplicate parts and engineering
plant—are the shareholders’ own property.
But that isn’t much consolation to them if
the assets don’t earn anything except direc-
tors’ fees, salaries, general exes, and the like.
Some of these items at present are simply
awe-inspiring.

ROYDS, LTD., Investors’ Advisers, Challis House, Sydney
tralia wants to keep every penny it has, and
provide employment for as many of its own
workmen as possible. The extent to which
the co. relies on this country may be judged
by the figures for the past year, when a par-
tial failure of crops in South Aus. and the
temporary stoppage of work at Broken Hill
converted a profit of (say) £3OOO into a
loss of £1093. Before that the co. had had
a run of unusual luck, owing partly to good
seasons, partly to the great prosperity of
Broken Hill. This shows what the co. has
done after paying its dividend: —
Dec.,’o7. June,T4. Dec.,’l4.
Assets apart from tugs
and plant .. .. £26,496 £30,345 £21,610
Liabilities .. . . 2,217 913 956
Surplus .. .. £24,279 £29,432 £20,654
During the half-year £6255 has been sent to
England to help pay for the tug that Aus-
tralia wasn’t allowed to build. This,
together with the loss for the half-year and
the payment of a dividend, has had a dam-
aging effect on the appearance of the figures
for the six months. As for the current six
months, shareholders must be prepared for
a repetition of last half-year’s experience.
In fact, if the Broken Hill mines get back
to their old position in a couple of years,
they will be doing very well. And until they
do, this co. is going to be' more or less of a
sufferer.
The 8.11.P.’s blast furnaces at Newcastle
will begin to create a market for iron ore;
hitherto it has only been sold in small quan-
tities as a flux. In other countries vast for-
tunes have been .made out of iron mines;
and high-grade friable ore, low in phos-
phorus, will be very valuable in time, even in
Australia.
The recent run of good developments at
Vegetable Creek (N.S.W.) should encourage
its small neighbor, Y Water Extended, to give
the show a fair trial. The co. holds 320
acres along the Graveyard line of lead. It
put down a shaft some two years ago to
130 ft., when the flow of water beat the small
plant it owned. Since then nothing has
been done, owing to the usual lack of funds.
The Graveyard is regarded by many as being
the main lead of the Emmaville district.
Good yields were obtained from its shallower
parts in the early days, but though several
old shafts were sunk to water-level, no ad-
equate provision was ever made to cope with
the flow of water. The geological features
and location of the area justify the expendi-
ture necessary to ascertain what is on the
bottom.
* * * *
After a long period of stagnation there is
a little activity in the Torrington (N.S.W.)
district. Plant is being erected at the
Butler’s tin mines, and preparations made
for a start. For various reasons, under-
stood to be apart from the prospects of the
mine itself, this low-grade show has been idle
for years. Hart’s, in the same locality, is also
doing a bit of work, and has some tributors
on average stone.
* * * *
The holders of the 60,000 shares of the
Southern Cross, Ardlethan (N.S.W.), have
no reason to feel gay. The 10-head battery
runs an odd shift occasionally whenever a
bit of outside ore can be obtained for a
crushing, and that’s the total. It has no
ore of its own available ; and the quantity
of ' custom ore available wouldn’t keep a
coffee mill going.
* *. * *
A certain amount of curiosity is permis-
sible regarding the possibilities of that pecu-
liar branch of mining in operation at Ballina
Beach, Richmond River (N.S.W.), to re-
cover tin, gold and rare minerals from the
beach sands. Success here should mean the
working of similar areas further south along
the coast. To the outsider difficulties appear
to be the quantity of black sand to be elimin-
ated, and the extreme fineness of the material
to be saved. The effect of storms, beneficial
or otherwise, is also a point to be considered.
Two more cases in which things haven’t
gone right since the public got in are re-
ported from the West. One is the Empress,
at Boogardie, which was going to pay divs.
almost from the jump ; the other is the Lady
Harriet, at Laverton. The Empress’s future
is menaced by an intrusive bar of diorite at
200 ft., and the Harriet has just announced
a poor return. Both shows were alleged
to have plenty of gold; and if results
approximate to those indicated by the pro-
moters’ assays cannot be shown, some inter-
ested person ought to ask why.
Sons of Gwalia (W.A.) treated 161.000
tons in 1914 for £250.500, or 2100 tons
and £3OOO less than in 1913. Costs totalled
£173,859, leaving- a surplus over working
exes, of £76,654. Two Is. divs. were paid
during the first half of the year, but for the
remaining half shareholders had to be con-
tent with 6d. per quarter. The reason for
the drop is that an intrusion of unfavorable
rock has cut off values in two lodes at the
south end of the 17tli and 18th levels, and
there is a danger of the same bodies in the
deeper-levels being similarly affected. '
Someone is trying to coax the Westralian
Government to assist prospectors to inves-
tigate Yampi Sound and other parts of the
distant Ivimberleys. All kinds of metals are
alleged to exist there, and. according to tales
told in Perth, a few tickles with a pick
are all that is needed to expose great wealth.
So much loss has followed the backing of
shows in this wilderness that in all proba-
bility the reply given by the State will be
“nothing doing.”
+ * * -X-
Murchison is elated over a new gold dis-
covery in hitherto untried ground near the
Pinnacles. One party dollied about 150 oz.
from a small reef, and another party has
since obtained a bit by the same means. The
find is a few miles from the group of shows
recently optioned by Hans Irvine for the
Black Range, or some kindred Melbourne
crowd.
Perth creditors’ application to wind up
Chaffers has been further adjourned. Mean-
time efforts are being made by the Board
to carry on in' the interests of both creditors
and shareholders. But, really, the share-
holders haven’t a hope.
From Weston’s (W.A.) : Edna May’s
third level has been extended 200 ft. and looks
as well as, if not better than, the No. 2. Ore
reserves have been materially augmented
during the past two months by the width of
stone exposed at No. 8 level. At Edna May
Central an improvement in the mill returns
is expected shortly. Meanwhile ore of good
width is coming in from the Edna May, but
of what extent and value only development
can determine. Edna May Deeps is pushing
down its main shaft and hopes to get a
development shortly.
Golden Ridge (W.A.) is sampling the
Gladsome, which adjoins the Sand Queen,
at Comet Yale. The owners’ price is
£60,000, but they are said to be willing to
take most of this out of gold won.
* * * *
Oroya Links’ surplus over working exes,
for last year was £261,126. Six months ago
the co. had £1720 bullion reserve, now it has
none. The Oroya and Brown Hill sections
being practically worked out, the future of
the co. depends chiefly on the Eclipse and
Croesus Prop, leases, which according to
official reports have some fairly good shoots
at depth. There isn’t, however, much for
shareholders to build on.
* * * *
South Kalgurli made £lB6O in December,
which was the highest monthly profit for
the year. The ore now milled averages 245. 4d.,
and the co. hopes to maintain this for
some time to come. Except in one or two
places the bottom of the property still looks
sick. It is the leavings of the past that is
sustaining life and enabling occasional divs.
to be paid.
A hot and strong dividend duties and tax
on profits bill, with a year’s retrospective
clause, is being pushed through the West-
ralian Assembly by the Scaddan Govern-
ment. It will meet trouble on the doormat
leading to the Council.
Chairman Baring gave shareholders the
latest' at the Kalgurli meeting: “Our ore has
not decreased in grade during the past year,
and manager Black considers it will not do
so for another 12 months. Beyond that he
will not venture an opinion. The time is
probably not far distant, however, when we
will have to be content with a lower grade
and a consequent reduction in profit....As
a result of the war increases have and will
be made in the cost of stores essential to our
working. . . .After payment of the 4s. div. on
January 6 and income tax we shall have in
cash remittances through bankers (not yet
matured) and investments some £50,000.”
The comparatively early death knell of the
mine is sounded in another paragraph of the
manager’s report: “With all the evidence
now before us it seems in the highest degree
improbable that any profitable ore bodies
will be found at greater depth than we have
now reached.”
To the end of December Ivanhoe (W.A.)
had coaxed £7,983,807 worth of gold out of
3,065,486 tons of ore. That makes the
average value 525. Id. Down to the end of
1902, when rather less than a third of the
value had been won, the average was 91s.
There was a steady slide downwards from
that point until towards the end of last
year the grade was kept pretty steadily
below 325., as against 375. lOd. in 1913 and
395. Od. in 1912.
New Langi Logan, Ararat, after six
months’ training, with costs covered by
monthly levies of 4d. from each of the 100,000
shares, has made a second effort, and six
fathoms for Sfoz. is the tally. Six fathoms
out of a gutter 800 ft. wide is not of course
more than a drop from the ocean. In
these days of mining dullness it will
need . continuous weekly returns of 30dwt.
dirt, with the puddlers at work on a
fair scale, to inflate the market balloon to
its original size. Even Cathcart Central’s
rich dirt doesn’t bring in the buyer and let
out the stale bulls. Toole will therefore
have to hustle up dirt for his puddlers, if he
wants to make a showing. But what of-the
Cathcart gutter"? That is the second string
to the windy harp, but the tune is no good
yet. Meanwhile it is comforting to hear
that wash, 3ft. up from the bottom of the
Langi gutter, carries a good sample of gold.
Maughan, Tom English or Jonah Ward
would have revelled in a luxury of that sort;
so it shouldn’t be such a long way to Tip-
perary for Bill Toole, unless the statement is
all press puffery.
The old Lord Nelson Co. (Vic.) has a
mortgage of £B5OO to represent the price at
which the new co. got the mine and plant
under the monthly instalment system. Cash
in hand is £2660, but this includes £5OO
received on account of the mortgage. From
this it looks as if the co. would have
£10,660 to disburse as a liquidation dividend.
If the new co. can find payable stone in the
sink of 200 ft. now being carried out the
£BOOO is safe. If not there will be all the
risks that give life zest when dealing with a
no-liability mining co. Anyway, five direc-
tors announce that they are eligible for the
job of worrying over what to do if the
monthly payment isn’t forthcoming on the
due dates.
* * * _ *
Close work is clone by the Carlisle, Ben-
digo, even though it has to crush at other
folk’s batteries. Mining from 1200 ft. up
it does everything, including development, for
14s. Sd. per ton. Consequently, out of
64dwt. stone a neat profit is netted and a
trifle left for a modest reserve. The co.
handles about 10,000 tons every six months,
and has been on and off the dividend list
until £67.000 has been paid out. Work is in
a belt of spurry country, where, at 1140 ft.,
stopes go out 30ft. in width. Here the co.
has 800 ft. to go to the boundary, so there is
plenty of stone to play with, but the man-
ager won’t testify in a whole-souled way that
it is all payable. Still that is the custom
of Bendigo.
-V. * * *
Not many mines put up a better record
than Birthday Tunnel, Berringa (Vic.) :
its 379,000 tons have averaged a shade under
4dwt., yet £68,000 of profit lias been distri-
buted. As the gold was worth £310,000,
the co. has received 16s. sd. per ton; divi-
dends represent a fraction under 3s. 7d. per
ton, and costs under 13s. This last includes
development. Nowadays when things are
tight mine costs are down to 11s. 84d., made
up of mining and filling, 3s. 11.6 d. per ton ;
development, 3s. 6.7 d. per ton; crushing,
2s. 1.2 d. per ton; hauling 2s. Id. Office
charges have to be added; but seeing
that in over a decade the Board hasn’t swal-
lowed more than £26SO, apart from the fort-
nightly petty-casli item usual with all Bal-
larat cos., very little went that way. The
secret of the success of the Tunnel, despite
all the heaves of the reef, lies in the size of
the formation, the presence of free gold and
the fact that manager Hansen is a past-
master in the saving of pence. Hansen, by
the way, has been disappointed in a recent
hunt after a sweetener south at No. 5,
although the indicator is helping him a bit.
Now he will push ahead to pick up a fresh
shoot in the big block of Kangaroo ground
tacked on to the Tunnel leases a few years
back. With such low costs, there is always
a sporting chance in Tunnels.
Mount I.yell is looking round for ways
to use the surplus power from its new
hydro-electric scheme, and is being tried
with an amalgamation of the Hercules,
Primrose and Tasmanian Copper, which
last, like the others, is a deadly mixture of
zinc, iron, lead, etc. Negotiations are still
in progress.
* * * *
Whether the Lyell Blocks shaft is to fall
into the maw of the Mount Lyell Co. won’t
be known quite as soon as some folk thought.
Now that the shaft is below 1300 ft., driving
north is going on at that point. It has taken
a bit of time to get the first Bft. driven on
the road to the shoot. The management
confesses that the country is hard. When
that is said in the Mount Lyell region it
means only a shade easier than conglo-
merate ; and as the co. has butted into that
jaspery stuff at 1200 ft., where it is driving
south , the hunt for the North Lyell'shoots
is likely to last longer than the sanguine
section bargained for. Of course the Lyell
crowd still sticks to it that the Blocks is
only opening up a shaft for its use in the
future. The faithful in the Blocks, how-
ever, don’t lose their confidence because they
are told that the shoots change their under-
lie back into the North Lyell at about
1000 ft. A color of bornite in either of the
Blocks levels would set them in a frenzy of
optimism and lead to a sharp flutter in
shares. That’s why the adverse conglo-
merate south hasn’t knocked the market
into pulp.
* * * *
Mount Elliott (Q.) is patriotic. Two of
its directors, R. H. Henning (London) and
Comte de Venancourt (Paris), are going, or
have gone, to the front. More than that, the
Board applied for £lOO,OOO of the British
war loan. Henning is an ex-Australian. A
brother of his helped to frame the Common-
wealth Constitution.
* * * *
“Ganmor”: The new concentrating plant
at Mount Morgan did not commence opera-
tions until early in 1914. The costs for the
six months ended November, 1913. refer,
therefore, only to the old plant. The cost
per ton of copper produced has varied like
this :
Six months ended November, 3 913 .. £B4 6 2
Six months ended November, 1914 . . 93 7 10
Increase in cost per ton of. . . . £9 1 8
Refining and realisation charges are calcu-
lated on the same basis in both periods, but
depreciation is not included in either. Mount
Morgan has scrapned its old plant and spent
over £266,000 in building “the most up-to-
date plant in Australia.” The net result
so far is an increase in costs of £9-odd per
ton of copper nroduced. Admittedly costs
at November, 1913, were outrageous. Those
for November, 1914, are absurd.
* * * *
General-manager Ward, of B. 11. Block
10, is going up to Papua for six months to
test the Misima gold shows held under
option. His real job will be to make sure
that tliey are not too low-grade to pay.
Clark’s, of Charters Towers, recently
listed on Southern exchanges, will provide
another peg to hang a little gamble on.
The property is a consolidation of old leases
on the Moonstone. Treasure and Lady Maria
reefs, all of which raised some good stone
in the early days. Crushings have been
reported at long intervals; the small tonnage
raised during development has returned from
£lO to £2l per ton. Under present condi-
tions it takes a few weeks to raise 100 tons
of stone; but when Clark’s workings are
connected with the Lady Maria shaft, the
stopes over the 469 ft. level should produce
more quickly. Nevertheless, unless the
returns continue unusually rich, there are
likely to be calls. There are 180,000 shares.
Last year 18 mines at Charters Towers
crushed 61,153 tons for £224,667. an aver-
age of £3 13s. sd. per ton. The Brilliant
Extended crushed 19,075 tons for £53,665,
Mills United 7152 tons for £30.115, Brilliant
Deeps 9188 tons for £31.523, New Brilliant
Freehold 8752 tons for £24,272. During the
last, quarter several small mines reported
good crushings; for instance, the Victory
crushed 591 tons for £5974. Papuan (on the
Moonstone) 456 tons for £5432, and Clark’s
128 tons for £2535. Clark’s has 180,000
shares and the Papuan 96,000. They are
both very patchy, but are quite likely to
enliven the local market.
* * * *
Last year the Waihi (M.L.) crushed
183:405 tons for £329,775, which brings the
total value of gold produced to £10,772,682.
Of this lot shareholders have received
£4,681,387. Dividends have recently been
at the rate of Is. every three months. As
with most mines owning a glorious past,
the market value is high in proportion to the
present rate of dividend.
* * * *
Burma Silver-lead Mines, which the Bake
View crowd probably wish they had never
heard of. has issued another more or less
gaudy report. This time the year's work is
alleged to have “shown exceedingly favor-
able results” ; and an optimistic opinion of
a technical committee, comprising Gilman
Brown Ivuehn, Dr. Heberlein and the
younger Hoover, is appended. These _ ex-
perts talk in half and three-quarter millions
regarding the tonnages exposed in the vari-
ous workings. Something laudatory is said
about the large size of the lodes, indications
of their permanency at depth, and the high
values in the bottom workings. But similar
tales have been told before, and those who
believed them and clung on to the stock
found themselves compelled to either find
more cash or make way for debenture-
holders and other preference people, who in
turn may have to stand back for others
later on.

Cognac Brandy.
TEN YEARS OLD.
MADAME JEANNE BROLA IN ARDUOUS
OPERATIC ROLES.
THE BEAUTIFUL-VOICED SOPRANO WRITES AN
EXPERT MEDICAL OPINION.
It has been the surprise and delight of music-lovers
and theatre-goers to witness the lavish display of
vocal ability and operatic talent so unquestionably
possessed by Madame Jeanne Brola, the distinguished
soprano of the recent Quinlan Opera Company.
Many of her singing roles were prominently opposed
to each other, yet she was as much at home in
“Madame Butterfly” as in “La Boheme,” and as
much so in “The Girl of the Golden West” as in
“Aida.” It appeared natural to her in that what-
ever she did she did well.
Madame Brola during the 1913 season deservedly
became intensely popular with Australin music-
lovers, but she freely admitted that so great and
exhausting had been her duties that she would un-
doubtedly have suffered FROM A GREAT MENTAL
AND PHYSICAL BREAKDOWN were it not for the
fact that she took Clements Tonic, the ideal medi-
cine for the artiste and professional. Her tribute to
this splendid strength-giving and health-preserving
medicine has been often published for the public
good, and should be read by all professional and
commercial persons who at times suffer from brain-
fag, anaemia, physical fatigue or nerve weakness.
Ladies of society who suffer from nervousness,
caused by exacting social duties, and who suffer
from brain-fag or insomnia in consequence, should
use this medicine because it induces restful, sooth-
ing and invigorating sleep.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, February 1, 1915.
My Dear Arini, —
Melba’s stay on the Johnsons’ station
(Frankfield), about three miles from Gun-
ning, finished up on January 31. The Voice
then caught the Melbourne express, which
had John Lemmone amongst its passengers,
and went off to Victoria to plunge into
more patriotic concerts. Flautist Lemmone,
whose musical ear is screwed into a hard
business head, looks after the details of the
Skylark’s efforts for Australian War Funds.
Concerning a statement which material-
ised in November that Melba had engaged
a suite of rooms at Manly, I have her autho-
rity to say there is no truth in it. She
hasn’t departed a hair-line from her inten-
tion to spend most of her time between
Coombe Cottage and concerts for the Cause.
But her plans may have to undergo revision
before long. She intended to leave for Eng-
land on March 17: but as the Unspeakable
will possibly be throwing his fez into the
Suez Canal about that time, our Chief
Soprano may have to either settle down or
take some other route back to Europe.
One member of the notable English family
which includes Commander Tyrwhitt—he
who has figured in two recent sea fights—
took up land in Queensland, with favorable
results. A daughter named Noel, who is
still enjoying the gleeful days of flapperhood
in Toowoomba, is an understudy of Uncle
Tyrwhitt so far as personal appearance
goes. Another relative, Walter, is a water-
color artist of some prominence. He had a
look at Bananaland some years ago, and
vented his impressions in a publication
called “The New Chum in the Queensland
Bush.”
The recent carnival on the shoulder of
Kosciusko brought forth some well-known
goffers. Nancy Parbury, the thin girl who
plays such a rattling good game, won the only
event she entered for,and got in three stunning
smacks that made the average golfing woman
sit up and take notice. Also J. A. Walsh,
a Brisbane man, made a sensational hit, by
driving a ball from the first tee a distance
of 255 yards. As this was an uphill smack,
the ball that rolled to its destination on the
first green made a record on the mountain
links. The fair and freckled Queenslander
helped to down the elusive argument that
the warm North is undermining the white
man’s physique. Bryan Holmes, the big
and burly Fiji sugar planter, who turns the
scale at about lost., played a game or two
on the same sward. Mr. and Mrs. G.
Andrews, also from Fiji, spent the holidays
on the mountain links.
Solicitor Rundle won the chief prize this
year at the carnival. This is the big silver
cup presented by president J. Baton, to be
competed for annually. Besides a lease of
the silverware, the winner received a per-
manent acknowledgment of liis might in a
pair of pipes. 11. Berkely, of the New-
castle Herald, won various events on the
mountain eyrie where the eagle builds its
nest. Tennis as an extra found Miss Kirk-
land and G. Andrews easy victors.
Thirroul, on the South Coast, is chiefly
remarkable for its surf and the variety of
surfers, plain and fancy, who patronise its
wave-banged shore in summer. As a side-
line it has an unusual taste in cottage
nomenclature, as thus: “Why Work.”
“Chirrup” and “You and Me.” There are
others, of course. Mrs. Maxwell and her
mother, Mrs. Walter Read, are putting in
the summer at one of the cots. The late
Walter Read was a well-known Rockhamp-
ton man who made a pile in business, and
unmade most of it by investing in station
property in Queensland.
Austinmere, an adjacent village, is shel-
tering numbers of up-country folks this
month, and a fine display of tanned calf
is said to promenade its sandy beaches o’
summer morns. Mrs. Charlie Badgery and
her daughters are in a roomy cottage which
overlooks the spray. Mrs. Charlie is auntie
to. Delfosse Badgery, the young N.S.W.
aviator who waltzed with the atmosphere
oyer Victoria Park the other afternoon in
his own home-made biplane. The squatting
Weedons are at the same address, and the
pretty wife of Dr. Martin, of Wagga, also
takes a dip in the surf thereabouts.
Mrs. Trefle, the widow of the late Minister
for Lands, is going to her brother’s home
in Tasmania for a change of scene. This
will be a few weeks hence, after she has
dried her eyes sufficiently to attend to the
business details that follow a funeral. When
you come to think of it, this habit of dying
is far too prevalent amongst our best people,
while our worst humans remain obstinately
tough and long-lived. Just this morning I
saw three well-known people who could
easily be soared.
Mrs. Leslie Wade, whose popular husband
passed out so suddenly last month, has been
hustled off to the country, She is a rather
frail, little lady, and her friends are trying
hard to repair the shock which she sustained
when the late Commissioner for Irrigation
flickered off the surface of things on the day
his friend J. L. Trefle was buried.
Mr. and Mrs. Jack Frith and family, of
Toowoomba, came back from England in
the Osterley. They had booked by the
Otway, but that much-engaged craft was
taken over by the Government for transport
work.
Cadet G. W. Muir, a nice Scotch boy who
went down on the Monmouth, was here on
the training-ship Mersey a while ago. So
was Middy Trounson, who was lost, with the
ill-fated Good Hope.
Anniversary Day Regatta, with the tem-
perature on the edge of 104 deg. in the saloon
of the flagship, was not wildly exciting till
the southerly burst up the harbor. Then
small sailing craft toppled over, and shriek-
ing canvas smacked the face of ruffled waters,
and rescuers hurried to submerged crews.
There were no casualties, for the Sydney
sailing man is an amphibious creature, and
as hard to drown as a crocodile. Governor
Strickland, whose steps were shadowed by
lie!]) d’Apice, came to town for the historic
event. The first great regatta was, of
course, not held in Sydney Harbor. It
was presided over by Noah, and, Judge
Backhouse not having been invented at that
time, there were no luncheon speeches.
The directors of the North Coast Steam-
ship Co. lent the Wollongbar, the usual deep-
sea mailboat being unavailable as flagship.
The managing director, genial Bob Bell, not
long back from Europe, took the head of the
flat timber substance on which the food
was laid out. He blushed profusely under a
healthy coat of tan at the exuberant helping
of melted butter the Minister for Education
poured over the North Coast Co. for handing
over the Wollongbar to the Regatta commit-
tee. His Ex. made a few brief remarks,
and then Judge Backhouse had his annual
opportunity to address the gathering as
chairman of the committee. The company,
greatly fortified by its refreshments, tore
itself away from the viands at 3 p.m., and
climbed upstairs in time to see the southerly
tumble the sailing man into the harbor.
Have you ever noticed that the" hotter the
day the warmer the lemonade the average
woman can' buy at such festivals as a re-
gatta? After hanging over the lounge rails
of the Wollongbar in the vain hope of catch-
ing pearls of wisdom from the speeches in
the saloon below, Woman panted along the
upper deck. Her object was to stalk some-
thing liquid with ice in the middle. But the
iced soft or hard drinks went to the male
visitor, and the inevitable warm gingerbeer
frothed over in the female hand. The con-
clusion that we really do belong to the
inferior sex is then gulped down in the silence
of despair.
William Pat Manning has resigned his
honorary billet as president of the Phil-
harmonic Society. In recognition of his
20-odd years’ connection with the Phil, he
was handed a large piece of plate in the form
of a loving-cup.
Actors’ Day, on February 6, at the White
City, threatens to break every previous
record. Ever since the war upset the only
universe we possess, the Australian mummer
has been putting in overtime at all manner
of patriotic shows. Yet no one is harder hit
than the footlight-ffivorite by Wilhelm’s mad
dash. The programme promises to extend
from Saturday till the middle of next week,
but the may cut it down under a new
code for the prevention of cruelty to scene-
shifters.
Rene Maxwell’s latest achievement is the
winning of the principal Scholarship of the
Guildhall School of Music. Landon Ronald
writes to Melba of his admiration for the
Sydney girl’s limpid soprano. Ronald, who
is chief of the Guildhall music centre, is
known here by his ballads, notably “Down
in the Forest.” This melodious lilt was prac-
tically introduced here by Melba. She is
about the only Australian soprano who can
give to the bird-like top note, at the end of the
sonsr, its true canary-tone. Rene, who went
to London last May, after a concert had put
£3OO in her purse, won the Royal Academy
Scholarship soon after her arrival in the Fog.
Now. as already hereinbefore mentioned, she
has snatched the coveted Guildhall prize in
open competition. This carries £SO per
annum for three years.
In moments of undue frivolity I have occa-
sionally told you, Arini. that pleasure ex-
cursions to the battlefields appeared to be
imminent. The incredible has happened.
Already personally-conducted tours are leav-
ing London and Bristol, and the tourist
packs up for the fringe of carnage as if he
were going to ’appy Margate for a week-end.
Some former South Australians, Mr. and
Mrs. G. 11. Atkins, are mentioned in a
“Joffre” (the irony of it!) touring party to
“Paris and the battlefields of France.” If
the trouble becomes more acute Kitchener
may be obliged to poll-tax travellers who get
in the way of the combatants. The tourists’
picnic hampers might also be seized in gross
for the starving Belgians.
The Osterley brought home a little coterie
of Bananaland folks, including Mrs. Alford
and her daughters, of Toowoomba.
Barrister Coffey is on the long trail home
via the United States. He left ’Frisco for
Sydney about January 19.
Hayley Lever, the Adelaide artist who
often exhibits here, is specially keen to fling
fleets of fishing boats on large areas of can-
vas. He is doing well in the U.S., and a big
picture, “Harbor, St. Ives, Cornwall,” is
going the rounds of art shows in Dollar-
land, and winning considerable applause.
A very tame and apparently law-abiding
collection of amateurs call themselves The
Vagabonds Club. In order to help the
Alexandra Hospital funds they produced
“The Profligate” at the Repertory Theatre
the other evening. The plas is rather dull,
in spite of its Family Herald atmosphere,
and the brawny young man, Jack Mackey,
who took the title role, looked like a promising
juvenile churchwarden. The other bad man in
the play, the older wicked boy of the piece,
rejoiced in the stage name of Lord Dangers.
But he, too, merely looked like a lord who
had fallen into reduced circumstances and
might reasonably be suspected of dishonor-
able intentions towards the nearest ham and
beef shop. Lotta Montgomery got more out of
her part as the country wench, Janet, than
did the rest of the company. The Vaga-
bonds Club might in future put its com-
bined efforts into a sparkling comedy with
much better results. The orchestra was
above the low-water mark usual at these
entertainments. Even the piano was more
or less in tune.
Gilbert Neill, the young man in charge of
the Tourist end of the N.S.W. Bureau, weds
Miss Mary Turtle, of Manly, before this
reaches you. Except for a quiet family
gathering the knot is to be without frills.
Ivy Shilling (the Teuton “c” has fallen
out of the dancer's name) is going to be
principal boj in the Birmingham panto, this
year.
Miss Frances Quirk, the Australian who
has been so much pushed along by para-
grapfiists’ nibs during her adventures in the
enemy’s country, was in Rome a few weeks
ago. There she was granted an audience
by Pope Benedict.
Australian artist Dora Meeson-Coates is
rounding up volunteers for the new female
police service in London.
Artist George Coates, formerly of this
continent, has put the last bit of color on a
portrait of the late Lord Northcote. The
subject is difficult from an artist’s point of
view, for the able Northcote had no fancy
beauty points or parlor tricks, and his most
flattered photos were distinctly ordinary.
Still, Coates has dressed his effort in all the
gauds of official millinery, and Lady North-
cote has approved of the result.
Mrs. Box, a sister of the only Melba,
passed this way the other day for a holiday
in Maoriland. Sister Nellie, by the way,
has definitely fixed up a tour in the U.S.
It starts in October, and will consist chiefly
of concerts, with a top-dressing of grand
opera.
Dr. Jim Woodburn. formerly connected
with St. Vincent’s Hospital, was in London
when the Great Shoot began. He intended
getting wider experience on the other side
before settling down in practice here. Now
he is busy at the interminable front, where
experience is mixed with gunpowder and
dreadful noises.
Dr. Woodburn, in a letter to a friend, re-
marks that the trenches in France are “no
fun” in midwinter. The only cheerful bit
of scenery he can see amidst the snow and
icy water is the countenance of T. Atkins.
According to Dr. Jim, the amazing British
Tommy is an incredible Mark Tapley who
hums snatches of “Tipperary” in a blizzard.
R. E. N. Twopeny, who used to be in
charge of the Pastoralists’ Review, is still
pursuing health in England. The last re-
port mentions that the Bath cure—which
presumably consists in swallowing mineral
water by the quart—was most efficacious.
Lieutenant Henry Talbot, of the 11th
Hussars, was reported missing some time ago.
Now he has the bad luck to be reported found,
wounded and a prisoner on German terri-
tory. It is about the worst kind of luck
the youthful ex-A.D.C. could have. The
enemy hasn’t earned a reputation for being
over-squeamish in caring for those who fall
into his hands on the sick list, whether they
be friend or foe.
Russian women who can afford to spare a
thought or two for fashion, are ordering fac-
similes of the fighting Cossacks’ uniform
coats. These are made in claret, green and
dove-color. The skirts are full and wide.
Even the cartridge pockets are faithfully imi-
tated. Mrs. Moscow adds a dagger and a
silver ear-ring. The large Cossack ring
hangs from one ear lobe.
A preliminary meeting, to decide what the
artists and art workers here can do for the
Cause, occurred in the R. A. Society’s
rooms. As the War office says it wants
more motor-lorries, a committee has been
formed to try to get one or two. The pic-
torial hat will go round at an early date.
C. .T. Dennis, who wrote an “Australaise”
for Tiie Bulletin, is being made the vic-
tim of theft wholesale. Sometimes the
larceny is acknowledged ; sometimes it isn’t.
If imitation is the sincerest form of flattery
the writer who catches the public ear is the
most flattered and shamelessly robbed per-
son on earth. A legal friend tells me that
felonious entry into premises by day is
house-breaking, which is bad; but similar
entry by night is burglary, which is much
worse, because it is liable to wake the
baby. My idea is that the man who steals
another man’s poem by day should be sen-
tenced to 12 months in gaol, and the one
who reprints a poem after sunset without
acknowledgment and without remunerating
the poet should be slain with the jawbone
of a sub-editor.
Julius Knight, looking thinner than of
yore, and with a violent sneeze in his head,
loses none of his drawing power as regards
Sydney audiences. With a vapor-bath
atmosphere inside the Royal, and a thun-
derstorm grumbling outside, the theatre held
a most satisfactory amount of cash on Satur-
day night. It appears to be part of the
“Scarlet Pimpernel” plot that Julius and
Irene Browne should burst into song as the
play w T ears towards a climax. Of the two,
and in spite of a frog in his throat, Julius
is the lesser evil. Irene, who slogs heavily
at the attractive part of Lady Blakeney, has
a top note that gives the shivery sensation
noticed when a pencil scrapes a slate with a
high squeak. Lizette Parkes, the beauty of
the present Knight team, is the charming
Suzanne, whose waist is of the 19in., dis-
tractingly-slim species.
Granting the energy and enthusiasm the
average Sydney club dame is focussing on
socks and other items for the Red Cross-,
one cannot accuse these e'ood ladies of hiding
their light under a thimble. For instance,
the Women’s Club is shouting itself hoarse
in the friendly pages of the British Aus-
tralasian. After an impassioned declara-
tion of its prowess one club member re-
marks : “I venture to state, but without the
military authority of the Federal Govern-
ment” (there’s a drop of soothing syrup for
the censor in this), ’‘that our Sydney Red
Cross has done more than all the other
States.”
Paderewski has given up the notion of
combing out his wonderful yellow mane in
Australia this year. He was to have given
a concert-season hereabouts commencing in
March. He is even uncertain, writing from
Switzerland, whether he will complete his
unfinished U.S. tour. When, feeling he had
enough of the ’Murkan eagle, Paddy last
made tracks for Europe, he left 36 concert
appearances amonast the missing, and the
Eagle is feeling rather sore about it.
The London Bobby is said to sho.w no
signs of professional jealousy towards the
Policewomen, who have sprung up like mush-
rooms since the war. These volunteers are
clothed in a feminine edition of Robert’s
uniform. They have to drill, obey orders
fi;om a chief constable, and make them-
selves generally useful. The business of
guiding refugees, and looking after the
knickerbocker members of the huge antheap
keep these Robertesses busy on their beat.
This autumn we are likely to have a
blizzard of topical hats. Even in its woe
Paris is not quite indifferent to fashion so
long as its freaks are tagged on to a passing
event. The “General Joffre” hat, as adapted
by French milliners, is a saucy junior-Napo-
leon tricorne, with a fluff of ostrich feather
climbing over its peaked ridge. An “Eliza-
beth” hat, in honor of Belgium’s plucky
Queen, is a mixture of toque and Belgian
soldier’s cap. It is designed to shade one
eyebrow, and is not suitable for heat waves.
The “Alexandra” is a dashing imitation of
a British Guardsman’s high-crowned cap.
Women who have a tendency to run to
feathers can stick any kind of stiff plumage
straight up in front of this headgear, and
moult in windy weather in the highest pin-
nacle of style.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.
“Public Servant” : When the war started,
the long-promised N. S. Wales Superannua-
tion Bill was on the stocks, and the Service
felt the lump of disappointment come into its
throat once more. Great was the relief,
then, when it was made known that the Gov-
ernment regarded its promise to bring on the
Bill before Easter and pass it as sacred.
The news was circulated that Premier Hol-
man was emphatic on the point. The De-
partments thereupon arose as one battalion
and said, “Hoch der Holman!” But we
didn’t know. The other night Carmichael
introduced the measure into the Assembly
and explained that, while it would be passed
into law, the scheme would not operate until
gazetted, and that would not be before the
conclusion of the war! It’s a poor subter-
fuge, but Holman’s subterfuges were nevei
robust.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, February 1, 1015.
My Dear Arini, —
Governor Stanley and Secretary Victor
Hood came down upon Middle Brighton last
week like wolves on a fold, and were back
at Macedon almost before the suburb knew
of the visitation. Their objective was the
Brighton Orphanage. It has been tending
motherless bairns for 01 years (Exon, lately
departed, was its guardian spirit for a big
slice of that time) and now it aspires to do
even better. Considering that it enjoys no
Government grant and is forced to depend
entirely on charity in consequence, it is
doing astonishingly well. A cooking school
has been added unto it; also four new class-
rooms. Sixteen girls will be daily initiated
into the mysteries of cooking, which will be
good for the girls and better for their coun-
try’s digestions.
President Dr. Wilson welcomed the visi-
tors, and the most glittering of them un-
loosed the usual flood of eloquence. It was
splendid to hear him tell of the small, dim
class-rooms in which he first started to learn
the wonderful things he can now talk about.
The small orphans cheered loudly, and
seemed proud to think that their governor
had been even more uncomfortable than
themselves in his youth. Then there was a
bit of Rule Britannia talk and more cheers,
after which the visitor fled, as described
above.
In the midst of war’s alarms Captain
Jackson, of the Coldstream Guards, has
plighted his troth to a younger daughter of
the Willie Pearson family, of Gippsland.
They trekked to Europe a while ago, and
ought to be back before this nibful of ink
is dry.
Frank Brown, who is closely related to
poet Louis Esspn and several other people
of talent and culture, and has done some
very fair work himself, has been arrested by
a charming stage lady, who proposes to keep
him in custody for the rest of his life. The
lady is Miss Edie (’aider, at present under-
studying Miss Minnie Love in the ’’Cinder-
ella” panto; but after the wedding, two or
three weeks hence, she and her captive are
to settle down in a place called Monea, near
Avenel. Frank Brown recently sold his
liberty for a mess of squattage in those
parts, and is growing mutton there.
Jennie Brenan is taking a brief holiday in
Sydney. She is staying with the Hugh ,1.
A ards at Potts Point—a menage where she
should hear plenty of “shop” talked. Hugh
J. was a champion dancer himself in his
early days.
Worth, the mightiest of all dress-designers,
has gone to the front, and so has his brother.
Also, the chief color-dreamer from Dreeolls
and Winter, whose little silkmark is a poem
inside a gown. Who would have thought
the drapery lords had so much fight in them?
Those British golf champions who haven’t
jone to the war have been playing a special
tournament for the benefit of the Belgian
Refugees’ Fund at Heaton Park, Manches-
ter. Michael Scott, who married an Austra-
lian girl, and golfed here for years and
years, was one of the combatants.
The A.W.N. Lea gue has been proudly ex-
hibiting its new ambulance in Collins-street.
It is spick and span and white, and has four
stretchers. Fevered by the purest form of
patriotism, the Wiinmen are now sending
round the bonnet for another.
The widow of “Weather” Ellery, first Gov-
ernment Astronomer in Victoria, died a few
days ago in Toorak.
Mrs. George Fairbairn and Mrs. Frank
Stuart are among the latest to donate travel-
ling kitchens to the military authorities.
I've given them three cheers apiece. Talking
of. these kitchen-contrivances, what is the
point of the long chimneys that tower above
them, shaking like a drunken man in an
ague, and offering a perfect mark for the
enemy’s artillery? Also, what is the object
of the sort of water-tank which splashes
scalding water over the roadway at every
step? If it was liable to fall on the German
one could understand. As it is, the con-
traption appears far likelier to boil our own
gallant defenders.
I held forth last week on the way Ameri-
cans love a princess. As a matter of fact, a
passion for any old royalty seems to be a
disease with them. Having no royal family
of their own, they create every other notable
a king or a prince or something similar.
There are Pork Emperors and Wheat Rajahs,
a King of Candies and a Prince of Harm
curers. And now the Republican papers
are featuring a “Suicidd Queen.” One could
become a duchess with very little trouble in
the U.S., I should think—for example, a
Drink Duchess.
Motorists Francis Birtles, Frank Hurley
and a bulldog have just done a 6000-miles
trip into North Australia from Sydney. The
Majestic Theatre has the film of the episode,
and there is a heap of incident and sensation
in it. The travellers are shown crossing the
Leichhardt, the Nicholson and the Cloncurry
rivers, and the trees on their banks are
beautiful to behold. There are artesian
bores of boiling water that make the Maori-
land geysers look silly, and long roads over
the plains, with good old bullockies winding
along. 40-strong, under the big trees. In
places the car swings through grass up to
the drivers’ chins. Elsewhere the tyres
knock over anthills as though they were
mere human beings. And yet these things
have sometimes taken 100 years in the build-
ing, and by comparison with one of them
the camp resembles a hat lying beside a
pyramid !
Pantomime producers could collect some
striking ballets from corroboree artists. In
one corrob. the natives glisten as if they
were in black silk tights, and each has a sort
of Hussar uniform painted on his gleaming
hide. The hair is mopped up high with
black and white wool, like a smart military
head-dress. The “painting” is done by
smeared blood daubed with fine feathers and
down. The “steps” are as light as a
Hielan’ mon’s prancing.
The blacks who fish in the Nicholson are
also a picturesque lot. They use large cir-
cular nets, on circus-hoop frames. They
wade down the stream in triangles, with head
arid shoulder above water, and the hoops
following. The fish leap with apprehen-
sion, or something, as the nigs pass. I don’t
know what they are, but they look like huge
salmon or Murray cod.
An alligator’s nest, with 60 eggs, suggests
a straw-stack. I daresay the business could
be carried on up North (as it is in some
parts of America) of having special breeding
places for the ’gators., and slaying the pro-
ducts for the sake of their ultimate purse-
and-Gladstone-suit-ease end. The- ’gators
are handled quite easily when they are
babies, and go two or three on a man’s hand.
Later they grow to weigh a couple of tons,
but as they take about 300 years to do it,
the business of cultivating them is obvi-
ously not one that gives any hope of “quick
returns.”
Camels are in the picture, too. They
would look like haughty Lady Yere-de-Veres,
so supercilious are their noses, were it not
for their feet, which resemble frying-pans.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Fred Youngs left for London and the Agent-
General’s Office on the Mooltan after a series of
dinner engagements. The oratorical bombardment at
some of them was- terrific. ; Their small son went
with father and mother. Miss Hilda . Cornish was a
passenger by the same boat; she is travelling with
the Walter Duffields.
E. H. Wainwright, who is with a battalion of the
Ilo.val Fusiliers, was on Christmas leave at Hartle-
pool at the time of the raid. He had a fine view of
the proceedings. He saw 50 houses on the “Front”
wrecked, and noted that some of the unexploded
shells had a diameter of 9in., and bore stamps of
2000 and 7000 metres. The .Tack Mouldens, who also
came in for it, have been living at Seaton Oarew,
nearby, for some time, and though their house Was
untouched, shells were whizzing overhead and landing
in the fields about them. It is said that the German
ships lay close in all night, and signalled in the
British code to the battery at the mouth of the
Tees' asking for the whereabouts of the rest of the
fleet. When the information was trustingly supplied
they waded in with light hearts and a feeling of
security.
The Sturt statue may cost, a bit more than was
expected originally. So a London committee lias
been formed, consisting of thy Agent-General, Mr.
J. B. Whiting and ! -'Myss; Sturt? ‘to look for a few
pounds. Also, Mr. Simpshn Newland and Josiah
Symon, K.C., have been added to the local committee.
The old well in the basement of the Prince Alfred
Hotel —one o'f the original sources of Adelaide's water
supply—is to be used in connection with the building
of the Town Hall. Adelaide is perforated with old
wells. They are only discovered, as a rule, when the
boarding over them gives way and astounded citizens
fall in.
The foundation-stone of the new building for the
Institution for the Blind was laid by president
Charles Goode on Saturday afternoon. The land
site faces the North Adelaide Children's Hospital.
Inspector George Orr died on Sunday, aged 66. He
came to S. Australia as a boy of 19, and, joining the
mounted police, saw much exciting service in the Far
North.
A little more war news from London :
Captain Blank has been staying here during his
four days’ leave. He says that many society women
are at the front solely for the purpose of showing off
their clothes, and naturally the men wish them to
the D —. The women also go down to the steamers
to see if they can catch a nice invalid. The sick and
wounded invariably prefer to go where men will
attend to them.
After that compliment to our sex, Yarrie,
let’s make up our minds to stay at home.
It was my ambition to powder my nose
coolly in the firing; line w T hen a press photo-
grapher was bandy, just to show the Ger-
mans what the Girls of the Bulldog Breed
think of them ; but I haven’t got the heart
now to go and be some sort of a ministering
angel. What’s the use when you know you’ll
c.nly be regarded as a maddening devil?"
How the war has changed things, my
dear! I read the other day that our own
Marshall Hall had brought back some good
news of clever Florence Hood, said good news
being that she had escaped from Prague.
“Escaped !” My brain whirrs like a clock-
work toy when I read about aspiring young
musicians “escaping” from any such place,
and remember the frantic exertions we had
to make to hoist them there.
In Benson’s Dramatic Co., which was play-
ing “The Merry Wives” in London at
Christmas time, the evergreen Mrs. G. B. W.
Lewis was going strong as Dame Quickly.
Rupert Conrick, one of the promising young
men of the same co., is another matter of
Melbourne interest, for Rupert was a star
Pupil at South Yarra school of histrionics
run by Josephine Saiiiuell, who got most of
her training from Mrs. Lewis long ago. So
the indefatigable Josephine now has her old
tutor and her young pupil acting under
Benson’s management. Oscar Asche, by
the way, w T as a Melbourne schoolboy before
lie went abroad and became a Bensonian
actor, and Cyril Keightley and others who
fished for tittlebats in the Yarra have be-
come associated with Shakspearian drama
in England. The sacred lamp of the “legi-
timate” is fed with a lot of Melbourne oil.
Many friends and admirers of Harrie
Ireland, who have lost sight of the auburn-
haired actress for two or three years, will
be glad to hear that Dr. McKenzie’s inter-
esting surgical theories re Harrie’s lameness
promise to be triumphantly verified. The
doc. diagnosed bone displacements and adhe-
sions and things, instead of rheumatism, and
under his treatment the patient lady sub-
mitted to more agonies than she ever
endured as a suffering heroine in Bland
Holt’s theatre of operations. For about 20
weeks she lay helpless on boards as hard as
those she used to walk upon. Now she is
able to get about the house on crutches
without assistance, and Dr. McKenzie pre-
dicts that she will presently waltz back to
stage life with renewed grace and vigor.
Apart from its surgical character, the treat-
ment has given Harrie Ireland a thorough
rest-cure.
Keith, son-nf-the Edward Carliles, who
went to the war as lieutenant in the Vie.
“Tropical” force, has a brother. Hildred, who
went with the Army Medical Corps. Sister
Maud is a staff nurse with the same lot, so
the Garble family is well represented in the
Great Disturbance.
The law case that everybody has been
waiting for comes due this week or next; but
the leading lady has broken her engagement
and left for a foreign land. So her glad
rags will not be seen in court. The other
lady, however, is prepared to come along with
her plain, unvarnished tale, and proceedings
should be of social interest. The case has
at least one side-issue which some people
would go miles to hear about —for there is
always a fear of the special bits being kept
out of the newspapers.
My statement that one of the bright Gil-
lespie girls of Mentone had just been married
in Colombo was much too previous. Miss
Alma Gillespie has sent word of her engage-
ment to a Mr. Maurer, whose address, when
lie is at home, is Calcutta. But the marriage
is mentioned only as an event likely to
happen very soon.
The Norman Brookes pair gave a grisly
account of their pleasure-trip to Flanders,
where they inspected the acres of death and
desolation. Nobody would have been sur-
prised if Norman had welded his racket into
a gun, as Anthony Wilding and many other
sports have done. Perhaps the sight of a
battlefield put him off the notion. In any
case, his health is too poor.
Beatrice Day. who used to collect for the
Red Cross outside theatres on “first nights,”
has now got a fresh patriotic notion. She
attends. picture-shows and such-like places
and recites at young men in order to make
them enlist. The young men, who have in
many cases taken their girls to the enter-
tainment expressly to avoid being sung and
recited at, simply bate it. But everyone
else approves, including the managements,
and the Empire is understood to be delighted.
Major George Raleigh, of Victoria, was
killed last week at the front while aviating.
He had been mentioned in despatches only
a week or so before his fatal flight. The
Ernest Raleighs are relatives.
Fred Sargood, eldest son of our lamented
Frederick Softgoods, belongs to Sydney
nowadays. But we are interested in him
still. Hordes of relatives trekked over from
here, accordingly, for the wedding of
daughter Nancy with John Antill Pockley.
John is a son of the doctor and a
brother of the boy who was killed in Papua
at the beginning of the war. The Antill part
of the family, have been mixed up in mili-
tary matters in Sydney since the earliest
days. There has mostly been a colonel or
two in evidence, and an Antill girl married
Colonel Lassetter, and tlieir son was wounded
in France the other day, and is now on his
way to Australia to recuperate. So you see
they are essentially the sort that fear no
foe in shining armor.
So Geoff Kiddle, of Walbundrie, is to
marry Auntie Phipps, after all. I used to
tell you so about once a month when the
Fullers were here, but I was always con-
tradicted. He is a large, aquiline-featured
young man with a gift for polo, 'and of
course you know what she is like. He was
in India when the war began, and left with
the first Expeditionary Force from there.
Since then he has been mentioned in
despatches. He got engaged during one of
the five-day trips to England which the
War Office allows the fighters to make.
Vanda Clarke, the tall, slim Mount
Schank Clarke girl, has been operated on
for appendicitis at Dr. Shields’ hospital in
Park Lane. Her mother is by her side, and
all was going well when the last bulletins
were posted.
The Hammersley girl, who, as I told you
two weeks ago, Jias become engaged to young
Rupert Ryan, is a sort of a cousin to Big-
john Forrest’s lady. The latter was a
Hammersley before she married.
Clive Baillieu is to be married to Miss
Rubv Clark on February 21 at St. John’s,
Toorak.
v ie . le trouble in a block of flats which
•shall be nameless. One tenant returned to
hei luxurious rooms after the theatre to
find them flooded with water, the carnets
ruined, the expensive wallpaper a S
smudge, and the pet. Persian cat insane from
dampness Detectives were summoned.
" ith relentless logic they deduced
presently-going on the evidence that a
stream of water was pouring through the
root—that the damage was the fault of the
flat above. Investigation proved that they
had not erred. The owner of the flat
above was away week-ending. Girl-like, she
had left the faucet of her liand-basin trick-
ling during an under-pressure of water.
The faucet turned to a torrent as soon as
suburbanites bad finished watering their
gardens and bathing the children. Hence
the devastation. Mrs. Flat-above re-
turned, rectified the slight damage done to
her tile-lined bathroom, and sent down an
airy message to say she was “sorry.”
“Sorry!” wailed the victim of her casual-
ness. “What’s the good of that! Who is
going to pay for the damage f” It is this
question which will have to be settled ulti-
mately, I fear, with the help of Costs.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

WARNING TO DYSPEPTICS.
Many interesting paragraphs have recently
appeared in the press concerning the remark-
able antacid properties possessed by Bisn-
rated Magnesia, and thousands of people
now regularly take half a teaspoonful of the
preparation in a little water after their
meals as a safeguard against indigestion,
dyspepsia, heartburn, flatulence, etc. It
appears, however, that in several instances
other forms of magnesia, bearing somewhat
similar names, have been used, often with
very unpleasant results. To guard against
dangerous substitution and to ensure getting
the genuine produce as recommended by
physicians, readers should ask their chemist
distinctly for pure Bisurated Magnesia, and
insist oii being supplied in buttles having the
full name —Bisurated Magnesia —blown in
the glass.*

A Simple Recipe for Clearing
the Complexion.
Exposure to hot winds and sun makes the
nerves and muscles of the face tired and life-
less, and the skin shrivelled, wrinkled and
unhealthy.
If anyone with a sallow, blotchy, wrinkled
or pimply complexion will only take'a little
(rouble in following the directions here given
they will find a marvellous improvement in
their complexion. Get some Puritol Wax
from the chemist or draper, and apply at
night, washing off in the morning with warm
water and soap. Then lap a little cold
water 10 times upon the face with the hands,
allowing it to get thoroughly round the
closed eyes. The treatment should be fol-
lowed out in every detail to ensure success,
and undoubtedly accounts for many of the
clear complexions so much admired by those
who do not yet know the secret. See that
you get the right kind, it will not injure the
most delicate complexion.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
No one can say that fortune is treating
Premier Holman badly-—if only there was
any kind of fortune that would keep that
unlucky person out of the pawnbrokery. So
far, seven months of the financial year have
ambled past, and the N.S.W. revenue is
£283,873 more than for the corresponding
period of 12 months ago. In the circum-
stances. Holman—who has no war on hand
but thinks he has —seems a great deal more
sorry for himself and his difficulties than the
case calls for.
A DIFFERENCE.
GRAN: "Wot! In swiminin' eigen?"
THE BOY (gloomily): “ Swimmin' be blowed!
It ain’t near big enough. Just soakin."
Colonel Holmes, the officer who sent the
captured German flag to Sydney’s Gaud
Mayor, and who has lately been explaining
the terms of Papuan surrender to a large
flock of critics, was good enough to throw in
some remarks about the conquered territory
—or the explored fringe of it. In the opinion
of the Colonel, it isn’t fit for occupation by
white people. No place freshly come to
ever was fit for white people. The first
conquering Colonel Holmes who arrived at
London said it was a foggy marsh. The
first Holmes who came to Sydney said it was
an inhospitable wilderness. And the first
of the same military breed to invade Vic-
toria abandoned Port Phillip because it w r as
too hopeless a place even for a gaol.
The Yankee agents who descended on
Australia in the hope of grabbing oil coun-
try in Papua and were promptly turned
down by the Commonwealth Government
are finding the Peake crowd in S. Aus. much
easier prey. Around Kingston in particular
hundreds of square miles of territory' have
been taken up on search licenses by people
who are not searching worth tuppence, but
who in some cases are actually negotiating
for the sale of their untouched areas for as
much as £BO,OOO. Of course it is only to
U.S. oil grabbers that money like that is
talked. The next six months will show
whether there is an oilfield in Australia,
li' there is one it will be the. Common-
wealth's misfortune that it will be controlled
by the most thoroughly inefficient Mines
Department in Australia. That is, it will
lie controlled so far as the Department
hasn’t allowed control to pass quite out of
its hands.

TRADE UNIONIST: “ That's all right boss, but
what are yer payin' for overtime?”
The most beautiful argument yet advanced
against Protection is submitted by Pollen
Bishop, an English water-color painter of
good standing, who lately settled near
Adelaide and is getting good commissions.
A section of the public is asking for a
removal of the present 25 per cent, duty on
works of art other than those coming direct
to a public Art Gallery. To the discussion
Bishop contributes this: —
The tax was imposed as an experiment, and it has
now been in force upwards of three years. Up to
two years ago I was an English artist, sending only
a certain proportion of my output to Australia. This
duty practically killed my export business, and the
only way of meeting it was to come out myselt and
work inside the tariff wall. Therefore I now com-
pete more strongly than before with the local artists,
and 1 believe other English painters are thinking
of following my example.
Australia, wanting population, puts on a
Protective duty; and when the duty does its
work and brings the population, Follen
Bishop asks us to stand off and observe the
failure ! Still, humorous as he is, you can t
refuse a hearing to a painter who talks of
“my export business” and is content to call
his immortal works an “output,” just as if
they were crates of cheese. ;~
Victoria’s closer-settlement policy is being
turned over by a commission, which is learn-
ing the old lesson that Government repur-
chase is a dangerous medicine to give the
patient. Members and ex-members of the
Lands Purchase Board say that again and
again country which had been for sale for
(say) £5 was promptly withdrawn when the
Government looked that way, and couldn't,
afterwards be got for less than, (say) £6 10s,
In some cases a deal was made —sometimes
for no better reason than that the .Minister
told the Board to go ahead —and then of
course the price of similar land in the same
district was puffed up to :£G 10s. also. The
existence of a buyer with plenty of borrowed
money to play with, and rotten ideas of values,
proved in Victoria, as everywhere else, a
curse to the private buyer, and a magnificent
thing for the land-jobber and the boodling
syndicate.
It is the business of a High Commissioner
to advertise; but Mackenzie, of MX., over-
did the drum-banging when he wrote to
Churchill, after the smashing of the Blucher
by Beatty’s battle-cruisers —“The gift of
New Zealand’s ship was a most far-seeing
act of statesmanship.’’ What the Maori-
landers themselves would have thought about
it if their remote and vulnerable islands had
been bombarded by raiding Germans is
another matter; and that the raiders didn’t
actually arrive was solely due to their
dread, not of the distant New Zealand, but
of the lingering Australia. Some varieties
of “far-seeing statesmanship” need a plain
business-like lifebelt to keep them from
foundering. It was Maoriland’s good luck
that Australia was able to supply the life-
belt.
Italy is preparing to abandon its neutra-
lity, and to “strike for ils own hand.” So
says Oceania, the organ of the Italians in
Australia. The stroke is to be delivered,
according to the same journal, in April, and
against Austria; and it adds that Italy
already has 1,250,000 men under arms. The
information to this effect comes from “the
very highest sources, which admit of no
possible doubt as to the accuracy of the in-
telligence.” Italy will demand the “lost,
provinces” which have always been claimed
by the Irredentists; and it will probably also
demand such possession of Yalona as the
British now claim of Egypt. The lone-hand
game will leave it free to make peace with-
out consulting any of the Allies, and even to
avoid war at all should Austria obligingly
yield without a fight; but this latter is not
probable. Austria has never forgotten Ous-
tozza and Lissa.
This strange announcement was found
last Thursday on the business paper of
N.S.W. Assembly :
Mv. Holman to move: “That, in view of the unpre-
cedented character of the financial situation, this
House is of opinion that the Government should he
prepared to enter into contracts with firms of
approved standing willing to undertake reproductive
works upon the system outlined in the agreement pro-
• posed to be entered into with Messrs, Norton Grif-
fiths, as laid upon the table on August 20, 1913,
and submitted to this House on October 2, 1913.
Now Holman’s revenue was still going
strong when last heard of, and he had no
war charges to meet, and the British and
Australian Governments were still finding
him loan money—also, in any case, he is
alleged to be an anti-loan Premier. So will
this slim and plausible cove say what is the
“unprecedented character of the financial
situation?” And will he state what he
thinks of State Enterprise, as he conducts
it, if it is to be a mere fair weather device,
and his Government has to take refuge in
the wude and hairy bosom of the Private
Capitalist as soon as a cloud appears on
the horizon?
THE RIGHT MAN GETS THE JOB
TOR ONCE.
It has been arranged that Alfred Deakin shall go
to the Panama Exhibition after all. — Item.
The Law seems to think that, if the
criminaleopard can’t change his spots, the
next best, thing is to force him to change
from one spot to the other. The other day,
in Adelaide, a mean type of confidence
trickster who had tried to operate on two
workless immigrants was “ordered two
months’ imprisonment, the warrant to be
held over for two days in order to give
accused a chance to leave the State.” This
was, in effect, a hint to hurry across the
Victorian border. It may not have been
within the powers of the South Aus. court
to order him right off the earth; but the
kicking of an undesirable from the State
where he is known to the State where he
isn’t known seems a foolish way of dodging
a responsibility and piling it on a decent
neighbor.
The trumping of poor old Joe Cook’s
“patriotic” trick was a droll episode in the
game of party politics. Joe expected to
make a point with his sudden outcry for
100,000 men. But Pearce got on a camp-
stool and shouted ditto. Then Hughes
screamed an eloquent statement of "the
Empire’s Need, and, before turning off his
How of eloquence, added: “I have noted
Mr. Cook’s remarks concerning the Govern-
ment’s war policy. He is standing firmly
behind the Government. This is most, satis-
factory.” Joe’s trick was trumped. He had
hoped to take up a position in front of the
Government, and found himself standing
firmly behind it.
