CULTURE AT BOOBOOK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In a patient and persevering way '
The scholars were grounded at Boobook school.
They learned pot-hookery every day,
Arithmetic and the Golden Hole,
The alphabet and the reason why
All beetles walk before they fly.
The teacher crammed them as best she could,
And mustered them daily The Flag to cheei !
But never once told them that nationhood
Is founded on factories built Out Here,
While the Union Jack that is German made
Is flown to the glory of German trade.
When there was need for it, now and again,
She walloped the boys with a German strap.
And little she recked of Australia wber.
She showed them the world on a German map,
Or, exhorting the class with an earnest air,
She rested the while on an Austrian chair.
Scribbling with chalk from a Prussian pit,
She showed Little Willie, the stupid and slow
(On a Wurtemburg blackboard expositing 'it),
Exactly how ’leven-times ought to g 0....
Bill, with a Bavarian pencil, took
A note in his German exercise-book.
And presently, doing his humble share
For a cultured, industrious Enemy,
Bill messed up his studious neighbor’s hair
■ With ink from a bottle marked “Saxony,”
And he drew blind pigs on a slate that came
From the hold of a boat with a German name.
And, later, a scrouging chirographist,
With a twist in his elbows, a knot in his knees,
And a dull, gnawing pain in his pen-wracked wrist,
“Love” —scratch and splutter —“your enemies
Seven times over, with never a break,
He wrote in his copybook (German make).
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
WHICH?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Smith owned a little back-blocks run,
A wife and pair of kids ;
He took no stock in Empire Days,
He made no yearly bids
For cheap applause by waving flags
And boasting of the shame
And anguish he would bring on those
Who sneered at England’s name.
Smythe was a Liberal of means.
He classed such folks as Smith
Among- the Empire’s Menaces.
He catalogued them with
The scoundrels who support Home Rule,
And strive for higher pay,
And burgle people’s homes by night,
And wreck their peace by day.
To Smythe, Smith was Disloyalty
Expressed in human form ;
A grasping, ill-conditioned dog,
A skulker and a worm.
Also, a waster to the core
In sentiment and deed,
On whom no country could rely
In time of stress or need.
War came, and Smith went to the front.
He left his home and wife.
He gave the Empire all he had —
His youth, his health, his life.
Smythe sent a Fund a thousand pounds
(It might have well been four),
And went on living as he had
For years and years before.
The press proclaimed that Smythe had played
A truly noble part.
Smith fell, unadvertised, abroad,
A bullet in his heart.
Which was the better patriot,
As men are judged by men,
The citizen who swung his sword ?
Or he who swung his pen?
N.S.W.
?

the 13tli at Caulfield) and the subsequent Newmarket

week. Judging from the principal discrepancies in
the estimates of the V.A.T.C. and 1 .R.C. handicap-
pers, one would sap that Flash ol Steel, at /st. 210.,
is the best-treated sprinter in the Newmarket, tor
the same gee got Bst. 31b. in the Oakleigh. dhe
scale of weights for the shorter flutter is about 61b.
higher on the average. Woorak, for instance, gets
This is said to be a “crushing” burden for a 3-year-
oiu to hum,) down the Newmarket course, but AYoorak
is the -popular fancy, nevertheless. Land of Song,
the imported nag with good British credentials, got
9st. and 9st. 31b. for the big events at Flemington,
which seems to explain his trainers announcement
that the Irish Derby winner will be kept to w.t'.a
races. All the same, there is no visible reason why
Land of Song shouldn’t have a go for the Newmarket,
anyhow, if lie is the flier - he is said to. be. Tn thd
Australian Cup the mucli-talked-about Sir Solo, from
Maoriland, is on the Bst. 51b. mark, along with St.
Spasa and Audacity. Sir Solo has done nothing
since he came to Melbourne to justify his notoriety.
At Flemington in November, and again on New
Year’s Day, the aged horse was carefully advertised
as a “cert” by tricky speculators who appear to
have started a tipping agency with ulterior motives.
The horse ran badly each time. Still, the winkers
and whisperers are glorifying his name, and the
looks quote the oft-defeated animal at a short price
for the Australian Cup.
The tipping agency aforesaid was at work last
week-end in the interests of De Gama, the brilliant
Maorilander, which won tln-ee bai d’""n° d-rD''* - c~e
Melbourne Cup meeting, and was otherwise remark-
able for having its nomination refused, ‘\vitho.,l c .-
planation,” by the stewards of another racing club.
De Gama was entered for a petty race at Sandowrr
Park —so petty that it was not generally thought of
as a likely journey for the great horse. Moreover,
one of the" nag’s most notorious backers was tipping
Maltravers as a cert for the same race, and the
newspaper prophets followed suit.. Consequently'
Maltravers was always first favorite in the betting,
whilst De Gama’s price fluctuated exceedingly, as
the result of the market operations of its notorious
friend. But De Gama won on the post, whilst the
cert. Maltravers was nowhere.
“Snaffle”: Sol Green retires from book-
making and will give more attention to his stud
estate, Shinlev. in the Warrnambool (Vic.) district.
Of course, breeding is consistent with ownership, and,
maybe, Sol will be more heard of in that line.
Thus Shipley may become historic, like de
Mestre’s Terrara, Tail’s Bvron Lodge, Fisher’s Mari-
bymong and Wilson’s St. Albans. For bookmakers to
own horses has always been regarded as suspicious.
Yet Australian records that way, in the higher ranks,
have been clean. Joe Thompson (Solomon) had a
genuine love of racing. The horse was not a mere
gambling machine to him when he owned a good
nag. He was in partnership with Jimmy Wilson, of
St. Albans. They experimented with smart coups,
but always legitimate, according to turf ethics.
Their biggest attemot was a quadruple—Darkie for
two steeples and Flying Scud for twe flats. Three
legs of this came off; the fourth failed. Flying
Scud was an amazing pony. Tkev got him in for
two big-horse handicaps at sst. 101 b., allowable then.
Joe made no secret of wbat he thought of Don
Juan in 1873. In a sportsmanlike spirit he said to
all bands: “This horse must win the Cup,” and he
took a party of quidnuncs down to St. Albans to
see the prodigy. The general verdict was: “A
feather-legged carthorse.” But “Augur” Chapman
put in a saving clause: “They run in all shapes.”
Of the army of players who have taken
part in inter-State cricket matches between N.S.W.,

Trumper, Gregory, Iredale (N.S.W.). McAlister, Gra-
ham, Laver (Vie.), Hill, Gehrs, Giffeu and Reed-
man (S.A.). Clem Hill, with 6169 runs, has the
best aggregate, and Noble, with 68, has the best
average.

“Sr”: Westralia is not the only State
that lias an acquaintance with rung-in prads. There
is Tassy, for instance. Recently a bob-tailed animal
called Tnrpinite won a trial race at a T.R.C. meet;
but some busvbodv looked at liis mouth, which was
wide open as'lie finished, and reckoned that, instead
of being a three-vear-old, as the nomination claimed,
he was”an old prad of five autumns. It is reported
that the crowd who worked the fake stood to win
£4000; but vulgar inquisitiveness as to the nag s
bona fides alarmed the books, and no pay-out was
made. Turpinite was duly disqualified, but the story
is not yet at its last chapter. Still, the ringing-in
is done, mostly at the trots. About a dozen trotters
who have scored in the Island* during the last two
years were swindles.

Sam Rainsbury, formerly well known in
racing circles, has weighed in. He w r as at one time
a prominent member of the Victorian Club, and con-
sidered to be well in, but died poor. Rainsbuiy
acted as commissioner for Robertson Bros, when
their horse Coronet won the V.R.C. Newmarket
Handicap in 1885, and some £70,000 was landed in
bets. #
“O’Cork” : J. O’Reilly, jockey, of W.A.,
who won the recent Perth Cup on Dollar Dictator,
handed a cold chop to that arrant foolery, inter-
viewing the winning jockey.” Instead of blatantly
feeding the reporter, as do the star mannikins of the
eastern States, with a lot of necessarily-imaginative
“details of the race,” O'Reilly briefly remarked, in
a rather bored tone of voice: “All I know about the
race is that I looked after my own horse and had
a clear, easy ride right through, without any trouble
at all.” Then he slipped away to ride in the Sap-
ling Stakes, and became some more winning jockey.
DAVE SMITH, TUG.
I. T. Garslake, the old and somewhat
disrespected Vic. trainer of gee-gees, died in Mel-
bourne last vveeek, aged 78. The snows of winter
never settled on Garslake s head, for in his middle
age his hair was dyed jet black, and, later on, he
were a dissipated-looking wig of raven hue. One
of the last horses of note that he owned and trained
was Straightfire, which ran some good races when on
the job, but was usually . off the job. Straightfire
.must have been alive or dead in nearly 100 races
altogether, and he was an 8-vear-old, with 7st. 81b.
lip when he ran second to Cremorne for a Caulfield
Cup. The late £ £ Smith of old confided his
racers to Carslake’s care. When the old man was
dropping out of notice his son, Brownie Carslake,
came to light as a successful jockey. Brownie was
jockeying in Austria when the war broke out.
Died recently in Melbourne a Hindu
Owner Of raceliorses named Mukand Lai, known
among- his compatriots as “the Prince.” Inciden-
tally, lie supplied his fellow-countrymen with hawk-
ers’' goods. Lh'l” started a disastrous turf experience
with a big bank balance, which grew gradually
smaller and beautifully less. Among his horses
was the famous Slipper, sometimes known as the
slippery Slipper.” Whenever Lai and his brethren
backed the animal it lost. When it was not fancied
by the discolored stable it invariably won. Lai
tried several trainers, but with no personal success.
Vt last he leased his gee-gees. Just about there he
died and the doctor said the trouble was consump-
tion’ Hard luck isn’t on the list of diseases.
Tasmania is to see a son of Carbine under
silk during its coming big meetings at Hobart and
Launceston. This is the imported Defence, who
was purchased a little while back by A. Wooliscio
from Baillieu Bros., of Melbourne. Defence was
one of the “also rans” in last Melbourne Cup, and
that about represents his general form to date.

The late Isaac Carslake, of Melbourne,
was known in his younger days as “Tommy Jones.”
As a jockey he was always Tommy Jones, and he took
out his training license in that name. “Carslake”
being so much more tony a name than “Jones,”
the old generation ot' racegoers never gave Isaac
credit for having inherited it, and when he registered
his proper appellation With the racing clubs he was
generally suspected of disguising himself in an alias.
Two sons of Carbine have now won the
Hobart Cup: Flintlock, a grey, who collected it in
1899, and Defence, who won the other day. Defence
was imported from England by the brothers Baillieu
(Melb.), and was owned by them until a few weeks
ago, when he was sold for 500 guineas. It is a pity
he is a gelding, as Carbine’s sons have all done well
at the stud. Flintlock, now in Westralia, is a case
in point.
* * * *
Monday’s sculling match over the Parra-
matta course between Alf Felton, of Sydney, and
Robert Ford, of the Hunter, for a wager of £2OO a
side, was a gift for Felton. Despite statements
ascribed to Northern judges, the match was gener-
ally looked unon as more than a doubtful sporting
proposition; for Felton has beaten Charlie Towns
• and Harry Pearce, and has done well at handicap
racing; whereas Ford, 24 years of age, has practically
done nothing outside handicap racing. On Monday
he rowed nicely, but entirely without dash, and his
opponent had the money in his pocket before a mile
had been covered. The verdict of umpire Bill Beach
was that a length and a half separated the men at
the gun, but • that was letting Ford down without
too cruel a bump.
Webb, of Wanganui, lias agreed to row
Hannan on the Wanganui for the championship of
Maoriland and £2OO a side at the end of May. That
is final—at time of writing.
Fritz Holland, the bald-headed welter
phenomenon from the U.S., clashed with Dave Smith,
heavyweight champion of Australia, at Sydney
Stadium on Saturday - night. It was one of the
closest and best fights .ever seen at that resort. There
is really little more ip say about it. Fritz fought
with extraordinary speed and' ■driming; Dave with
tremendous power and skill .and''*resolution. They
both took heavy punishment, I>JVe looked rather the
worse of the. two, owing to his Yankee friend’s
attentions to his eye's, but Fritz probably felt as
bad as anyone in the house from time to time.
Nevertheless he kept his customary smile glued on
his countenance to the end. Neither went within
miles of knocking the other out. Towards the finish
every man with long hair, high-heeled hoots and a
wad' of chewing-gum in his mouth opined that Fritz
had won by as much as from Hoboken to Chicago ;
most of the rest of the spectators reckoned that Dave
was simply giving Holland a boxing lesson. One or
two advanced the view that a draw would be a fair
thing, and were howled at furiously. Harald Baker
said the winner was Dave Smith. It was a fair
decision. #
How futile it is, by the' way. for anyone
to be dogmatic about a boxing match! Last week
Jeff Smith hit Darcy low, and indicated that he
knew he had erred by standing off while his seconds
besought him to go in and finish his wilting oppo-
nent. Six ring.-sid.ers to whom the writer -spoke
never saw the blow. The writer, who was opposite
Dave Smith, did not see that astute second throw in
the towel. At every contest all the experts round
the ring miss dozens of hits. So. of course, does the
referee. Harald Baker, for example, with all his
advantages, missed the low hit in the Darev-Smith
disturbance. If people who are within a few feet
of the fight fail to note so many details, how much
less equipped to blither are the uproarious ones on
the back seats! The plain fact is that no one sees
more than about three-quai;ters of anv fight ; and
that is not nearly enough for spectators to. judge
arrogantly on. #
.Timmy Hill lias at last forfeited the Aus-
tralian featherweight title. He doesn’t like making
the weight, and he never gets any peace from
aspirants to the title. The featherweight gaud is the
most sought after in Australia. Geikie, Melville,
Thorn —all Bananalanders —-Ellis and Simpson claim
the goods, and each can show some cause for his
claim. The title reverts to Queenslander Joey Russel,
who has been resting. He intends to come forth
from seclusion and argue the case.

Walker got a run, and at tlfe eleventh head led
10—5. He continued to itiow away, and. at the
sixteenth score was 15- 7. At the eighteenth
head the game was given up. The winner is Pro-
fessor of Modern Languages at Auckland University
College. ' # #

victory over N.S.W. Things were blue for the
Southerners' when -Ryder brought oft a sensationa
catch at cover-point and dismissed Mailey, who had
been helping Bardsley to pull the game out of the
fire Further, Ryder was top-scorer in both innings
(55 and 93 not out)’, and captured two wickets for
19 in one innings. In three matches for A ictoria in
inter-State cricket this season he has piled up 267
runs (highest aggregate on his side), and his aver-
age stands at 66.75.
Victorian left-hander 11. Ironmonger
bowled “a treat" in N.S.W. second innings, and
finished with six wickets for 73 runs. This seasons
Sheffield Shield matches have been his harvest. He
has taken 26 wickets for 409 runs, which over-
shadows all other efforts with the ball in the
tourney. Incidentally, this is Ironmonger’s first year
in the' inter-State class. ■

“Stumps”: Cricket is threatened with
extinction unless interest in it can be revived. lo
me it seems that more suddenness must be intro-
duced. In no other sport or pastime (billiards is a
business) is the agony prolonged over one day.
Events like race meetings, which are strung out o\ei
a week, arc complete each day. People have acquired
the habit of wanting to see the finish before bedding
down for the night, and the cricket enthusiast who
pays his money on Friday, knowing that the result
Won’t arrive until Wednesday next is getting
scarcer. Cricket on baseball lines ought to make a
good game, with rules so framed that quick run-
getting would be essential, tour spells to each side
(sav, half an hour long) at the wicket, alternately,
could be allowed, each side to make as many runs
as time permitted, irrespective of the fall of wmkete
o' •>« in baseball, (sav) five out all out. The result
would be quite unlike the game of cricket handed
down bv tradition, but it would make fo a-„ood
afternoon’s sport; and what is tradition that spoit
should be sacrificed to it? # *

The English supporters of Australia s
sprint freak, Donaldson, are far from satisfied that
their man’s recent defeat by England s ex-amateur
streak, Applegarth, was proof of track superiority.

race did not say if Applegarth made the journey.
Anyhow a series of matches between the runners is
a sure thing, and. already negotiations have been
floated for a dash over 200yds. for a £5O purse.

“The Bunkered Idiot” : I can see a green
mile of golf-links spread out like a carpet, and
specks of golfers making their religious procession
across it. Thev interrupt their mysterious pilgrim-
age every little while to bend double and lean then
foreheads on the grass. This- act of worship is
addressed to the invisible lost ball, which, when you.
are close up to it, is about as small as a dray-
horse’s liver pill. The ball’s,, part of the game is
to lie quietly under a tuft of grass and say nothing.
It does it splendidly; in fact, the reticence of the
lost golf-ball is a lesson to the . Kaiser, that patter-
comedian of the battlefield. Doubledmp: golfers with
empurpled faces may kick the grass oi the field and
howl inquiries all round—the .remains stolidly
in hiding, and doesn’t even betray itself
in its sleeve. I have seen this sort of thing many
times and, because I am convinced that golfers hate
it I ’ am' perfecting a new ball ■ with fi’i interim
rattle which will be wound up by the caddie befoie
the ball is clubbed by the maniac it belongs to.
The rattle will be started by the impact and will
continue for a minute. Besides facilitating the d,s-
coverv of the wretched mil it will make the m-'
more like that of a shell, and obviate the necessity
of shouting explosively at inoffensive grangers who
happen to be in the way. I also intern ‘ni S oa?
golf balls with interior phonographs which will soar
through the air shouting such phrases as Lookout,
vou fool!” and “I belong to Murphy! , but, ot
course, these will be more expensive.
LIFE AND THE LIVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some men are haunted by the conviction that all
grass is mud, that everything is cronk, and that the
world in general is a take-down. — Bulletin.
“Lite is real —earnest, too!”
Rats! Just stow it, Mr. Poet!
It’s a rotten sham all through—•
Crook and cronk; a “have”; a “do.”
Well I know it!
Life’s a frost, and here we freeze
Till Perdition (that’s Ignition) !
He who plays Diogenes
Honest man or thing ne’er sees—
Hopeless mission!
Life an optimist will shake
To his centre. All is “schlenter” !
When the whole world’s on the make,
Till your exit one long take —-
Down —why enter?
Life —I own up my mistake —
Once I trusted ; but, disgusted
Now, put “wise” and wide awake,
I assert that Life’s a fake —•
Herewith busted !
P.S.
Life’s a dish I can’t digest—
As I eat it makes me shiver.
It lies heavy on my chest;
Life depends, ’tis manifest,
On the liver!
VICTORIA.
BILL BILE.

WALTHER & STEVENSON’S
CATALOGUE OF SADDLERY
AND HARNESS—the very thing
I wanted, too. It’s the best
thing of its kind I’ve seen. There’s
a range of saddles, bridles and
harness here that would suit any
rider that ever crossed a horse,
and any horse that was ever
foaled. These long, flap saddles
and the leather points to the
panels, instead of padding down
the sides, is a champion idea. My
word, that’s a fine seat in No. 16
Saddle—it has just the right dip.
The seat in No. 59 takes a bit of
beating, too —it has the right clip
and narrow waist. A chap would
only leave that saddle for a
feed. Hullo! They’ve got the
Sole Agency for George Eland’s
Whips! So George is in Sydney
—I never use anyone else’s whips
when I can get his. The worst
of it is they are so good that the
beggars pinch them. Here’s a
list of prices for the whips, with
handles to match.
“I like that bridle 148—it
would suit me. A ringhead with
rein and billet in one piece—
cheap, too. I must get one of
those fly veils for the old Neddy
—they fit under the bridle, and
will save the old chap a good
deal of trouble. I’ll write to
Walther & Stevenson to-night.”

The man whom the Turks really miss is
old Moses. He led his great expedition out
of Egypt along the dry route which the
Ottoman army, now making for Egypt, re-
gards as little short of hopeless. But Moses
had a habit of striking the rock when
water ran short, and he always got a sup-
ply. Nowadays there is abundance of rock
but not a pennyworth of Moses.
Joe Cook, ex-Prime Minister and naval
expert, is also orchardist at Ivoro-Vo, Baulk-
ham Hills, a charming spot near Parramatta,
where Mrs. Cook makes the family jam,
raises the family egg and chicken, and culti-
vates the strictly domestic virtues. Here
many little Cooks live a care-free life, just
as happy as if Pa had never been turned
down by a capricious public. A son, an
enthusiastic young man, manages the
orchard, and this year got a revenue ot
£4 13s. fid. from a single plum tree.
Dead at Bathurst (N.S.W.) Henry King,
whose title to fame rests on the tact that he
helped to unearth the huge nugget (6301 b.
gross) which was found in the claim ot
Beyers and Holtermann, at Hawkins Hill,
on June 21. 1871. When cleaned up the
speck yielded £12,000. Holterman com-
menced* as a digger in 1856 at. Hawkins
Hill (Hill End). Two years later the
workings were given up, gold being too
scarce. After various unsflccessful resump-
tions, in the beginning of 1871 he opened
out a vein yielding 50oz. per ton- J°e y ® rs i
promptly sold out his shares for £*o,ooo. but
llolterman held on and didn’t do much good
afterwards. Before he ‘‘struck it ’ he kept a
barber’s shop at Hill End.
The destroyers built at Cockatoo Dock
(NS.W.) are not the first war vessels con-
structed in Australia. No. 1 was the 60-ton
Spitfire, launched from Cuthbert’s yard
(Sydney) during the Russian scare in 1855.
Its dimensions were 51ft. on the keel, lbtt.
beam, and sft. 6in. draught. It carried one
long 32-pounder on a traversing carriage.
When the scare petered out the craft was
employed tending the lighthouses, and, later
on, it humped produce from the Northern
Rivers to Sydney. In 1865, about the end
ot the United States Civil War, Victoria tried
its hand, at Williamstown, on a weird “gun-
raft,” consisting of two flat, hardwood boats,
fastened together and covered so as to form
a platform 46ft. long and 28ft. wide. Upon
this was mounted a smooth-bore b2-pounder.
“Moutere”: The badly worn silver-coin
nuisance is rather acute in Sydney just now.
Almost every day I get a doubtful coin from
somebody and wear out my life trying to
trade it off on somebody else. Often there
is a slight indication of engraving on it
when held sideways to the light, but I can t
get tram conductors to juggle with it long
enough to convince themselves that it really
is a piece of minted metal. The matter
becomes serious when it is my last coin and
it is a case of walking home if I can't cash
it. Surely there ought to be some means ot
getting such stuff out of circulation. While
it remains it is a constant cause of loss to
the poorer members of the community ; but,
then, why should a Labor Government worry
• about the poor? They vote instinctively for
Labor, and if they were better off they
mightn’t. Which is a piece of political
philosophy as old as the pyramids and as
corrupting as a portfolio.
“Gombi”: The Law an ass? Nonsense!
Listen to my story. In a township on the
lower Darling consisting of four pubs, two
other houses and a sandheap, the local con-
stable summoned two men for language.
The P.M. travelled 150 miles to hear
cases, and was assisted by a J.P. The
Bench disagreed, and the cases were ad-
journed. Next month a new P.M. travelled
'350 miles and then adjourned the case, as
the J.P. and P.M. at the first hearing were
not present. P.M. No. 1 started off for the
next court, but was bogged half-way by a
mixture of one inch of rain and two of dust.
Next month the cases were withdrawn, and
Justice was satisfied. The expenses ran to
about £lOO. And nobody knows if the two
men used the wicked words or not, though
everybody knows that there isn’t a soul in the
place who hasn’t used worse when the local
goat has insisted on breaking in and eating
his last sock.
“J.G.”: Has any Bulletin reader met. a
shortish little man clad in a pepper and salt
suit, wearing a squat little boxer on his head
and carrying a. well-thumbed bible under ins
arm? When he struck our place the other
day and knocked at the door I expected to
fin'd one of the usual duns or hawkers on
the mat. But the little man wasn’t one.
He nodded to me, opened his. Bible, and said
that he was merely sowing a few seeds oy
the wayside. Then he read a line or two,
closed the Book, clapped it under his arm,
said good-day, and moved on. _ I watched
him visit two or three of my neighbors, and
he went through the same brief performance
with them. He was one of the most indus-
trious sowers of seed I ever knew, but his
chances of a good harvest are meagre. lo
get anv sort of a crop you must harrow the
seed in. My industrious friend merely
scattered it on the surface, as it were, and
the old carnal crows came and —where, alas,
was the seed?
Houston Stewart Chamberlain, a rene-
gade Englishman who has settled in Bavaria,
has pumped in a good deal of the hot air
which fills the afflicted German head. A
son of the late Rear-Admiral William
Charles Chamberlain, this unbalanced Eng-
lishman married the only daughter of
Richard Wagner, the poet and composer;
and after that he became more pro-German
than the Prussians themselves. He went
to Dresden in 18S5, possibly with the in-
tention of settling there, but five years
later passed on to Vienna, where he re-
mained until 1908. In 1892 he began
writing (in German) books belauding the
German and Germanic peoples. He asserts
(particularly in his “Foundations of the
19th Century”) that the Germans are the
true Chosen People, and that all the great-
est men of history have been of Germanic
blood. Even St. Paul! In fact, he suspects
Jesus of being a German. Whereupon a
German writer, J. L. Reimer, recalls that
Jesus had the blue eyes and roseate skin
of the Teuton, and alleges that the name
Jesus mpans nothing more nor less than
German!

Are You a Victim of
CATARRH!
You know what Catarrh is, with its humiliating and discomfort-
ing symptoms.
A slight cold develops into a stubborn and protracted one, while
the discharges from the nose are most offensive and annoying.
As the inflammation extends further up into tire head and the
delicate mechanism of the ear is attacked, there is a continual
buzzing and ringing in the ears, and frequently the hearing is
partially or totally lost. Headaches are almost constant, wit 1
sometimes acute pains in the eyes, and the nose becomes s>
plugged up that the patient must breathe through the mouth,
while the slimy secretions are forced back into the throat,
requiring almost continual hawking and spitting to dislodge them.
At certain stages of the disease the odor of the breath becomes
very bad, to the mortification and embarrassment of the sufferers,
and the disgust of all who come near them. Gradually the
mucous discharge finds its way into the internal organs, and a
most miserable form of Dyspepsia, or Catarrh of the Stomach, is
the inevitable consequence. The blood becomes contaminated by
catarrhal poisons and unhealthy matter, and these are
distributed through the body.
Catarrh is then a deep-seated, dangerous, constitutional or
systemic disease, and its effect upon the general health and
system is soon apparent —appetite and strength are gone, weight
is lost, despondency takes the place of confidence, and the disease
frequently settles in’some vital organ or terminates in Bronchitis,
Asthma, Neuritis, or dread Consumption.

beginning its use. If quickly takes up its work of killing the disease

discharges in .he nose and throat —mucous that is finding its way into
your stomach and poisoning your system.

Legal Notices.
WILLIAM SUTHERLAND, who re-
sided in Warwick Street, Glasgow, son
of the deceased William Sutherland,
Plumber, in Lauriston of Glasgow, disap-
peared in or about the month of September,
1877, he being then resident in New South
Wales, Australia.
A PETITION has been presented in the
Court of Session (Lord Anderson, Ordinary)
at the instance of Mrs. JANE SUTHER-
LAND or KERR, Widow, residing at
Dunrobin, 245 Nithsdale Road, Pollok-
shields, Glasgow, and OTHERS, under The
Presumption of Life Limitation (Scotland)
Act 1891, praying the Court to find that the
said first-named William Sutherland has
disappeared ; that the date on which he was
last known to be alive was 24th September,
1877, and that he died on the said date; or
if not to find that the said William Suther-
land be presumed to have died on 25th
September, 1884, in which Petition the
Lord Ordinary (Anderson) on 15th Decem-
ber, 1914, pronounced an Interlocutor ap-
pointing the said Petition to be advertised
twice, at an interval of a week, in the
Glasgow Herald and Sydney Bulletin;
and allowing all parties interested to appear
for their interest, if so advised, on or before
Tuesday, 16th March, 1915.
NOTICE of the said PETITION and
INTERLOCUTOR is HEREBY GIVEN,
and all parties who have knowledge of the
movements of the said William Sutherland
since the date of his disappearance are re-
quested to communicate with the subscribers.
ALEXANDER JUBB & TAYLOR,
Writers,
124 St. Vincent Street. Glasgow; or
MENZIES, BRUCE-LOW & THOMSON,
W.S.,
23 York Place, Edinburgh,
Agents for the Petitioners.
16t.h December, 1914.

There is nothing much in that to interest
the old Land Mortgage Bank shareholders.
A few years back, when the unfortunate
concern was trying to make the job of dying
as painless as possible, the Freehold Assets
Co. stepped in, bought practically the whole
of the shares, and is now watching its pur-
chase die. It shouldn’t have to watch for
very long. Of the host of properties thrown
on its hands after the smash there is only
£lO worth left. All that remains, therefore,
is to go on collecting the balance of purchase
money as it becomes due. This shows how
the job is progressing
Dec.,’l3. Dec.,’l4.

Net assets .. .. . . £118,911 £118,936
There are 145,770 shares, each paid to
10s. 6d. If the outstanding balances
are paid up. and the mortgages are all right,
each share is represented by 16s. 3d. worth
of assets. The Freehold Assets Co. gave 12s.
for those it bought, and then paid up half a
crown, so, on present book values, the buyer
is still something solid to the good. How
much the old Land Mortgage shareholders
are to the bad is too painful a subject to
resurrect. ,

As the profits shown for the half-year have
been reduced by £164 for depreciation of
premises, the co. has done exceedingly well,
War and drought have raged; but evidently
they haven’t yet cut into the commission
earnings. And the co. has other earnings
which will tend to grow rather than shrink
with bad times. It has a paid capital of
£20,000, and’of this and its reserves no less
than £34.461 is in public securities. From
money dropped into such property the
return for the next few years is likely to
be higher rather than lower. All round, the
investments, in spite of their gilt-edged
character, supply probably 7 p.c. on the capi-
tal ; and that doesn’t leave a great deal tor
the commissions to do in order to keep the
present 11 p.c. div. going. The co. doesn t
publish a profit and loss account showing
its commissions separately ; but it mentions
that the value of the trust estates in hand
is £1,884,081. Six months ago the total
was £1,817,893, and 10 years ago £981,021.
Substantial business is thus assured no
matter how bad a slump there may be. In
that respect a trustee co. is unusually lucky.
A man can’t postpone his death just because
things have gone flat. So the jobs of the
undertaker and the trustee co. go on just
as usual, even though both of them may
have to give more funeral for less money.
Big Hill Central. Stawell (Vic.), the
show that was worked by Wilkinson and
Co., may yet prove a valuable stand-by to
the mining industry of the district. A good
deal of prospecting work has been done by
the present co. on the large bodies of low
grade ore, and the results are cheering—in
fact, of late, payable. At the 150 ft. level
150 ft. of ore is already proved, assaying
from 10s. to 50s. for a width of 14ft.. while
at the 75ft. level another large body of
payable ore has been proved so far for a
length of 130 ft. The assays are very good
and regular, while a rise going up at one
place is in ore assaying £5 18s., showing
that the bonanza stuff is not yet all ex-
tracted in Stawell. Appearances at present
justify the prediction that the Hampshire
end of the Big Hill, so long neglected until
Wilkinson and his party attacked it, .may
compensate for the waning fortunes of the
Magdala and Sloane’s end. It is certain
that in turning down the option once held
over the property the S. Australian and
Westralian slimes plant crowd threw away
a better thing than they knew.
45- &
Mount Bischoff (Tas.) is trying hard to
restore its fortunes. Manager Millen has
visited Melbourne to. explain matters, but
the market seems indifferent. All the same,
the recovery of metal prices and a new
smelting contract with Y Water (N.S.W.)
will help this old concern. Bischoff is smelt-
ing without purchase, which is good to the
man who expects a speedy rise. This policy
has enabled it to get the Briseis business;
and Briseis has been for years the largest
alluvial tin producer in Tasmania. There
isn’t, indeed, the slightest excuse for send-
ing tin concentrates to be smelted in the
Straits or Europe. We have just-as-good
smelters at Sydney (N.S.W.), Launceston
(Tas.) and Irvinebank (Q.).

THE history of the piano industry in Australia is a record of
a long and continuous struggle against clever and unscrupulous
German methods. The strong anti-German feeling engendered
by the present war has not dictated the attitude of Australian piano
manufacturers, whose interests lie now, but no more than they have
always done, absolutely parallel with the advance of the British
Empire, and the victory of the Allies in Europe.
The production of pianos in Australia has afforded employment
to many hundreds of highly-skilled and highly-paid Australian
craftsmen; has assisted in the development of other Australian
industries, both primary and secondary, but has in no wa;y contributed
to the building of German Dreadnoughts, the equipment of German
soldiers, nor the purchase of the shells and bullets to shoot down our
soldiers.
Beale & Co. Ltd. are the founders of piano manufacture in
Australia. The business was established over 18 years ago by its
present controlling Director, Mr. O. C. Beale (born at Mount Mellick,
Oueen County, Ireland) ; the Directors and Managers throughout
the business are also descendants of British families for many
generations-; the whole of the capital employed is entirely Australian,
and the few necessary supplies for the factories are Australian or
from the countries of the Allies.
Your personal judgment of Beale Pianos and Beale Player-
Pianos is sought, and we will demonstrate their superiority —fully.

The Deadly Submarine
Of all the engines of war, the submarine is just now
very much in evidence. It may be of interest to know
that the motive power for submarines when travelling
on the surface is motor spirit, large quantities of Pratt’s
brand being used for this purpose. When submerged,
however, the power and light are derived from electric
storage batteries, which have previously been charged
by the motor-engines above referred to. This use of
electricity permits of movements under water without
the tell-tale exhaust of a motor engine, besides pre-
serving the purity of the atmosphere in the submarine
—thus enabling long journeys under water to be made.
N.B.—Pratt’* enjoy* the full confidence of the
Allies, who are using it in enormous quantities.
Pratt’s Spirit’
Vacuum Oil Co. Pty. Ltd.
Throughout the Commonwealth and N.Z.

IN BANANA LAND
“Politicus”: Re boycotting of Teutonic
goods. We are too fond of assuming that
after the war nobody will buy stuff “made in
Germany.” The public memory is always
short, the public conscience mostly negli-
gible. When Wilberforce was beating the
anti-slavery drum a London draper put up
a placard : “No goods made with slave-grown
cotton sold here.” A -rival opposite put up
a sign: “All goods made with cheap, slave-
grown cotton.” The latter got the trade.
Moral: Trust the people, but keep your tariff
“high.”
“Cleveland” from the North:—
“Tula” (B. 14/1/’l5) throws more light on the
question of the destruction of N.Q. timbers than
most correspondents. Like him, I hate to see good
commercial timber going up in smoke ; but the posi-
tion is that the small cocky has to start in and make
good pretty early in the deal, unless he wishes to be
strangled by the mortgage or the bank. Now,
making good requires the removal of unsuitable
vegetables from the holding promptly, so that cane
may be grown or forage provided for Strawberry.
The cocky will discuss till the cows come home how
many figures his cheques would run into if he could
get the wood to market. Southern theorists, who
cry out about the iniquity of destroying so much
fine timber, ought to come up and show how it is
to be saved. If there’s even a pound an acre in it,
we want that pound badly. We don’t burn the
stuff for fun. “Well, let it stand,” says the critic,
taking up his last trench. Certainly; but what good
is that to anybody ?
“McNortli”: North Queensland has once
more appealed to the Commonwealth Weather-
Bureau for a meteorological station, and
has once more been given the answer that
encourages wrath. The present system of
telegraphing from Melbourne warning of an
approaching disturbance is of little use, for
the hurricane that destroys houses and ships
is also liable to smash telegraph lines and
interfere with wireless, and the man in
Cairns who, after watching his house and
props aeroplane over the horizon, receives a
“wire” from the South announcing that the
whirling devastation is on its way is apt to
be sarcastic. At some places on the N.Q.
coast no weather notices at ajl are posted
from Saturday night to Monday morning.
So the cyclone, which is a sinner and no
respecter of the Sabbath, has 30 hours each
week-end in which to approach unadver-
tised. The remarks, opinions and disserta-
tions of coastal skippers in this connection
will be forwarded on application wrapped
in asbestos., Last year the citizens of Cairns
and Townsville tried to establish a station
of their own, but the project fell through
for some reason or other. Lots of projects
fall through for some reason or other.

SYDNEY PRESS: “If you keep on walloping him from your end , we might get a kick out
of the old boss yet.”
The Queensland climate goes about its
business in a treacherous way. Any self-
respecting, deadly heat would let its victims
know it was killing them. The Bananaland
article just lures them on. The usual coun-
try crowd had gathered at a Dalby pub the
other day to talk the same old crops and
weather and politics. To vary proceedings
someone remarked that the company was a
very fragile lot, and proposed that it should
adjourn to the nearest weighbridge. There
were 15 of them, and their average weight
was lost. 71b. One of the crowd, aged 34,
was 17st. 41b. ; two others (aged 44 and 35)
couldn’t do better than 15st. odd ; three went
between 14st. and 15st., and the rest, with
the exception of a midget of 9st. 111 b., went
over 12st. A climate that treats a doomed
crowd like that has a lot to live down.
From London
“The most continuously beautiful of the Australian
States..!’ Native-born residents of long standing
might {pause before being dogmatic on that subject;
but your rev. tripping tourist has no difficulty in
making up his mind on the evidence of a few days’
observation from railway-carriage windows. The Rev.
Dr. Adeney, who recently went to Australia to whip
up the Congregationalists, pins the ribbon on Queens-
land’s broad bosom. Brisbane is “really a wonder,”
and its Queen-street is the champion thoroughfare of
the Commonwealth. Toowoomba is “a holiday resort
and place of retirement for elderly folks.” Queens-
land houses are built on piles to ‘'avoid the white
ants, provide a security against floods, and furnish a
nice shady place under the floor for the family wash-
ing.” But the soul of Adeney was shocked and
grieved at the discovery that there were less than 30
Congregational churches to add to the continuous
beauty of the North.
The Trespasser.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Was there nobody there when the moon was
full?
It might have been only a crackling leaf,
Or a petal blown from a blossom sheaf *
Or the snap of a burst toadstool.
Yet in humble flowers and haughty weeds
I heard the call of a pipe of reeds!
A minor lilt to the blare of day,
The garden at night held its usual sound —
The suspiration of sweating ground
Would explain it all away—
But not the flicker of flaming eyes
Or the vines a-trail from the shaggy thighs!
Oh, yes! We can prove so much indeed!
Did the rosy “Pride of India” move
Looping the trellis close as love,
With its lavish jungle weed.
Some crawling snail we should find, no doubt,
Or a sportive spider swinging out!
We could cry it was only wind of dawn,
The moan bf dreams that the. wakeful hear;
But a rose leaned down to the pointed ear
Of a crafty, peering faun —
And it was never a dawn bird’s throat
That gave the breath for that trickling note!
Was nobody there when the moon was full?
The dove cote sheltered a grey quartette,
But they slept, lid-blind, to the moonbeams’
fret.
Nor guessed at the silver pull
That drew me out of a restless bed
To lurk in a coppice heart instead !
The rose clung shuddering to her stem.
Fluttered the leaves on her dainty tree
As she sobbed : “He is here to rifle me”
’Neath her dewdrop diadem.
And yet when the thin faun lips drew near
I heard her coo to his furtive ear!
Was nobody there through the unmarked
hours?
A quince-rod bent in the grove beyond.
A shadow passed o’er the gleaming pond
Of a head enwreathed with flowers.
And this morning, deep in the moss-mat’s
woof
I found the print of a blunted hoof!
And the rose—no more will her red heart
stir.
She is bruised and wilted and spent and
torn,
As though the burden of lust was borne
And the soul kissed out of her.
But she did not turn from me as I came.
She seemed too weary to think of shame.
Was there nobody there when the moon was
full?
It might have been only a widening leaf
Where an opening moonflower sighed relief,
Or the puff of a dried toadstool....
Yet all day one voice in the leaf choir leads
The echo left by a pipe of reeds!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, January 30, 1915.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii,—
The other day the Mayoress presided over
a band of eager women, who decided to
induce the public to go to a carefully-pre-
pared aquatic carnival on February 27, and
shell out just a little bit more towards the
squelching of the Prussian. Mrs. Jenkinson
was in the same chair an hour or two later,
encouraging another party of helpers, who
will commence operations on February 17,
with a concert in aid of the aeroplane fund.
I think no other State has an aeroplane
fund. Barrister “Tom” Macleod is the lead-
ing spirit here. He is a Queenslander who,
some time ago, went to England and quali-
fied in aviation. His flying pal, Jones, is
English-born, but has become air-naturalised
along the Downs. These two, with 18 or
20 helpers, have been toiling for the past
six weeks at the construction of an aero-
plane, which, when completed, is to be used
to instruct ambitious local youths how to
fly, so that in the near future the Australian
forces may include native airmen. True,
the Government had thought of the same
thing, and has its own machines and in-
structors. Still, Macleod and his machine
are not likely to be in the way.
Violet Paget is busy on,her campaign in
aid of the fund for the dependents of sol-
diers. On February 16 she will direct a
matinee at Palace Gardens, where all the
theatrical shows in Brisbane will be repre-
sented. That this fund is deserving is bet-
ter realised since the first few deaths have
occurred in Egypt. One soldier left a wife
and seven little Australians.
Canon and Mrs. Hay, who have been to
England, returned home recently, and this
afternoon the entire parish, or as much of
it as wasn’t in hospital, or at the races, or
otherwise engaged, rolled up to Mrs. Kenric
Brodribb’s moated grange and expressed
pleasure at having once more in their midst
the Christian influence and example of the
wanderers. The shepherd spake unto them,
saying: “Do! the admiration is mutual.”
Talking about shepherds, it will no doubt
interest you to know, my dear, that Com-
mander Davies, of the Tiger, which helped it-
self to some made-in-Germany navy the other
day in the North Sea, has a brother in
Bananaland. He also is a fighter, and de-
votes his energies to the battle which is
without end against the Devil, the founder of
the Gerhog house; The Queensland mem-
ber of the family goes to the root of things,
while the naval scion attends to details.
Mrs. Price, of Ashton Hall, Albion, gave
a pleasant party this afternoon, in honor of
her son Jack and his new and ornamental
bride. Another pretty girl wil join the
great mixed pairs handicap next Wednes-
day, when Mollie Ferrier will take for a
partner Julian Mackay, who is a squatter
by trade, somewhere in N.S.W.
Miss Laage, the popular and energetic
headmistress of the Girls’ Central State
School, is hack from England, after a holiday
of 12 months. She brought with her a supply
of up-to-date information and a store of fresh
health.
The Q.T.C. races to-day were fairly-well
attended, and the congregation included the
Arthur Morgans and daughter Alice. Mrs.
Cecil Palmer wore one of the new checked
voiles in her favorite shade —blue. Mrs.
Arthur Pierson sported an enviable ostrich
plume, and Mrs. Blume was also conspicu-
ous under ostrich foliage. The lioss Munro
family wore, amongst other things, an air
of excitement when the family horse came
forth to carry the family purse. I forget
where he left it.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
A patient man is Daniels, ex-M.L.A., who
was jockeyed out of the Cambooya seat
some 14 years ago. Daniels has since
invented a potato-digger that bids well, and
has spent a number of years in Maoriland.
But to-day lie is back in Brisbane, reopening
his old Cambooya grievance. The fact that
some seven or eight Parliaments have since
intervened doesn’t trouble him.
When Colonel Holmes made terms with
the Germans in Papua, he agreed to pay
the Government officials three months’ full
salary if they didn’t shoot. This money,
along with some other allowances agreed
upon, has been paid out month by month
by the Commonwealth Government. To
give the devil his due, the German Govern-
ment hasn’t kept the Commonwealth waiting
for a refund. The whole lot' has already
come to hand, the United States Consul-
General acting as intermediary in the tran-
saction.
The other day, owing to the collapse of a
road, six .Maorilanders and their motor-car
fell 120 ft. down the Buller Gorge. The
clumsy member of the party acquired a
broken jaw and collar-bone. It doesn’t take
much to put some people out of action.
N. S. Wales Public Service Association has
duly decided, by ballot, to register as a
trades union, the voting being 650 for and
523 against. The Association has 4000
members, and there are 14,000 civil servants
in the State. Now, the 523 who voted in
the minority, and a lot of the 3000 members
who didn’t take the trouble to vote, and the
10,000 people who couldn’t be bothered to
join the Association are all howling together
about the iniquity of the thing. A threat is
made that if the registration is persisted in
a petition against it will be loaded up and
fired point blank, or even blanky. But see-
ing that the people who don’t join an asso-
ciation, and thus place themselves in a posi-
tion to vote on its proceedings, have no right
to raise objections to its acts, and that
those who had votes and didn’t use them
have only themselves to heap odium upon if
the decision hurts them, and that the quali-
fied folk who did vote placed themselves
under an obligation to accept the resolution
of the majority, the noble 650 are not view-
A scrap of biography:—
Peter of Servia (aged 70J years) must be a tired
old man these days. He is one of the Children of
the Klepht, as distinguished from the rival dynasty
of the Obrenovitches who were the Children of the
Pig-Dealer. He spent most of his life in exile in
very middling circumstances, and he could seldom
afford the front-room suite at the hotel. He married
an inexpensive sort of princess on the strength of his
expectations and his descent from Karageorgevitch,
the Klepht, who was murdered in an hotel by Obre-
novitch, the pig-dealing prince. He buried his wife
in exile, also his father ; and his children were born
in exile. When he was 59 years of age the throne
came , his way at last; but the joy of the
old man was mitigated by the recklessness
of his eldest son. Peter rules over a country
about one-tentli the size of New South Wales, and
with a population of about 4,500,000. He has had
three big wars on his hands inside three years.
When the latest war broke out he somehow scraped
up a few hundred thousand soldiers, and a few coppers
in cash. The Austrians bombarded his capital for
four months, and finally captured the ruins, and for
a while it seemed that the little kingdom was lost.
But Peter is resolved, above all things, that he won’t
become a cheap exile again. He did lofty stunts (as
the Yankees would say) in front of his army on a
horse that was probably borrowed, and which most
likely had to climb a tree for its food. The army
responded, and the Austrians got the surprise of
their lives, and fled out of the country. Then, as
something like 1,000,000 Servians were starving, and
there was no money left, Peter—according to report
-—cut down the palace expenses to £3 a week, and
there being probably no palace left the amount
might be sufficient. Now it is announced that Ger-
many has lent 400,000 troops for a fresh attack on
Servia; so, presi mably, Old Man Peter is bracing up
for a fresh effort And according to all the evidence,
Old Man Peter will win.

“ Yes, It Gives a Queenly

TOILET TALKS.
THE average woman of to-day is usually
satisfied if her complexion is passably
pretty, little knowing that an exquisite skin
is now within her reach, providing she has
the good sense to stop using cheap made-up
preparations, and adopt what has now be-
come known as the mercolised wax habit.
It is a pity that every woman in the land
cannot be apprised of this simple method.
The few to whom its virtues are already
known have complexions that are the envy
of all their friends, and, as is usually the
case, the majority of them refuse to divulge
the secret. However, the formula has now
been verified, and is known as
mercolised wax. Enquiry at
several chemists’ shops has re-
sulted in ascertaining that this
substance has been known to
the select few for some years as
a complexion beautifier, but it
is only until quite recently that
it has been used to any extent
for this purpose. It was also
explained how mercolised wax
renews the complexion, but as
this is rather technical it is not
proposed to repeat it here. It
will be sufficient to say that
through some peculiar action the
wax absorbs the dead and dis-
colored outer cuticle, revealing the fresh
young complexion which every woman has
underneath. The process occupies about ten
days, and the change is so gradual and im-
perceptible that no discomfort whatever is
caused. The treatment is perfectly harm-
less. and the wax may be used as often as
desired.
what about that shampoo? Pre-
sumably like a good many others you
follow blindly in the beaten track, without
a moment’s thought as to where it will
eventually lead you. It is an impossibility
to expect the same make of shampoo to suit
each individual head of hair. In some cases
it would take out too much of the natural
oil, and in others not enough. Fair folks
require a milder shampoo than those with
dark tresses: therefore the logical course to
M
pursue is to make yoUr own shampoo and
graduate the strength according to the sus-
ceptibilities of your hair. The brush will
tell you whether you are treating the hair
properly. Like a plant in congenial soil the
hair will grow and flourish providing the
conditions are right; but abuse it as most
women do by washing it with some strong
alkaline solution and the effect is practically
the same as if you poured weed-killer over
growing plants. Before leaving the subject
I should like to mention that a few months
ago my local chemist recommended me to try
ordinary stallax in place of the usual made-
up powders, and I must say that
this substance, to my mind,
forms an ideal medium for the
purpose mentioned. If anything
it makes the hair rather too
fluffy and wavy, but some folks
prefer it so.
IT is astonishing the number
of women who suffer from
the humiliating affliction of
superfluous hair, and I am sorry
to say that through ignorance
or wrong treatment, the majority
of them have made matters con-
siderably worse by causing the
hairs to grow more quickly and
stronger than before. It ' only requires
a little thought to realise the folly
of it all. Removing the surface growth
for a few days may give temporary
relief, but in order to obtain per-
manent results the hair roots must be
killed. Caustic depilatories are worse than
useless, and on no account should the
hairs be pulled out. Apart from the
electric needle, which is a costly and pain-
ful process* any chemist will tell you
that the best home treatment is pure pow-
dered pheminol, which has a devitalising
effect upon the hair roots, in addition to
which it removes all trace of the objection-
able hair in a few seconds, q.nd the
recommended treatment gradually, but surely,
dissolves out the hair follicles, which
is the only method of permanently ridding
oneself of this terrible disfigurement. [?

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Calabash : Don’t look too far ahead. When
the Australian manufacturer, by the aid of a patriotic
tariff, has learned to supply the Australian popula-
tion—which will probably number 50,000,000 by that
time, owing to the influx of artisans, also agricul-
turists and shopkeepers and others to supply these
artisans —and the foreign debt has been paid off,
and we have our own ships and our own sailors, and
there is a surplus of manufactured goods for export,
then we can begin to think of your problem. That
will be about 400 years hence. You and Mrs. Cala-
bash and the whole Calabash family will be dead
then. Do your duty to-day, Calabash, and set a
good example for next day. . . .Pilgarlic: Anybody
can write the tale of mystery until it gets to the
last chapter. Then you have to explain the secret,
and the difficulty comes in. Just start this way:
(1) The heroine is alone in a compartment of an
express train; (2) she dozes off for a moment; (3)
she wakes up, and finds a dead body upside-down in
her compartment; (4) she leaps from.the train, and
falls on a German military leper in a spiked helmet ;
(5) the train is never heard of again, and apparently
the universe has swallowed it up; (6) the heroine
gets home and finds that her cottage has vanished
in a day, and a palace has been built on her bit of
land; (7) she is arrested on a charge of bigamy.
That is a good first chapter, but how do you reveal
the secret in the last chapter ?.... The Chee-ild: Good
par. Sorry mostly covered by a recent contributor.

The incurable Freetrader isn’t quite dead
yet. One “W. Buckingham” wrote in the
Sydney Telegraph (22/1/T5) as follows:
The present high cost of living is very largely
due to our Protective policy. We have determined,
in our wisdom and generosity, to send the foreigner
all our best goods in the shape of wool, wheat, hides,
tallow, dairy produce, frozen mutton, beef, copper,
silver, gold, and have determined to take nothing in
return. And for fear he should want to send us
some of his best goods in exchange we have put a
tariff barrier across our ports so that he shall not
be able to do so. All we are prepared to receive
from him . is a number of flat pieces of metal of a
circular shape and yellow color. Not that we have
only need of these pieces of metal, for we have
millions of them lying in our banks doing nothing.
Still, we are prepared to receive more of them in
order that we might put them into holes in the
ground under buildings set apart to receive them.
In addition, we are prepared to live on the worst of
' the things we produce, and if there are any things
we cannot make we are going to do without them.
Quite a number of people fancied that we
had gone into debt to the extent of well
over £300,000,000 through buying more goods
than we could pay for, and that we are still
borrowing because we buy more foreign
goods than we can pay for. But W. Buck-
ingham believes that we have vast surplus
wealth buried in holes in the ground. It
would be a great joy to Fisher and Holman
if Buckingham would show them even one
-of these, holes
LINES ON THE DEATH OF MY FAITHFUL DOG.
I was startled by a piteous wail
When I called her by her name,
And gently I spread a bag
Across her quivering frame ;
Her time was up, alas, poor brute
Her life I could not save
And within the shadow of a tree
I consigneed her to her grave.
. O’er the fatal exit of a dog
Tis folly for to mourn
Though by her death I realised
A deep affection gone.

In moving the adoption of the Report the Chairman, Mr. George J. Cohen, said:—
The Directors have the pleasure to-day of presenting to you the Report and Balance-sheet of the
Company for the half-year ending the 31st December. . t)lp
Before moving their adoption I will explain the principal items and, as usual, compare me
figures, with those of twelve months ago. , .. , ... •, +h .-- j.
The total figures show an expansion of over half a million, and on the debit side tnis _
represented by balance of Capital paid up £23,000, Additions to Reserve Fund £lOO,OOO, and an
increase of £1,071,000 in our Deposits, while of the other items on that side our Notes in Circulation
have been reduced by £6770 for Notes presented and paid, and our Bills in Circulation are less j
£657,000. Profit and Loss shows slighty better. . . . ,
On the credit side of the Balance-sheet there are the following changes in our Liquid Assets.
Coin and Cash at Bankers is less by £85,000, while our holding of Australian Notes has increase
b> Money* at short call in London shows £538,000 less, and Bills Receivable in London andßemittances
in transit a little over a million and a half less; this latter, of course being prmcipally cansed by the
delay in the realisation of the Wool Clip and other Produce ordinarily largely dea
6nd of British Consols and Government Securities has been increased by £255,000, mainly
by our subscribing for the War Loan Stock recently issued by the British Government. We have ais
made a temporary advance of £900,000 to the State Government. with last
With these various charges our Liquid Assets show a reduction of £679,000 as compare
year, and bear a ratio of a little over fifty per cent, of our liabilities to the public. v. v
The other items on the credit side, Bills discounted and all debts due to Bank, are grea e y
£1,191,000, but this also is largely the result of the delay m handling the Wool Clip, proceeds o'
would ordinarily in many cases have come in before the end of the year in reduction of advances
With the appropriation now recommended our Reserve Fund will stand at £1,755,000. *
We have provided amply for any possible contingencies and losses that may arise in consequence u
the Only a few days after our last Half-yearly Meeting the war in which the Empire
out and affected in a greater or lesser degree the operations of all the Banking Institutions in
world. Though we, of course, have shared in the disabilities arising from the restriction oi
generally, the present Balance-sheet shows you that our position is a very strong one.
You will observe by the comparison of the figures the progress shown. Our policy has been o k p
the Bank in a strong position, and we have preferred to keep more cash m London not bearing t
than under normal conditions. Not only have we done so, but we have been able to P van + affe 0 f
special assistance to valued customers here and in London, and this without having to take
the moratorium there. . , , , the
We have contributed liberally to the War Fund here and in London, and have 52 of our men in u
Expeditionary Forces, including Lieutenant-Colonel Onslow Thompson, one of our Directors, while we
also 30 to 50 generally absent from the Bank on Home Defence. .
The officers have been given leave of absence till the end of the war, without the
emoluments Or advantages under our Pension Fund. „. 1P Mr.

“Hayle,” Belmont Rd., Mosman, N.S.W.
—“A skin trouble first started on tlic side of
my nose and quickly spread over my face
and head, causing my face and head to
swell, making my features unrecognisable.
I cannot very well explain how I suffered,
but the pains were very severe, causing great
distress. I was unconscious for days: could
not rest day or night for pain, itching and
burning. The doctor pulled me through the
first attack all right, but it kept breaking out
on my face every few months, but not so
bad as the first attack. It would disfigure
me for days, so much that I would not let
anybody but our own family see me.
“Seeing the Cuticura advertisement in
the Herald I procured the Cuticurr, Reme-
dies. lam thankful to say I have not had
a return of it, and that is twenty odd years
ago. It brought it out of my body in per-
spiration. I would have to change my
clothes several times a day.’’ (Signed)
Miss Alice Denning, Aug. 5, 1912.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Bagman” describes the most prolific cow
on the N.S.W. North Coast: —
Concerning the threat of N.S.W. Minister for
t\ orks Griffith to abolish the free ferry on the Clar-
ence River, there’s room for his energy in another
direction. As a constant Coaster, it has always
struck me as a singular generosity that the Govern-
ment should expend about £9OOO annually to pro-
vide gratis tugs in the Northern River ports. Pass-
ing an ancient paddle steamer on the Ballina the
other day a coastal skipper told me that it was a
free State tug, for which the taxpayers were called
upon to find £l5O a year. (Incidentally, the relic
was too aged and feeble to tug anything.) Another
port pays £lOOO a year for a tug—which really does
tug. Once a fortnight she hauls a contraption which
looks like a sea-going tipdray, and, once a month, a
four-cornered ketch that would be better melted
down for cockroach oil. The cost works out at about
£2O a tow. In other river ports I’ve been in the
same sort of extravagance prevails. Ancient mariners
remember the day when the tugs were bought to
assist a large fleet of sailing craft which have been
wrecked or lost or mislaid long ago. Steamers now
do the business.
*** i *
“Dipso”: I entered a . Darlinghurst pub
in search of a long draught lager.' I put
down 6d. The beer-queen pointed coldly to
a proclamation on the wall: —
By order of the Honorable the Attorney-General of
New South Wales for War Tax Saloon Bar drinks,
7d.
For the first time in my life I longed to
make a speech. I wanted to tell her that
N. S. Wales didn’t make war taxes any
more than Manly Beach. That the Attorney-
General doesn’t fix the prices of saloqn bar
drinks. That I had that very afternoon
bought a similar-sized beer in a George-
street saloon-bar for 6d. That I was being
taken down and knew it. All I did, how-
ever, was to pay the 7d. Why does one
never do anything else in such circum-
stances?
“Starboard”: The late Archibald Currie,
the old sea-dog who founded the Currie line
of steamships between India and Australia,
was the High Priest of Thrift, so he
was in his proper place as chairman of the
Savings Bank of Victoria. There is a story
about Captain Currie calling a chief steward
from one of his liners before him to order
that “in future ye mustna’ gi’e ja-am in
the fo’castle, as it’s gey dear, an’ isna’
guid for them.” At the Marine Board old
Archie Currie, who was never heard to smile
during his long and honorable career, had
occasion to reprimand a skipper who had
taken a passenger-ship too near a pier-head
one dark night and had harmed its figure-
head.. “Losh, mon,” fie said, “think wee!
ower yer conduc’. The sheep was worth a
quarter of a meelyun, the cairgo aboot
anither quarter meelyun, all good propputy,
every beet o’ it, an’, an’, besides, mon, there
were human lives in yer chairge.”
D’Arblay Burney, the Burgoyne Co.’s Aus-
tralian manager, gives a gloomy forecast of
the coming S. Aus. vintage. Last year the
State made close on 4,000.000ga15. This
time there will be barely 1,000,000 —not
even enough for the inter-State demand ; and
this just as Britain is refusing to touch the
German-grown light white wines, which S.A.
could so well supply instead. However, there
are good stocks in hand from the last two
big vintages. Burney gives ah excellent tes-
timonial to the improvement in cellar
methods in Australia generally, and, especi-
ally in view of the new prejudice in England
against everything connected with the Rhine,
feels “entirely optimistic regarding the future
of the great wine-producing industry of the
Commonwealth.”
Illawarra (N.S.W.) runs, as a side-line
to coal-mining, the harvesting of blackberries.
The womenfolk and children of the miners
are paid 10s. (id. a ctvt. (about a penny a
pound) for gathering the fruit, the bulk of
which goes to the jam factories. This year
it is expected that the crop will weigh 250
to 300 tons, and about £3OOO will be handed
round amongst the pickers. In Maoriland
the Westland mining community have a
pleasanter side-line-—catching whitebait. The
women and children do the catching, and get
7s. Gd. per kerosene-tin.
*-**•#
“P.H.”: A small type of mill, patented in
1893, and but lately introduced to Victoria,
promises to take the production of flour for
home consumption away from the big cen-
tral mills and restore it to the wheat-grow-
ing localities, thereby saving the growers
large sums in haulage and other charges.
The machine is of British invention and
make. One, costing £2200, was installed
about two years ago at Ensay, E. Gippsland,
by R. C. Edwards, late milling expert to the
Vic. Government, and has been in successful
operation since. This particular mill, which
is large of its kind, treats four bags of wheat
an hour. The flour is pronounced first-class
by the district bakers, and is all consumed
locally.

MAXIM SILENCER
For Attaching
to RIFLES.
wRMmMMmMM
Improves Your Marksmanship.
Ensures Noiseless Shooting.
The Marvel of the Shooting World.
HOW IT WORKS.
The Silencer checks the muzzle blast. Instead of the powder gases being liberated into the
air instantaneously when the bullet emerges from the muzzle, as in the ordinary gun, the gases
aie caugh t aJ b e y niad/to "whirl around inside the Silencer. This whirling forces the gas to fly
out from the centre by centrifugal force, leaving a central space, just the same as when water is
whirled around in a set bowl, a hole or space forms in the centre, this leaves the space for the
bullet to make its nassage. The gas cannot pass through this space until it slows down. 11ns
causes it to discharge into the atmosphere gradually. This absolutely prevents report noise and
also reduces recoil over two-thirds.
As the hole in the Silencer is much larger than the bullet the latter does not touch anything*
in passing* through, and consequently accuracy of flight is just the same whether the Silencer is oft
or on.
HOW MUCH IT REDUCES THE NOISE.
The Silencer absolutely annuls all the noise of the report as can easily be understood from
its construction It also'reduces the recoil over two-thirds. The reason for this is that there is
a. very strong tendency for the Silencer to be blown off the end of the gun. This tendency amounts
to a forward pull on the gun barrel. This forward pull counterbalances part of <-he backwaid kick
of the gun barrel. , . . , . , ,
The only noise the Silencer does not control is the noise made out in the air beyond ..he gun
by a high velocity bullet in its flight. This noise is a crack like the crack of a whip and not
a “whistle” or a “shriek” as is commonly supposed The noise is made in the wake of the
bullet, and is caused by the same thing that causes the air to crack when a whiplash is snapped.
The use of the MAXIM SILENCER has developed several heretofore
unknown facts regarding shooting. Knowledge of some of these facts will he
found to increase the pleasure of using the Silencer.

The War that Joseph Lost.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f HE cattle on a thousand hills
Were chewing up the dew,
Which night in careless bounty spills
Out of her apron blue —
Clear diamonds in the morning light,
Wet to the boots and cold to bite.
Remote, unfriended, somewhat slow,
I sat upon a stone
To view the melancholy show,
In which I was alone
Save for the company of three
Brown sparrows and an angry tree.
The tree was burnt and black and sore,
For war had passed that way,
With shot and shell, the day before
And blown lfis boughs astray.
And passing on, with footsteps red,
Had mostly bombed its own self dead.
So fierce a war it was indeed,
So bitter and unkind,
It made the whole wide landscape bleed,
And tore and rent the wind.
I knew it would have handled me
As roughly as it did the tree.
But I was in a little cave
Some distance up the hill,
Close hidden from the strong and brave,
Whose guns were meant to kill.
When wars come round I mostly try
Particularly not to die.
Of course, wars differ in their style,
For some are loud and grand,
While others wear a sort of smile
And eat out of the hand;
And one young war I used to meet
Was always chilly in the feet.
Some wars run up and down the world,
And smash and burn and shout;
And some are nicely oiled and curled,
And some are short and stout;
While some will burrow underground
And hide in dirt without a sound.
Some wars get shot when they are young,
And others live so long
That in the end they must be hung
(Or hanged, if that is wrong) ;
But every kind of war I know
Wants to become a picture show.
The war that passed me yesterday
Was of the swifter kind ;
I saw that it was young and gay
With features ill-defined ;
And yet it was a solid strife,
Largely composed of noise and knife.
At three o’clock it disappeared
Completely out of sight,
And when my manhood I upreared,
To see that all was right,
A tall old gentleman drew near
With whiskers fixed to either ear.
He asked if any wars had been
Around my place that day.
A small one that I might have seen
Had strangely gone astray.
He said that as it was a pet
It must not get its stockings wet.
He told me that his luck was out,
For all the wars he bred
Got mixed in an unholy rout
And every one was dead,
And this war cub that went so fast
Would absolutely be his last.
I told him where I saw the thing,
Describing how it walked.
And how the creature wore its sting,
And how it ate and talked.
“My war,” he cried, “beyond a doubt!
I’m sorry that I let it out.”
“It might come home again,” I said,
“If it is fond of you.”
But solemnly he shook his head
And mourned: “Mine never do.
I lose them all as safe as sin
Once the confounded shots begin.
“The last large war I owned,” he said,
“Is gone beyond recall.
A mob of Russians shot it dead
And ate it, guns and all.
I fear the brutal Servians fell
On the young war I love so well.
“Still, if it chance to call again,
Just try and catch the tiling.
Perhaps I may as well explain
I have a job as king.
Also as emperor. In fact.
I boss the whole large Austrian act.
“I let my two wars out to play
With three that Billy bred.
Such large, sleek creatures could not striy
Too great a length, he said.
‘Joe,’ he declared in accents loud,
‘Those wars of yours will do you proud.’
“Now Bill’s three wars are getting sick,
Though still they can be found,
Because he taught the things the trick
Of hiding in the ground,;
But rabbit wars, that burrow deep,
Are profitless affairs to keep.”
A tear fell from the monarch’s eye,
And down his whiskers white.
I said: “Now. Francis, do not crv.
It is a painful sight
When one whose second name is Joe
Lets his face corrugate with woe.”
He wept: “My little war is gone
And I shall never see
An infant warlet sitting on
Its young victorious knee;
Beside the Drina even now
Perhaps they drive it like a cow.”
What could I say, what could I do?
I sorrowed for his pain;
But that small war, as well I knew,
Would never smile again ;
And Bill’s own wars were out of blood
To wet their burrows in the mud.
The cattle on a thousand hills
Had sunny grass to eat.
Below were towns and docks and mills
And wine and corn and meat.
I promised if his war came near
To try and hold it by the ear.
tv. s.w.

’Tween Drinks.
A TRIUMPH OF CHRISTIAN
SCIENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DOCTORS of Divinity and doctors
of medicine are the king spielers
of creation.” Penfeather bit his
drink savagely. “The confidence man who
bustles a dolt from Cowyards out of 9d. in
copper and a brass pin is execrated by all
the best judges, but the parson who gives
you a spurious pass-in check to Paradise
for fifty guineas, or the Medical Gent, who
charges you a hundred for giving your indi-
gestion a Latin name, is an ornament and
a social joss whom no one dreams of hitting
in the neck with an axe.”
“ ‘For two things the earth is disquieted,’ ”
quoted Mackaboy, the barman, “ ‘For a ser-
vant when he reigneth, and a fool when he
is filled with meat.’ Proverbs, treble X. I
thought yon were going too gay with that
bowl of pickled cucumbers in the pub offer-
tory not an hour since.”
“What of it? At one time or another
I’ve paid the medical profession five or six
hundred to fit me up for the reception of
unusual nutriment, and how am I served bet-
ter than the chuckle-head from Cowyards
if the whole blessed system, apparatus, para-
phernalia and outfit breaks down in the
presence of two inches of mashed cucum-
ber ?”
“Many worthy physicians are instructing
us re the inadvisability of eating at meals,”
said Patrick thoughtfully.
Penfeather uttered a howl. “Ellen-
tommy ! I’ve tried it, but a man must eat,”
he said.
“That would appear to be a popular error.
But why persist in giving your repairs to
these scientific operatives whom you de-
spise? There are others —the faith-healers,
for instance.”
“A push of patent jack chumps. I’ve
tried them too. They ought to be in the
Asylum for Queer and Curious Sorts.”
“Not necessarily. If faith will move
mountains why shouldn’t it shift a brace of
bolted cucumbers? You tried to work won-
ders in a week. If I could not digest my
food any other way I’d give Christian science
a fair and open trial. This Mary Baker
Eddy wasn’t altogether wrong. I knew a
chap once who proved the efficacy of hard
and frequent thinking with a religious
basis.”
The barman squinted one-eyed through the
glass he had been polishing, and resumed his
light employment—not that the glass needed
it, but because gentle exercise seemed to
assist his peculiar process of recapitulation.
“The chap’s name was Strettle—-Victor
Strettle —of sufficient parentage but no par-
ticular position, and when I first knew him
his only sizable aspiration was to secure
the local championship for putting the
pewter. Before the itch for drink predo-
minated he had been a busybody with the
ladies, I believe, but he was only about 32
and still a presentable person.
“Victor Strettle concerned me little as a
nimble drinker, and there were no confidences
between us till that night when he came in,
and finding me alone called for a medium.
This was sufficiently unlike Victor to excite
remark. Previously nothing less than the
longest was Mr. Strettle’s measure.
“When I served the gentleman, and turned
my back to titivate the bottles on the shelves
(you’ve noticed how fastidious I am) I
caught sight of Victor as reflected in the
ornamental mirrors, pouring the black con-
tents of a small phial into his.
“I was just in time to prevent mishap.
1 foreclosed on Victor’s beer. ‘No. you don’t,
old son,’ I said, ‘not here and no\,.’
“He laughed quite pleasantly. ‘Why
not?’ he asked.
“ ‘Because I hate to have them die about
the house. It’s a nasty job sweeping them
out. And I loathe coroner’s juries.’
“ ‘So do I,’ Victor agreed. ‘They’re rotten
company for a hot afternoon. But this
time, your ready wit’s a bit too sudden on
the jump.’ He passed me the phial.
“The inscription was ‘Tincture of Bitter
Aloes.’ I slumped, as a mail is apt to do
who has mistaken a small farce for a con-
siderable tragedy. ‘lt’s a quaint taste in
refreshments you have,’ I said limply.
“Victor grinned again. ‘l’ll admit I don’t
care over much about this mixture con-
sidered as a drink,’ he said. ‘ln fact, I’m
taking it because I don’t like it.’ He took
a sip, and wrung a fearful face. Then he
look another sip. ‘You see,’ said he, ‘this
is the very last, and I don’t want it to leave
any pleasant memories. I want it to taste
so that there will be no melancholy regrets
when I think back on beer in the days to
come.’
“I admitted it was a new one, and that it
showed some gumption. ‘Why this sudden
high moral purpose?’ I asked.
“ ‘She’s fair,’ said Victor; ‘very fair, and
small, and slight, with quiet, grey eyes.’
“ ‘lndeed,’ said I, ‘then this is only a tem-
porary abatement. You’ll be back to try if
after all you have not done beer a grave
injustice when the fairness, the smallness,
slightness and greyness have begun to appear
quite usual attributes in a damsel.’
“ ‘Never. Take me or leave me, that girl
shall never think me a sot.’ Victor raised
his glass of beer and bitterness. ‘Here’s to
the clean life,’ he said.
“He drank the dose, rolling the last gulp
on his tongue to get the true inwardness of
it. Then he took a tiny piece of blue ribbon
from his pocket and stuck it in his hat.
‘Hers.’ he said. ‘I sniped it last night.’
“The removal of Victor’s hat revealed the
fact that Victor was bald. From his eye-

BACKACHE CIVES THE WARNINC.
Have you ever thought what it is tbat makes your Back Ache, your Limbs Ache and
Swell, your Ankles and E-yes Dropsical, and disturbs you so often at nigbt?
It is kidney trouble, and
the above are some of the
