BACKACHE AND KIDNEY TROUBLE.
Two Sydney People Cured.
Mrs. E. Wilkinson, 29 Campbell Street. St.
Peter’s, Sydney, says : “For some time I suffered
agony from lumbago-like pains across the small of
my back. These pains prevented me from bending,
or getting proper rest at night, and seriously
hampered me when attending 'to my household
duties. Sometimes I was so bad I could hardly
move, and if I tried to get about the house the pain
was almost unbearable. After trying all sorts of
remedies without success, Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills were brought under my notice, so I got some,
and they did me good almost at once. After taking
two bottles of these pills I was ever so much better,
and eight bottles completely cured me. That was
12 months ago, and I have been well ever since. I
always keep a bottle of Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills in the house, and take a dose occasionally, and
they keep me in splendid health.”
Two years later Mrs. Wilkinson says : “I have
been free from backache since Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills cured me three years ago.”
Mr. W. H. Jeffries, King Street, Newtown.
Sydney, says: “About five years ago my kidneys
commenced to bother me, no doubt brought on from
hard work, such as lifting and bending over at my
work. My back was lame and aching, and this
handicapped me a lot, for it was a difficult matter
to stoop, and caused increased pain. I tried a good
many remedies, but they did me no good. A triend
advised me to take Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills,
so I sent for some. I used them as directed, and
when I had taken about half a bottle I knew, they
were benefiting me. Thus encouraged, I continued
with the treatment until I was quite cured, and I
have not felt the slightest sign of backache since.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills have made a different
man of me. and I recommend them to all who suffer
with backache.”
Five years later Mr. Jeffries says: * Doan s I>acK-
ache Kidney Pills cured me of backache five years
ago, and lam still free of this trying ailment. I
need say no more, my lasting cure speaks for itself.

“He showed me his scalp again, and it

“ ‘She will be pleased,’ I said.
“ ‘Yes, she is delighted, dear girl. In six
months I shall probably have quite sufficient
hair to marry on, and as I have a little
money saved I purpose devoting my full
moral energy to the cause for some time.’
“After that, Victor gave himself up to the
business of willing, and slowly but surely the
hair came up. It was half an inch long,
black and thick, when he left. The hotel
was too expensive,, he said. He was going to
live in a small room and make his money
last as long as possible. The possibility of
having to interfere with bis noble experiment
in order to earn his daily bread inspired the
departure.
“I saw nothing of Victor Strettle for 12
months or more, and he had faded from my
mind. But one warm afternoon I came upon
a strange, snaggy object on a seat in Fitz-
roy Gardens. It had long, tangled hair hang-
ing to its shoulders, a voluptuous growth of
whiskers swept its chest, and wound tough
tendrils round its vest buttons. Its face was
thin and pale, and it had large, disconcerting
eyes with odd pupils. It was either a pro-
phet escaped out of the Book of Ahab or
Victor Strettle.
“I was fully employed for five minutes re-
viving his memory.
“ ‘Yes, yes,’ he said, ‘the Rev. Mr. Mack-
boy. I remember you. You see, I’ve done
it.’ He tossed his matted mane, he plunged
greedy talons into his indecent whiskers.
“ ‘You’ve done it, all right,’ I admitted.
‘Hair enough for a mattress factory. But
what of Kathleen?’
“ ‘Kathleen?’ he said in some bewilder-
ment.
“ ‘She was small, and fair, and slight, and
had quiet, grey eyes. You were to have wed
her, if you could grow hair enough.’
“ ‘Why, yes, of course,’ he said eagerly.
‘She was willing, but it wouldn’t do—it
would never do. I couldn’t think of it. You
see’—he dropped his voice to a whisper—‘l
couldn’t think of anything but my hair. If
I cease to think of it it will fall out. You’ll
excuse me, won’t you? But I mustn’t take
my mind off it for any length of time. A
man mustn’t lose hold of his hair. If he
does it comes out in clumps. When you’ve
willed it in you haven’t done ; you’ve got to
will it to stay in.’ He pulled a couple of
strands from his whiskers, looked at them
piteously, and began to whimper. ‘Dear me,
look what you’ve done—-look what you have
done, you wretched fellow !’ he wailed.
“He sank back in the seat, hunched up,
liis eyes closed, and so I left him snivelling
miserably.
“I know no more of Victor Strettle, but I
think it very likely that if you inquired at
Kew you would find a permanent boarder there
whose fond belief is that he has willed the
whole asylum so full of hair that it billows
out of all the windows and chimneys. There
are the elements of immortal fact in Chris-
tion Science, Penfeather. It cured Strettle.
But you must nersist with it. Remember,
rum was not made in a day.”

Der Droubles of Hans.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
<t OT damn der Kaiser! is vot I say.
If I had him here I vood bunch
his het.
“I lefd Shermany fifdeen years ago. I
am nod a Brussian; lam vot you gall
Socialist —anyding yar like; but ,1 do not
like der Kaiser. I vos born in Bavaria;
andt I like not der serving in der dam
Brussian army. I liaf seen mine beoble
obbressed by the army—by own beoble. mind
you, forced to go wid der army. Dey haf
to obey Brussian orders. So I ged oud.
The Brussian is nod Sherman anyway. He
is a Lett and a Wend and a dash heathen.
“Der Emperor of Shermany, who is dam
Brussian and no Sherman, he dake my
broberty; but' I ged over to Ameriga. I
am now Amerigan citizan. lam broud of
it. Den I gome to Ausdralya, and I geds
more broberty. Bud I still remain Amerigan
citizan. Der Emperor of Shermany is no
Sherman. He is a dam Wend heathen, and
his ancestor was a money-lender.
“Den der war gome. I orffer of my ser-
vice to defend Ausdralya, bud I say: ‘I do
nod want do figlif ava.v, bud if Brussian
droops gome to Ausdralya I fight against
dem.’ Dey say, ‘Dam sb.v !'
“Id was all der damfool Kaiser, vit dake
my broberty. I haf a law suit, and dey say
I a Sherman, and der ding is heldt over dill
the var is done, and Imy broberty have
nod. Den, ven I ged on a dram der news-
boy asts me to buy a baber, and ven I say
‘Nein’ he say ‘Sherman sby !’
“And all der dime I hade der Kaiser —
der sgoundrel that says he is Sherman and
isn’d. His vamily was bawnprokers.
“Ad der Klob I ged an engagement and I
go mine instrooment vid; bud der beoble
dalk one another vid and dey say do me:
‘Sorry, but dere is some misdake ;we really
did not wand another insdrooment.’
“I vill mayke dem bay; bud Ido not
vant der money only ; I vant do work.
“Dey all durn on me. They dink I vant
der Brussians do vin, and if de Brussians
game to Ausdralya I vood be shod !
“I blay der game of gards, and Fretty,
when he haf der lod of tricks, he lean over
ven I drump his ace and say: ‘Do
Hell vith der Kaiser!’ Den, ven I bud der
right bower on his left he say : ‘Der Kaiser’s
a dam degenerate !’
“Id is nod an insult, but id is meant to be.
“‘Got dam the Kaiser!’ I say from mine
hard der truth oud. He obbress my beoble.
he robt me ; and now he make my life like
dis !”
I noticed a friend of mine signalling me,
and excused rilyself. The friend explained
that he wanted me to join him in a drink.
“But I’m not going to ask that darned
German.” he said. “A fellow ought not to
drink with you, speaking to a chap like that.
He’s a blanky spy ! All Germans are spies!”
I tried to explain that the Kaiser wasn’t
any sort of a real German, but I didn’t get
a hearing.
